Sawm — Fasting

Fasting Qada for Fainting and Non-Continuous Insanity
Intra-Hanafi expansion on qada for fainting and insanity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi rulings for qada of missed fasts due to fainting and insanity, and explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on congenital versus acquired insanity.

Core Idea
Continuous insanity exempts from qada; non-continuous insanity and fainting require qada, but congenital insanity differs.

When a person misses fasts due to unconsciousness or insanity, does he have to make them up? The answer depends on whether the condition was continuous or not, and within insanity, whether it was present from birth or acquired. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq navigates these distinctions, including an important intra-Hanafi disagreement on the legal status of congenital insanity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The passage discusses qada (making up) for missed Ramadan fasts. Identify the ruling: qada is wajib in some cases, but not in others. List the cases: fainting, non-continuous insanity, continuous insanity.
2
Analyze the reasoning for fainting
For fainting, the first day is made up because intention is presumed; subsequent days require qada due to lack of intention. There is no exemption because fainting does not typically last the whole month.
3
Analyze the reasoning for insanity
Insanity negates intention, a condition for fasting. Continuous insanity (lasting the whole month) exempts due to hardship (haraj). Non-continuous insanity is like sleep – obligation remains. Then note the key dispute: does congenital insanity (from birth) differ from acquired insanity?
Understand that the original obligation is based on dhimma and legal capacity.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
Identify the positions: Abu Yusuf obligates qada for both congenital and acquired by istihsan; Muhammad differentiates – no qada for congenital (like minor), qada for acquired. Later scholars prefer Muhammad's view. Also note the secondary dispute on regaining consciousness on the last day after zawaal.
Use the madhhab_dispute_map to follow the arguments.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position: congenital insanity exempts from qada; acquired non-continuous insanity requires qada. For fainting, qada is required always. The fatwa is on the corrected view regarding the last day.

Source Text

يقضي اليوم الأول لوجود الصوم فيه وهو الإمساك المقرون بالنية إذ الظاهر وجودها منه، ويقضي ما بعده لانعدام النية ولا فرق بين أن يحدث الإغماء في الليل أو في النهار في أنه يقضي اليوم الأول، وإنما ذكر المصنف حدوثه في ليلته ليعلم حكم ما إذا حدث في اليوم بالأولى لوجود الإمساك وهو ليس بمغمى عليه. وأشار إلى أن الإغماء لو كان في شعبان قضاه كله لعدم النية، وإلى أنه لو كان متهتكاً يعتاد الأكل في رمضان أو وصححه في النهاية والظهيرية لأن الصوم يصح فيه كالليل. اعلم أن الجنون ينافي النية التي هي شرط العبادات فلا يجب مع مطلقاً للحرج وما لا يمتد جعل كالنوم لأن الجنون لا ينفي أصل الوجوب إذ هو ثابتة له باعتبار آدميته حتى ورث وملك وكان أهلاً للثواب كأن نوى صوم الغد بعد غروب الشمس فجن فيه ممسكاً كله فلا يقضي لو أفاق بعده وصح إسلامه تبعاً، وإذا كان صح المسقط الحرج لزم اختلاف الامتداد المسقط فقدر في الصلاة بالزيادة على يوم وليلة عندهما، يلزمه قضاء جميع الممتد منه بالذمة وهي وعند محمد بصيرورة الصلاة ستاً وهو أقيس لكنهما أقاما الوقت مقام الواجب كما في ينوي فلا شك في عدمها قوله : ( وعن محمد أنه فرق بينهما إلا في الأصلي على الأصح ا هـ. لكن في شرح الجامع الصغير لقاضيخان وجواب الكتاب مطلقاً فيجري على إطلاقه وهو الصحيح. نص عليه في المنتقى قوله : ( وصححه في النهاية والظهيرية أي صححا ما في غاية البيان وكذا He makes up the first day because fasting existed in it, which is abstention coupled with intention since the apparent state indicates its existence. He makes up the subsequent days due to absence of intention. There is no difference whether fainting occurred at night or during the day regarding making up the first day. The author mentioned its occurrence at night to convey the ruling for occurrence during the day a fortiori, because abstention existed and he is not unconscious. He indicated that if fainting occurred in Sha'ban, he makes up all of it for lack of intention; and if he was a habitual eater during Ramadan or a traveler, he makes up all for lack of evidence of intention. His statement: (And for non-continuous insanity), i.e., he makes up if he misses it due to non-continuous insanity, which is that it does not encompass the month. Continuous is that it encompasses the month, and it removes hardship unlike less than that because the cause existed – the month and legal capacity. The obligation has benefit: he becomes demanded in a way that does not cause hardship in performance, unlike the continuous where performance causes hardship so no benefit. Fainting does not typically encompass the month so no hardship, otherwise he might die since he does not eat or drink. He made it absolute including congenital and acquired insanity, which is the apparent narration. From Muhammad that he differentiated between them because if one reaches puberty insane, he joins the minors so address is absent, unlike if he reached puberty sane then became insane – this is the choice of some later scholars. Included in non-continuous is if he becomes conscious on the last day of Ramadan, whether before or after zawaal – that is the month, contrary to what is in Ghayat al-Bayan from Hameed al-Deen al-Dharir who said: if he becomes conscious after zawaal on the last day, nothing is required of him. It was corrected in al-Nihayah and al-Zahiriyyah because fasting is valid at that time like the night. Know that insanity contradicts intention which is a condition of worship, so it is not obligated at all due to hardship; what is not continuous is treated like sleep because insanity does not negate the original obligation – it is established for him due to his humanity such that he inherits, owns property, and is eligible for reward. If he intended tomorrow's fast after sunset then became insane and remained abstaining the whole day, he does not make up if he recovers after – and his Islam is valid accordingly. If hardship is the excusing factor, then the extent of the excusing continuity differs – in prayer it is set at exceeding one day and night according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and according to Muhammad it makes prayer six prayers which is more analogous but they made time stand in for obligation. There is no doubt of its absence. His statement: (And from Muhammad that he differentiated) i.e., he said: if one reaches puberty insane then recovers part of the month, he does not have to make up the past. Hisham narrated from Abu Yusuf that he said: by analogy no qada, but I apply istihsan so obligate qada for the past part of the month because congenital insanity does not differ from acquired in any ruling. There is no narration from Abu Hanifa on this, and later scholars differed on the analogy of his madhhab. The most correct is that he does not have to make up the past – as in al-Mabsut and al-Inayah. In Mawahib al-Rahman: we obligate qada if he recovers part, and only drop it in congenital on the most correct view. But in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir by Qadi Khan and the answers of the book, it is absolute and acts on its absoluteness, which is correct. Texted in al-Muntaqa. His statement: (And he corrected it in al-Nihayah and al-Zahiriyyah) i.e., they corrected what was in Ghayat al-Bayan, and likewise in al-Inayah, al-Mujtaba, al-Mi'raj, and the fatwa is upon it, and it is the choice of Shams al-A'imma as in al-Imdad, and he acted upon it.
Source pages p. 507

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the obligation to make up missed fasts due to unconsciousness and insanity. The author begins by stating the general rule: a person who becomes unconscious must make up the first day because intention is presumed from the night, but subsequent days require qada due to lack of intention. This applies whether unconsciousness occurs at night or during the day. The author then distinguishes non-continuous insanity (ghayr mumtadd) from continuous insanity (mumtadd). Non-continuous insanity requires qada, while continuous insanity exempts due to hardship. The key point is that insanity negates intention, but the original obligation remains in the dhimma (legal capacity). A significant point of difference within the Hanafi school is whether congenital insanity (asli) and acquired insanity (arid) are treated the same. The author reports that Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani differentiated: a person who reaches puberty already insane is like a minor – no obligation, but if he was sane then became insane, qada is required. Abu Yusuf by istihsan obligates qada in both cases. There is no explicit narration from Abu Hanifa. The later scholars (mutakhkhirin) prefer that congenital insanity exempts from qada, while acquired non-continuous insanity still requires qada. The passage also discusses a special case: if a person becomes conscious on the last day of Ramadan after zawaal, the correct view (as corrected in al-Nihayah and al-Zahiriyyah) is that he must fast that day, contrary to Hameed al-Deen al-Dharir. The fatwa is upon this corrected view. The overall principle is that hardship (haraj) is the criterion for exemption: continuous insanity throughout the month creates hardship, so qada is dropped; non-continuous does not, so qada is required. The same criterion applies in prayer, where unconsciousness exceeding one day and night exempts from qada of prayers, but for fasting, the month-long continuity is rare so it does not exempt.

Key Terms
جنون غير ممتد (junūn ghayr mumtadd): Insanity that does not last the entire month of Ramadan; the person is conscious for some part of the month.
إغماء (ighmāʔ): Unconsciousness; in fasting, the first day of fainting is not made up because intention is presumed from the night, but subsequent days require qada.
نية (niyyah): Intention, which is a condition for the validity of fasting. For obligatory fasts, it must be made before dawn. In the context of fainting and insanity, intention is presumed for the first day of fainting but absent for subsequent days and for the insane.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on qada for missed fasts due to congenital insanity (from birth) that lasts the entire month?
A) Qada is wajib because the person has a dhimma.
B) No qada is required because the person never had the legal address for fasting.
C) Qada is wajib only if the person regains consciousness during the month.
D) No qada is required only if the insanity continues beyond the month.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the obligation to make up missed fasts due to fainting or insanity depends on the continuity of the condition and, for insanity, on whether it is congenital or acquired; continuous and congenital insanity exempt from qada.

Imsak without Intention: Rulings for Traveler, Menstruating Woman, and Mistaken Actions
Ruling on imsak without intention with examples and intra-Hanafi difference

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a person who becomes obligated to fast during the day without having intended, and distinguish when qada is required versus kaffarah according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Without intention, no valid fast, but sanctity of Ramadan demands imsak and qada, not kaffarah.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author explains several scenarios where a person must refrain from eating during Ramadan despite not having made an intention to fast. The common thread is the sanctity of the time (hurmah al-waqt) and the distinction between being a valid faster and merely observing the outward respect. We will examine the ruling, the reasoning, and the intra-Hanafi difference on kaffarah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subjects and their initial state
List the people mentioned: a traveler who arrives during the day, a menstruating woman who becomes pure, one who takes suhoor thinking it is night but dawn has risen, and one who breaks fast thinking the sun set but it has not. They all have no intention for the day's fast.
All these persons were not obligated to fast at the beginning of the day due to travel, menses, or mistake.
2
Determine the obligatory actions
They must refrain from eating for the rest of the day (imsak) and make up that day (qada). This is due to hurmah al-waqt (sanctity of the time).
Imsak is wajib; qada is wajib.
3
Identify what is not required
No kaffarah (expiation) is required, even if they eat after the mistake. Reason: they are not valid fasters because they lacked intention from the start.
Kaffarah is only for deliberately breaking a valid Ramadan fast.
4
Understand the intra-Hanafi difference
According to Abu Hanifa, no kaffarah in any case. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn), if they eat before zawaal (midday), kaffarah is required because they lost the chance to correct the fast; after zawaal, no kaffarah.
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view: no kaffarah at all.
5
Apply the general principle to the four excuses
The text mentions four excuses that can affect fasting: childhood, insanity, fainting, and sleep. Each has its own ruling on whether qada is required, but the principle of imsak without intention remains.
These excuses do not change the core ruling of imsak but affect obligation and qada.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نية صوم وفطر ولو قدم مسافر أو طهرت حائض أو تسحر ظنه ليلاً والفجر طالع أو أفطر كذلك والشمس حية أمسك يومه وقضى ولم يكفر كلمة عمداً بعد أكله ناسياً المستحاضة، وفي الصوم باستغراق الشهر ليله ونهاره، وفي الزكاة باستغراق الحول، وأبو يوسف جعل أكثره ككله. وأما الصغير فقبل أن يعقل كالجنون الممتد فإذا عقل تأهل للأداء دون الوجوب إلا الإيمان، وأما النائم فلكون النوم موجباً للعجز لزم تأخير خطاب الأداء لا أصل الواجب ولذا وجب القضاء إذا زال بعد الوقت. ولما كان لا يمتد غالباً لم يسقط به شيء من العبادات لعدم الحرج والإغماء فوقه، فإن امتد في الصلوات بأن زاد على يوم وليلة جعل عذراً مسقطاً لها دفعاً للحرج لكونه غالباً ولم يجعل عذراً في الصوم لأن امتداده شهراً نادر فلم يكن في إيجابه حرج وبهذا ظهر أن الأعذار أربعة صبا وجنون وإغماء ونوم. وقد علم أحكامها والله الموفق للصواب.
قوله (وبامساك بلا نية صوم وفطر) أي يجب القضاء لأن المستحق هو الإمساك بجهة العبادة ولاعبادة إلا بالنية وأما هبة النصاب من الفقير فإنها تسقط الزكاة بدون نيتها باعتبار وجود نية القربة. وفي غاية البيان وقد مر أن المغمى عليه لا يقضي اليوم الذي حدث الإغماء في ليلته لوجود النية منه ظاهراً فلا بد من التأويل لهذه المسألة. وتأويلها أن يكون مريضاً أو مسافراً لا ينوي شيئاً أو متهتكاً اعتاد الأكل في رمضان فلم يكن حاله دليلاً على عزيمة الصوم اهـ. وكذا في النهاية . ورده في فتح القدير بأنه تكلف مستغنى عنه لأن الكلام عند عدم النية ابتداء لا بأمر يوجب النسيان، ولا شك أنه أدرى بحاله بخلاف من أغمي عليه فإن الإغماء قد يوجب نسيانه حال نفسه بعد الإفاقة فبنى الأمر فيه على الظاهر من حاله وهي وجود النية. القضاء فقط إلى عدم وجوب الكفارة لو أكل لأنه غير صائم، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة بوجوب، وعندهما كذلك إن أكل بعد الزوال، وإن أكل قبل الزوال تجب الكفارة لأنه فوت إمكان التحصيل فصار كغاصب قوله ولو قدم مسافر أو طهرت حائض أو تسحر يظنه ليلاً والفجر طالع أو أفطر كذلك والشمس حية أمسك يومه وقضى ولم يكفر كأكله عمداً بعد أكله ناسياً ونائمة ومجنونة وطئتا لما قدمنا أن كل من صار أهلاً للزوم ولم يكن كذلك في أول اليوم فإنه يجب عليه الإمساك لأنه وجب قضاء لحق الوقت لأنه وقت معظم، وإنما وجبا المسافر والحائض لما تقدم أن أصل الوجوب ثابت عليهما، وإنما المتأخر وجوب الصبي إذا بلغ والكافر إذا أسلم فإنه وإن وجب عليهما الإمساك أيضاً لم يجب القضاء الوجوب في حقهما أول الجزء من اليوم كما بيناه، وكذا لو تسحر وهو يظن بقاء الليل فبان خلافه أو أفطر ظاناً زوال اليوم فبان خلافه وجب الإمساك قضاء لحق الوقت بالقدر الممكن أو القضاء على الأداء بخلاف لعدم مصححاً له في نور الأيضاح قوله : أراد بالظن الخ) قال في النهر : لا يصح أن يراد بالظن هنا ما
Intention of fasting and breaking fast. And if a traveler arrives, or a menstruating woman becomes pure, or one takes suhoor thinking it is night but dawn has risen, or breaks fast similarly while the sun is still high, he restrains himself for the rest of the day and makes it up, and does not expiate. Like one who deliberately eats after eating forgetfully. The woman with prolonged irregular bleeding (mustahada) [has other rulings]. Regarding fasting, by encompassing the month day and night; regarding zakat, by encompassing the year; Abu Yusuf considered most of it as all of it. As for the minor, before understanding he is like continuous insanity; when he understands, he becomes capable of performance but not obligation except for faith. As for the sleeper, because sleep causes incapacity, deferring the address of performance is necessary, not the original obligation; hence qada is required when the impediment ends after the time. Since it is not typically continuous, nothing of worship is dropped due to lack of hardship. Fainting is above this: if it extends in prayers beyond a day and night, it is made an excusing factor dropping them to avoid hardship because it is common, but it is not made an excuse in fasting because its extension to a month is rare, so no hardship in obligating it. Hence the excuses are four: childhood, insanity, fainting, and sleep. Their rulings are known. And Allah grants success.
His statement: (And by abstaining without intention of fasting or breaking fast) i.e., qada is required because what is owed is abstention with the aspect of worship, and there is no worship without intention. As for giving nisab to a poor person, it drops zakat without intention by virtue of the presence of intention for qurba. In Ghayat al-Bayan: it was mentioned that the unconscious does not make up the day he fainted in its night due to the apparent existence of intention, so ta'wil is necessary for this issue. Its ta'wil is that he be a sick person or traveler not intending anything, or a habitual eater in Ramadan, so his state is not evidence of determination to fast; likewise in al-Nihayah. This was refuted in Fath al-Qadir as unnecessary effort because the discussion is about the absence of intention from the start, not due to something causing forgetfulness. There is no doubt he is more aware of his state than one who fainted, because fainting may cause him to forget his state after recovery, so the ruling is based on the apparent of his state which is existence of intention. Qada only, and no expiation if he eats because he is not fasting. This according to Abu Hanifa is obligation; according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) also if he ate after zawaal; if he ate before zawaal, expiation is required because he missed the possibility of fulfillment, so he becomes like a usurper. His statement: And if a traveler arrives, or a menstruating woman becomes pure, or one takes suhoor thinking it is night but dawn rose, or breaks fast similarly while the sun is high, he restrains himself for the day and makes it up, and does not expiate, like one who eats deliberately after eating forgetfully, and like one who sleeps, or is unconscious, or has intercourse? Because we mentioned that whoever becomes eligible for obligation and was not so at the beginning of the day, he must restrain himself because the time is honored. The traveler and menstruating woman have [special rulings] because the original obligation is established for them, the delayed obligation for the minor when he attains puberty and the disbeliever when he converts: although they must also restrain themselves, qada is not required because the obligation in their case begins from the first part of the day as we explained. Similarly, if one takes suhoor thinking night remains and it turns out otherwise, or breaks fast thinking the day has ended and it turns out otherwise, he must restrain himself as a duty to the honored time to the extent possible, or qada upon performance unlike [something]. For lack [of something] correct in Nur al-Idah: his statement: he meant by 'zann' here… [text cut off]
Source pages p. 508

Commentary
This passage addresses cases where a person is not actually fasting (no intention) but must still refrain from eating during the day out of respect for Ramadan (hurmah al-waqt). The text begins by listing several scenarios: a traveler who arrives home during the day after having not intended to fast (e.g., he was on a journey and thought he did not have to fast), a menstruating woman who becomes pure during the day, a person who eats suhoor mistakenly thinking it is still night but dawn has already broken, and one who breaks his fast thinking the sun has set but it has not. In all these cases, the ruling is: they must refrain from eating for the rest of the day (imsak), make up that day (qada), but no expiation (kaffarah) is required, even if they ate deliberately after the initial mistake. The reasoning: they are not considered valid fasters because they had no intention; however, once they become aware of the time, they must honor the sanctity of Ramadan. The text then discusses the four general excuses that can affect fasting: childhood, insanity, fainting, and sleep. For each, it explains whether the original obligation remains and whether qada is required. The passage also clarifies a refutation: some scholars (like in Ghayat al-Bayan) tried to reconcile the ruling for the unconscious with that for the mistaken eater, but Fath al-Qadir says there is no need—the unconscious person had intention presumably, while the mistaken eater had no intention from the start. Finally, it notes that according to Abu Hanifa, no expiation is needed if the mistaken eater eats after zawaal; eating before zawaal may require expiation because he missed the chance to correct his fast.

Key Terms
إمساك (imsāk): Abstaining from eating, drinking, and other fast-breakers for the rest of the day out of respect for Ramadan, even if not actual fasting.
حرمة الوقت (ḥurmat al-waqt): The sanctity of the time of Ramadan; it demands that even a non-faster refrain from eating publicly or out of respect, but it does not make the day a valid fast.
قضاء (qaḍāʔ): The act of making up a missed fast on another day; required for any day where one was obligated to fast but did not do so validly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school (mufta bihi), a traveler who arrives home at 11 AM and immediately eats must do which of the following?
A) Imsak only, no qada, no kaffarah.
B) Imsak, qada, and kaffarah.
C) Imsak and qada, but no kaffarah.
D) No obligations because he was traveling.

Key Takeaway
A person who becomes obligated to fast during the day without prior intention must do imsak and qada, but no kaffarah, because the day was never a valid fast.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate

Fasting Obligation Arising Mid-Day: Imsak, Qada', and No Expiation
Explaining rulings for those who become obligated to fast mid-day

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a person who becomes obligated to fast mid-day, explain why expiation is not required, and distinguish between zann and shakk in this context.

Core Idea
When obligation arises mid-day, you must refrain and make up, but no expiation—your mistake was not deliberate.

The Hanafi school provides clear guidance for situations where a person who was not fasting (due to travel, menstruation, or a mistaken assumption) realizes that they must fast for the remainder of the day. The key ruling is that they must immediately refrain from eating and drinking, and later make up the fast. However, they are not required to pay expiation because their action was not a deliberate violation. This lesson explores the reasoning behind this ruling, the evidence from the Sunnah, and the practical implications of intention and certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenarios and the common ruling
Read the passage to list the specific cases: traveler returning, menstruant becoming pure, and someone eating under a mistaken belief about the time. Note that all share the same ruling: they must refrain for the rest of the day (imsak) and make up (qada') but do not pay expiation (kaffarah).
The common factor is that the person was not deliberately fasting when they ate or broke the fast.
2
Understand the basis for imsak and qada'
Why is imsak wajib? Because the time is sacred and must be respected. Why is qada' wajib? Because the act of fasting was not completed validly; it must be made up. Note that these obligations arise even though no valid intention to fast was made at the beginning of the day.
The intention (niyyah) is normally required for a valid fast, but here the obligation to refrain is due to the sanctity of the moment.
3
Examine the evidence against expiation
The text cites the hadith 'Leave what causes you doubt for what does not cause you doubt' and argues that since the person acted on a reasonable assumption (e.g., thinking it was still night), they did not commit an intentional violation. Therefore, no kaffarah is due. Only qada' is required.
The Hanafi position emphasizes that intention (niyyah) determines the nature of the act: the person intended to eat at night, not to break the fast.
4
Distinguish between zann and shakk in the ruling
The passage clarifies that 'zann' (strong assumption) regarding the time does not break the fast if later proven wrong—only qada' is needed. If 'shakk' (doubt) remains unresolved, the fast remains valid because certainty (original night) is not overridden by doubt. This follows the usul principle of istishab.
If one eats purely out of doubt without any basis, the fast is broken and qada' is wajib, but still no kaffarah.
5
Apply the ruling to a practical scenario
Consider a traveler who returns home at 10 AM on a Ramadan day and has not eaten since early morning. According to the ruling, he must immediately refrain from eating and make up the fast later. He does not pay expiation because he was not intentionally fasting when he broke it.
The same applies to a menstruating woman who becomes pure mid-day: she must refrain and make up, but no expiation.

Intention of fasting and breaking fast. If a traveler arrives or a menstruating woman becomes pure, or one eats suhoor thinking it is night but dawn has risen, or breaks fast similarly while the sun [has not set], he must refrain for the rest of the day and make up, and need not expiate, like one who eats intentionally after eating forgetfully...
Source pages p. 508

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses rulings for various situations where a person becomes obligated to fast mid-day. The main ruling: if someone who was not fasting (traveler, menstruating woman, or one eating under mistaken assumption) discovers that they actually must fast, they must refrain from eating/drinking for the remainder of the day and make up the fast later, but no expiation is required. The reasoning is that their action was not deliberate defiance; they acted based on a reasonable assumption or were previously exempt. The passage also clarifies the difference between assumption (zann) and doubt (shakk) in these rulings, citing the hadith 'Leave what causes you doubt for what does not cause you doubt.' The author notes that if one eats based on a strong assumption that night remains, and then it becomes clear dawn had broken, they must make up but no expiation because they acted on original certainty. If doubt remains unresolved, the stronger view is that no make-up is required as certainty is not overridden by doubt.

Key Terms
الظن (al-zann): Assumption or strong probability; in this context, the belief that it is still night based on circumstantial evidence.
الشك (al-shakk): Doubt; uncertainty about the time. In fasting, doubt about fajr time requires refraining from eating, but if unresolved, the fast remains valid.
الإمساك (al-imsak): Refraining from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse for the rest of the day as a respectful observance of the sacred time.
القضاء (al-qada'): Making up the fast on another day; obligatory for those who break the fast due to a valid excuse or mistake.
الكفارة (al-kaffarah): Expiation for deliberately breaking a Ramadan fast without excuse; not required in cases of mistake or forgetfulness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) He must pay expiation because he missed the fast.

Key Takeaway
A person who becomes obligated to fast mid-day must refrain from eating and make up the fast, but no expiation is required because the act was not deliberate.

Qada' When Mistakenly Eating Suhoor After Fajr
Explains qada' without kaffarah for assumption-based violations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that qada' is wajib but kaffarah is not when a fasting person eats suhoor based on assumption (zann) that Fajr has not risen, and later discovers it had risen.

Core Idea
Eating with assumption about time: qada' due to invalidated fast, no kaffarah due to lack of deliberate certainty.

This lesson examines the scenario where a fasting person eats suhoor believing it is still night, but later learns Fajr had already risen. The Hanafi school distinguishes between making up the fast (qada') and expiation (kaffarah). Understanding this distinction is crucial: kaffarah is reserved for intentional, certain violations; doubt and assumption prevent it. We will walk through the ruling, its evidence, and the reasoning behind the distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its context
The person eats suhoor while assuming it is night (ظن ليلاً) or assuming Fajr has not risen (تسحر مع ظن طلوع الفجر) but in reality Fajr has already risen (والفجر طالع).
The key is the person's mental state: assumption (zann), not certainty.
2
State the ruling from the text
The text says: 'فعليه القضاء ولا كفارة' – upon him is qada' and no kaffarah. So the fast is invalidated and must be made up, but no expiation is required.
Qada' is wajib for any invalidated Ramadan fast regardless of intent.
3
Analyze the dalil
The Hanafi proof includes the hadith 'دع ما يريبك إلى ما لا يريبك' (leave what makes you doubt for what does not). This implies that acting on doubt removes full liability.
The dalil is zanni in transmission but supports the principle that doubt blocks kaffarah.
4
Distinguish from cases with confirmation
If an upright person (رجل عدل) informs the fasting person after eating that Fajr had risen, then qada' becomes wajib. Without such confirmation, if it is only overwhelming assumption (غلب على ظنه) that he ate after Fajr, no qada' is required.
This shows that mere internal assumption is not sufficient to impose qada'; only external reliable information triggers it.
5
Apply the principle to other scenarios
The same principle applies when one breaks the fast at maghrib based on assumption: qada' is wajib, and kaffarah is not due because of the presence of doubt (شبهة).
The underlying rule: Kaffarah is only for deliberate, certain violations (عمداً مع العلم).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن ظن ليلاً أو تسحر مع ظن طلوع الفجر والفجر طالع فعليه القضاء ولا كفارة... وأراد بقوله «والفجر طالع» تيقن الطلوع... ولو غلب على ظنه أنه أكل بعد طلوع الفجر لا قضاء عليه ما لم يخبره رجل عدل... وأشار إلى أن التسحر ثابت واختلف فيه؛ فقيل مستحب، وقيل سنة... واعترضه في النهر بأنه إنما قيد بالليل ليطابق قوله «أو تسحر»... وأراد بالظن في قوله «أو أفطر» كذلك غلبة الظن لأنه لو كان شاكاً تجب الكفارة... وفي البدائع ما يخالفه ولفظه: وإن كان غالب رأيه أنها لم تغرب فلا شك في وجوب القضاء عليه. واختلف المشايخ في وجوب الكفارة؛ فقال بعضهم تجب وقال بعضهم لا تجب وهو الصحيح لأن احتمال الغروب قائم فكانت الشبهة ثابتة وهذه الكفارة لا تجب مع الشبهة.
And if he assumes it is night, or eats suhoor assuming Fajr has risen while Fajr is actually rising, then upon him is qada' and no kaffarah... And by his saying 'while Fajr is rising' he means certainty of the rising... And if it is overwhelmingly likely to him that he ate after Fajr, there is no qada' upon him unless an upright man informs him... And he indicated that suhoor is established; there is difference: some say it is recommended, some say it is sunnah... And it was objected by the author of al-Nahr that he only restricted to night to match his saying 'or he eats suhoor'... And by assumption in his saying 'or he breaks fast' he means overwhelming assumption, because if he were doubtful, kaffarah would be obligatory... And in al-Bada'i there is something contradicting it: 'And if his overwhelming opinion is that the sun has not set, then there is no doubt about the obligation of qada' upon him.' And the mashayikh differed regarding the obligation of kaffarah: some said it is obligatory, some said it is not, and that is correct because the possibility of sunset remains, so the doubt remains, and this kaffarah is not obligatory with doubt.
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with fiqh rulings when a fasting person acts on assumption (zann) or doubt (shakk) about the time of Fajr or sunset. The author first reiterates the matn: if one assumes it is still night and eats, but later finds Fajr had risen, qada' is required but not kaffarah. This is because the act is unintentional and the damage is incomplete; the intention was not to break the fast. The text then discusses the scenario of overwhelming assumption (ghalabat al-zann). If one is fairly sure he ate after Fajr but no confirmed report, there is no qada' according to the most authentic narration. However, if an upright person informs him, qada' is required. The author clarifies that 'assumption' in the matn includes doubt, because if one merely doubts, the ruling is the same. He then explains the case of breaking the fast at sunset: if one mistakenly assumes the sun has set and breaks his fast, but later finds it had not, qada' is obligatory, and there is disagreement on kaffarah. The correct position is no kaffarah because the presence of doubt (shubha) prevents it. The passage also notes a difference among scholars regarding the status of suhoor (pre-dawn meal): some say it is mustahabb, others sunnah. The relied-upon view is that it is mustahabb. Finally, the author outlines the various possible scenarios (24 or 36) based on combinations of doubt/assumption, time of day, and whether confirmation occurs. The Hanafi school consistently emphasizes that qada' is required when one breaks the fast intentionally or mistakenly, but kaffarah is only for deliberate, certain violations without doubt.

Key Terms
ظن (zann): Assumption or belief that one thing is true without certainty; includes both weak and strong presumption (ghalabat al-zann).
شك (shakk): Doubt, equal probability between two possibilities.
غلب على ظنه (ghalabat al-zann): Overwhelming assumption that is close to certainty but still falls short of yaqin.
قضاء (qada'): Making up a missed fast on a later day.
كفارة (kaffarah): Expiation for intentionally breaking a Ramadan fast without valid excuse; consists of freeing a slave, fasting two months, or feeding sixty poor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling when a fasting person eats suhoor assuming it is still night, but later certifies that Fajr had risen?
A) The fast is valid, no qada'.
B) Qada' is wajib, kaffarah is not.
C) Qada' and kaffarah are both wajib.
D) Kaffarah is wajib, no qada'.

Key Takeaway
When a fasting person acts on assumption (zann) about the time and later confirms the time was wrong, qada' is wajib but kaffarah is not, due to the presence of doubt (shubha).

Kaffarah Debate When Breaking Fast on Mistaken Sunset Assumption
Explains intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on kaffarah when breaking fast with mistaken sunset assumption

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether kaffarah is obligatory when one breaks the fast based on an overwhelming assumption (ghalabat al-zann) that the sun has set, and identify the relied-upon position.

Core Idea
Breaking fast with mistaken sunset: qada' wajib, but kaffarah is dropped due to doubt (shubha).

This lesson delves into a specific scenario mentioned in the passage: a fasting person who breaks his fast based on an overwhelming assumption that maghrib has entered, but later discovers the sun had not set. While qada' is undisputed, the Hanafi mashayikh differed on kaffarah. We will explore the arguments and understand why the majority view favors no kaffarah. This lesson also clarifies the difference between doubt (shakk) and overwhelming assumption (ghalabat al-zann) in this context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the point of dispute
The scenario: a fasting person's 'overwhelming opinion' (غالب رأيه) is that the sun has set, so he breaks his fast. Then he finds the sun had not set. The scholars agree that qada' is wajib, but they differ on kaffarah.
The text says 'وإن كان غالب رأيه أنها لم تغرب فلا شك في وجوب القضاء عليه. واختلف المشايخ في وجوب الكفارة'.
2
State the two positions
Some mashayikh said kaffarah is obligatory; others said it is not. The text states that the correct (صحيح) view is that it is not obligatory.
The word 'الصحيح' indicates that the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is adopting the view of no kaffarah.
3
Analyze the reasoning for no kaffarah
The reason given: 'لأن احتمال الغروب قائم فكانت الشبهة ثابتة وهذه الكفارة لا تجب مع الشبهة' – because the possibility that the sun had set remains (at the time of action), so doubt (shubha) is present, and kaffarah is not obligatory with doubt.
This is a key principle: the probability that the act was actually permissible (sunset) at the moment of action creates a legal impediment to kaffarah.
4
Contrast with the case of doubt (shakk)
The text earlier says: 'وأراد بالظن في قوله «أو أفطر» كذلك غلبة الظن لأنه لو كان شاكاً تجب الكفارة' – By assumption in the matn, he means overwhelming assumption, because if he were merely doubtful (shakk), kaffarah would be obligatory. This seems to contradict the later dispute. How?
This requires careful resolution: The matn (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) says 'or if he breaks fast assuming it is night' – the commentator interprets 'assumption' as overwhelming assumption (ghalabat al-zann), not doubt. If it were doubt, kaffarah would be obligatory according to some? Actually the text says 'لأنه لو كان شاكاً تجب الكفارة' – this might be a minority view, or it refers to a different scenario (e.g., doubt about the sunset itself). The later dispute clarifies that the majority opinion rejects kaffarah even in overwhelming assumption.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon view is that no kaffarah is due in cases of mistaken sunset assumption, based on the principle of shubha. This is consistent with the earlier case of suhoor where kaffarah was also not due.
The overall Hanafi principle is that kaffarah is strictly for intentional, certain violations without any element of doubt (shubha).

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن ظن ليلاً أو تسحر مع ظن طلوع الفجر والفجر طالع فعليه القضاء ولا كفارة... وأراد بقوله «والفجر طالع» تيقن الطلوع... ولو غلب على ظنه أنه أكل بعد طلوع الفجر لا قضاء عليه ما لم يخبره رجل عدل... وأشار إلى أن التسحر ثابت واختلف فيه؛ فقيل مستحب، وقيل سنة... واعترضه في النهر بأنه إنما قيد بالليل ليطابق قوله «أو تسحر»... وأراد بالظن في قوله «أو أفطر» كذلك غلبة الظن لأنه لو كان شاكاً تجب الكفارة... وفي البدائع ما يخالفه ولفظه: وإن كان غالب رأيه أنها لم تغرب فلا شك في وجوب القضاء عليه. واختلف المشايخ في وجوب الكفارة؛ فقال بعضهم تجب وقال بعضهم لا تجب وهو الصحيح لأن احتمال الغروب قائم فكانت الشبهة ثابتة وهذه الكفارة لا تجب مع الشبهة.
And if he assumes it is night, or eats suhoor assuming Fajr has risen while Fajr is actually rising, then upon him is qada' and no kaffarah... And by his saying 'while Fajr is rising' he means certainty of the rising... And if it is overwhelmingly likely to him that he ate after Fajr, there is no qada' upon him unless an upright man informs him... And he indicated that suhoor is established; there is difference: some say it is recommended, some say it is sunnah... And it was objected by the author of al-Nahr that he only restricted to night to match his saying 'or he eats suhoor'... And by assumption in his saying 'or he breaks fast' he means overwhelming assumption, because if he were doubtful, kaffarah would be obligatory... And in al-Bada'i there is something contradicting it: 'And if his overwhelming opinion is that the sun has not set, then there is no doubt about the obligation of qada' upon him.' And the mashayikh differed regarding the obligation of kaffarah: some said it is obligatory, some said it is not, and that is correct because the possibility of sunset remains, so the doubt remains, and this kaffarah is not obligatory with doubt.
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with fiqh rulings when a fasting person acts on assumption (zann) or doubt (shakk) about the time of Fajr or sunset. The author first reiterates the matn: if one assumes it is still night and eats, but later finds Fajr had risen, qada' is required but not kaffarah. This is because the act is unintentional and the damage is incomplete; the intention was not to break the fast. The text then discusses the scenario of overwhelming assumption (ghalabat al-zann). If one is fairly sure he ate after Fajr but no confirmed report, there is no qada' according to the most authentic narration. However, if an upright person informs him, qada' is required. The author clarifies that 'assumption' in the matn includes doubt, because if one merely doubts, the ruling is the same. He then explains the case of breaking the fast at sunset: if one mistakenly assumes the sun has set and breaks his fast, but later finds it had not, qada' is obligatory, and there is disagreement on kaffarah. The correct position is no kaffarah because the presence of doubt (shubha) prevents it. The passage also notes a difference among scholars regarding the status of suhoor (pre-dawn meal): some say it is mustahabb, others sunnah. The relied-upon view is that it is mustahabb. Finally, the author outlines the various possible scenarios (24 or 36) based on combinations of doubt/assumption, time of day, and whether confirmation occurs. The Hanafi school consistently emphasizes that qada' is required when one breaks the fast intentionally or mistakenly, but kaffarah is only for deliberate, certain violations without doubt.

Key Terms
ظن (zann): Assumption or belief that one thing is true without certainty; includes both weak and strong presumption (ghalabat al-zann).
شك (shakk): Doubt, equal probability between two possibilities.
غلبة الظن (ghalabat al-zann): Overwhelming assumption that is close to certainty but still falls short of yaqin.
شبهة (shubha): Legal doubt or ambiguity that prevents strict liability.
قضاء (qada'): Making up a missed fast on a later day.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct Hanafi position, what is the ruling when a fasting person breaks his fast with overwhelming assumption that the sun has set, but later discovers the sun had not set?
A) Qada' wajib, kaffarah wajib.
B) Qada' wajib, no kaffarah.
C) No qada', no kaffarah.
D) Kaffarah only.

Key Takeaway
In cases of mistaken sunset assumption, the Hanafi mufta bihi is qada' without kaffarah, because the possibility of sunset (shubha) blocks the severe penalty.

Sawm — Fasting
advanced
15 min
Eating with Presumption (Zann) and the Ruling on Suhur in Fasting
Fasting rulings on eating with presumption and suhur

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings for a fasting person who eats based on presumption or doubt about sunset/dawn, and explain the legal status and evidence for suhur.

Core Idea
If a fasting person eats with a valid presumption of night, qada is required; kaffarah is dropped due to shubha; suhur is sunnah, not wajib.

This lesson unpacks a dense passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq that tackles two related but distinct issues: first, what happens when a fasting person eats based on a presumption (zann) that the night has entered or that the day remains; second, the legal nature of the pre-dawn meal (suhur). You will explore the intricate scenario-based rulings, the role of istishab (presumption of continuity), and how Hanafi scholars derived the recommended status of suhur from the prophetic command.

Guided Path
1
Understand the role of istishab and presumption
Recognize that the ruling of night's continuity is presumptive; when two presumptive evidences conflict, the original (night) prevails. The term 'zann' in the passage means predominant presumption (ghalabat al-zann), not mere doubt.
Istishab is a usul principle that presumes the continuation of a previous state until evidence of change.
2
Distinguish between zann (presumption) and shakk (doubt)
Note the difference: zann is a preponderant belief, shakk is equal probability. The legal consequences differ: kaffarah is obligatory for doubt but dropped when there is a valid presumption (shubha).
The passage explicitly states: 'if he were merely doubtful, the expiation would be obligatory'.
3
Analyze the 24 scenarios based on presumption type and outcome
Work through the cases: the person either presumes existence of the permissive cause (mubih - sunset) or the prohibitive cause (muharram - daytime remaining). Each scenario is further divided by what becomes clear later. Trace the rulings for qada and kaffarah.
The text also discusses a refinement to 18 cases by al-Zayla'i, critiquing the 24-case division. Understand the reasoning behind both.
4
Identify the mufta bihi: kaffarah not obligatory with shubha
The correct Hanafi position is that expiation (kaffarah) is not obligatory when there is a valid doubt (shubha), even if the person had a presumption. The passage cites al-Bada'i' stating: 'this expiation is not obligatory with doubt'.
This is a key practical outcome: Muslims are not burdened with extra kaffarah in ambiguous situations.
5
Examine the suhur hadith and its interpretation
The hadith 'Tasahharu fa inna fi al-sahuri barakah' is cited. Analyze how the imperative form is interpreted as recommendation (sunnah/mustahabb) rather than obligation, due to contextual evidence like 'barakah' (blessing). Also note the intra-Hanafi disagreement on its exact legal category.
The definition of 'sahur' (with damm or fatha) and the ruling on eating with doubt are covered.

كتاب الصوم فصل في العوارض
الجماعة إلا أبا داود «تسحروا فإن في السحور بركة» (۱) والسحور ما يؤكل في السحر وهو السدس الأخير من الليل. وقوله «في السحور هو على حذف مضاف تقديره في أكل السحور بركة بناء على ضبطه بضم السين جمع سحر، فأما على فتحها وهو الأعرف في الرواية فهو اسم للمأكول في السحر كالوضوء بالفتح ما يتوضأ به وقيل : يتعين الضم لأن البركة ونيل الثوب إنما يحصل بالفعل لا بنفس المأكول. كذا في فتح القدير. ومحل الاستحباب ما إذا يتيقن بقاء الليل أو غلب على ظنه أما إذا شك فالأفضل أن لا يتسحر تحرزاً عن المحرم ولم يجب عليه ذلك، ولو أكل فصومه تام لأن الأصل هو الليل. كذا في الهداية. وفي الفتاوى الظهيرية : وإذا تسحر ثم ظهر أن الفجر طالع أثم وقضى .اهـ. وهو بإطلاقه يتناول ما إذا غلب على ظنه بقاؤه فتسحر ثم تبين خلافه فإنه يأثم. وفي البدائع : وهل يكره الأكل مع الشك؟ روى هشام عن أبي يوسف أنه يكره، وروى ابن سماعة عن محمد أنه لا يكره، والصحيح قول أبي يوسف وعن الهندواني أنه إذا ظهر علامات الطلوع من ضرب الدبادب والأذان يكره وإلا فلا، ولا تعويل على ذلك لأنه مما يتقدم ويتأخر اهـ. والسنة في السحور التأخير لأن معنى الاستعانة فيه أبلغ، وكذا تعجيل الفطر كذا في البدائع. والتعجيل المستحب التعجيل قبل اشتباك النجوم ذكره قاضيخان في شرح الجامع الصغير. ولم أر صريحاً في أن الماء وحده يكون محصلاً لسنة السحور، وظاهر الحديث يفيده وهو ما رواه أحمد
كلامهم وأكبر الرأي، فلذا اقتصر في البحر على الأربعة والعشرين. ويراد بالظن حينئذ ما يشمل غلبته، ويرد عليهما جعل الشك تارة في وجود المبيح، وتارة في قيام المحرم ولا وجه له لأن الظن إنما صح تعلقه بالمبيح تارة وبالمحرم أخرى لأن له نسبة مخصوصة إلى أحد الطرفين، فإذا تعلق الظن بوجود الليل لا يكون متعلقاً بوجود النهار وبالعكس. وأما الشك فلا يتصور فيه ذلك لعدم ترجيح أحد الطرفين فيه، فإذا شك في قيام زيد كان معناه أن قيامه وعدمه على السواء فكان متعلقاً بكلا الطرفين، فيكون معنى شكه في طلوع الفجر في وقت احتمال وجود الليل ووجود النهار في ذلك الوقت على السواء، فكان الحق في التقسيم أن يقال : إما أن يظن وجود المبيح أو وجود المحرم أو يشك، وكل منهما إما أن يكون في ابتداء الصوم أو انتهائه، وفي كل من الست إما أن يتبين وجود المبيح أو وجود المحرم أو لا يتبين فهي ثمانية عشر تسعة في ابتداء الصوم، وتسعة في انتهائه . صنيع العلامة الزيلعي فإنه لم يذكر إلا ثمانية عشر وذكر أحكامها ا هـ. وهو كلام حسن
Book of Fasting, Section on Incidental Matters. The entry of the night into existence; as for the ruling of its remaining, it is presumptive because the statement of istishab and the indicator that would cause the absence of presumption of night's remaining is a presumptive evidence, so two presumptive evidences conflict regarding the existence of night and its non-existence, so they cancel each other out, and one acts on the original, which is the night, and its completion therein. And what is meant by presumption in his saying "or he broke the fast" is likewise predominant presumption, because if he were merely doubtful, the expiation would be obligatory. Thus in al-Mustasfa. And it was transmitted in Sharh al-Tahawi that there is disagreement among the scholars concerning it. And if nothing becomes clear to him, then upon him is make-up (qada). And in al-Tabyin, concerning the obligation of expiation, there are two narrations. And if it becomes clear that he ate after sunset, then expiation is obligatory. And it was restricted to his presuming the existence of the permissive cause (mubih) because if he presumed the existence of the prohibitive cause (muharram), such as presuming that the sun had not set and he ate, then upon him is make-up and expiation if nothing becomes clear to him or if it becomes clear that he ate before sunset. And if it becomes clear that he ate at night, then nothing is upon him in all that we have mentioned. Thus in al-Tabyin. And in al-Bada'i' there is what contradicts it, and its wording: If his predominant opinion is that it (the sun) has not set, then there is no doubt about the obligation of make-up upon him. And the scholars disagreed concerning the obligation of expiation; some said it is obligatory, and some said it is not obligatory, and this is the correct position because the possibility of sunset remains, so the doubt (shubha) is established, and this expiation is not obligatory with doubt. So the summary is that either he presumes or he doubts. If he presumes, then it is not free from either his presuming the existence of the permissive cause or the existence of the prohibitive cause. If it is the first, then it is not free from either nothing becomes clear to him, or it becomes clear that his presumption was correct, or it becomes clear that it was false. And each of the three either occurs at the beginning of the fast or at its end, so they are six. And if he doubts, then they are twelve in the existence of the permissive cause, and similarly in the existence of the prohibitive cause, so they are twenty-four. And their rulings are known from the text explicitly and implicitly, so let one ponder. He pointed out that the pre-dawn meal (suhur) is established, and there is disagreement concerning it: it is said to be recommended (mustahabb), and it is said to be sunnah. The first was chosen in al-Zahiriyyah, and the second in al-Bada'i', each of them confining himself to it. Its evidence is the hadith generally here, and its being called suhur is based on presumption (zann) and the obligation? Actually it is not correct to express it here because it becomes clear that it occurred during the day, and if he presumes it is daytime, then it is correct to call it suhur also based on the possibility of the night remaining. Ponder his statement: (presumptive evidence) what is appropriate is 'two presumptive evidences' or an explicit statement of the first? He says because the statement of istishab is a presumptive evidence. His statement: (and it was transmitted in Sharh al-Tahawi that there is disagreement among the scholars) I say: what will come from al-Bada'i' of validating the non-obligation of expiation when his predominant opinion is that it has not set necessitates validating non-obligation in case of doubt even more so. His statement: (and in al-Bada'i' there is what contradicts it, etc.) it cannot be said that the contradiction can be removed by interpreting what is in al-Bada'i' as applying to when it becomes clear that he ate at night. His statement: (so they are twenty-four) al-Nahr increased them to thirty-six by making predominant presumption a separate category along with presumption and doubt, so the categories emerging from the first division are three, each with twelve, so they reach what he said. And some virtuous person objected to him that there is no benefit in distinguishing between them (i.e., presumption and predominant presumption) here because they did not differentiate between them in the ruling, as is evident to one who ponders the expression of al-Zayla'i and others. Yes, there is a conceptual difference between them: mere weighting of one side of the ruling in the intellect is the basis of presumption (zann); if that weighting increases until it approaches certainty, it is called predominant presumption (ghalabat al-zann).

Book of Fasting, Section on Incidental Matters: the congregation except Abu Dawud: 'Take the pre-dawn meal (sahharu), for in suhur there is blessing' (1). And as-sahur (with damm on sin) is what is eaten at dawn, which is the last sixth of the night. And his statement 'fi al-suhuri' (in the suhur) involves a deleted genitive construct, its implied meaning being 'in eating suhur there is blessing' based on its vowelling with damm on the sin, plural of sahar. As for with fatha, which is more well-known in transmission, it is a noun for what is eaten at dawn, like al-wudu' (with fatha) is what one performs wudu with. And it was said: damm is definitive because blessing and obtaining reward occur only through the action, not through the food itself. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. The place of recommendation is when he is certain of the night's remaining or has predominant presumption. As for if he doubts, the better is not to have suhur to avoid the prohibitive cause, and it is not obligatory upon him to do so. And if he eats, his fast is complete because the original is the night. Thus in al-Hidayah. And in al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyyah: If he had suhur and then it becomes apparent that dawn had risen, he sins and makes up (qada). End. And its absolute wording includes when his predominant presumption is its remaining, so he had suhur and then it turns out contrary, so he sins. And in al-Bada'i': Is it disliked (makruh) to eat with doubt? Hisham narrated from Abu Yusuf that it is disliked, and Ibn Sama'a narrated from Muhammad that it is not disliked, and the correct is the statement of Abu Yusuf. And from al-Hinduwani: If signs of dawn appear, such as beating of drums and adhan, it is disliked; otherwise not. And no reliance is placed on that because it can be earlier or later. End. And the sunnah in suhur is delay because the meaning of assistance is more effective therein, and likewise hastening in breaking the fast. Thus in al-Bada'i'. And the recommended hastening is to break before the stars are entangled, mentioned by Qadikhan in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir. And I have not seen explicitly that water alone suffices to achieve the sunnah of suhur, but the apparent meaning of the hadith indicates it, and it is what Ahmad narrated: their statement and the greater opinion, so al-Bahr confined himself to twenty-four. And by 'presumption' (zann) then is meant what includes predominant presumption. And there is objection against both regarding making doubt sometimes in existence of the permissive cause and sometimes in existence of the prohibitive cause; there is no basis for it because presumption can only correctly relate to the permissive cause sometimes and to the prohibitive cause other times because it has a specific relation to one of the two sides. So if presumption relates to the existence of night, it does not relate to the existence of day, and vice versa. As for doubt, that is not conceivable because there is no weighting of one of the two sides. So if one doubts whether Zayd is standing, it means that his standing and not standing are equal, so it relates to both sides. So the meaning of his doubt about dawn at a time is that the probability of night's existence and day's existence at that time are equal. So the correct in division is to say: either he presumes existence of the permissive cause, or existence of the prohibitive cause, or doubts. And each of them either at the beginning of the fast or at its end. And in each of the six, either it becomes clear that the permissive cause exists, or that the prohibitive cause exists, or it does not become clear. So they are eighteen: nine at the beginning of the fast and nine at its end. The practice of the great scholar al-Zayla'i is that he only mentioned eighteen and mentioned their rulings. End. And it is good speech.
Source pages p. 510

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on the section 'Fasl fi al-'Awarid' (Section on Incidental Matters) from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It deals with two main issues: (1) the ruling on a fasting person who eats based on a presumption (zann) that the night has entered (permissive cause) or that the day remains (prohibitive cause), and (2) the ruling on the pre-dawn meal (suhur).

The author begins by explaining the legal principle of istishab (presumption of continuity) regarding the night: the ruling of night's continuity is presumptive because it is based on istishab, which is a presumptive evidence. When two presumptive evidences conflict (one for night remaining, one for day having entered), they cancel out, and the original ruling (the night) is upheld. The author clarifies that the term 'zann' in the phrase 'or he broke the fast' refers to predominant presumption (ghalabat al-zann), not mere doubt; if the person was merely doubtful, expiation (kaffarah) would be obligatory.

The passage then details several scenarios based on whether the person presumes (zann) or doubts (shakk), the type of presumption (presence of the permissive cause vs. presence of the prohibitive cause), and what becomes clear later (nothing, confirmation, or contradiction). The total possible scenarios are enumerated, with a discussion on whether it is 18, 24, or 36 cases. The author notes that the relied-upon position is that expiation is not obligatory when there is a valid doubt (shubha) because kaffarah is dropped with doubt.

The second part covers suhur: it is either mustahabb or sunnah, with evidence from the hadith 'Tasahharu fa inna fi al-sahuri barakah'. The definition of 'sahur' (with damm) or 'suhur' (with fatha) is explained. The ruling depends on certainty or predominant presumption of night remaining; if there is doubt, it is better to avoid suhur, but if one eats, the fast is valid. The Sunnah is to delay suhur and hasten iftar. The author also discusses whether it is disliked to eat with doubt, citing narrations from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, with the correct position being that of Abu Yusuf (it is disliked). Finally, a critique of the earlier division of 24 cases is presented, suggesting that the correct number of distinct scenarios is 18, following al-Zayla'i.

Key Terms
الظن (az-zann): In this passage, zann refers to predominant presumption (ghalabat al-zann), not mere speculation or doubt. It is a preponderant belief about the existence of something (e.g., night or day) that influences legal consequences.
المبيح (al-mubih): The permissive cause that allows breaking the fast, i.e., the occurrence of sunset (dusk). If a person presumes that the sun has set, he may eat based on that presumption.
المحرم (al-muharram): The prohibitive cause that forbids eating, i.e., the remaining of daytime (that the sun has not yet set). If a person presumes that it is still day, he should not eat.
السحور (as-sahur (or as-suhur)): The pre-dawn meal eaten before the Fajr prayer during Ramadan. The author defines it as food eaten at dawn (sahar), which is the last sixth of the night.
الشبهة (ash-shubhah): A valid doubt or ambiguity that arises from a legitimate possibility, which in Hanafi law can drop the obligation of kaffarah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct Hanafi position stated in this passage, what is the ruling on kaffarah (expiation) for a fasting person who eats based on predominant presumption (ghalabat al-zann) that the sun has set, when it later becomes clear that the sun had not set?
A) Kaffarah is obligatory because he ate during the day.
B) Kaffarah is not obligatory because the possibility of sunset creates a valid doubt (shubha).
C) Kaffarah is obligatory only if he was doubtful (shakk) rather than having presumption.
D) Kaffarah is obligatory and qada is not required.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, qada is wajib for breaking a fast even with a mistaken presumption, but kaffarah is not obligatory when a valid doubt (shubha) exists; suhur is a recommended practice based on the prophetic hadith, not an obligation.

Virtue and Ruling of Suhoor in the Hanafi School
Explains suhoor definition, virtue, and ruling with doubt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of suhoor, explain the Hanafi interpretation of the hadith 'tasarrahu fa inna fi al-suhuri baraka,' and apply the rule to cases of certainty and doubt.

Core Idea
Suhoor is a recommended sunnah, not an obligation; its blessing is in the act of eating, not merely the food.

Suhoor, the pre-dawn meal, is a beloved practice in Ramadan. This lesson unpacks the famous hadith about its blessing and the Hanafi ruling on when to eat it, especially when uncertainty about dawn arises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what suhoor is and its legal status: mustahabb (recommended), not obligatory. The hadith command is interpreted as recommendation due to contextual evidence.
Hanafi usul distinguishes between commands for obligation and recommendation.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary evidence is the hadith: 'تسحروا فإن في السحور بركة' (Take suhoor, for in suhoor there is blessing). It is narrated by the group except Abu Dawud.
The hadith is ahad (single-transmitter), making it zanni al-thubut.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative 'تسحروا' indicates recommendation (nadb) in Hanafi usul. This is because other evidence (ease, companion practice) shows it is not obligatory. The blessing is in the action of eating, not the food itself.
Linguistic analysis: suhoor with dammah means time, with fatha means food; the blessing attaches to the action.
4
Apply to the doubt scenario
When one is certain or strongly believes night remains, suhoor is recommended. If in doubt, it is better not to eat to avoid the risk of eating after dawn. However, if one eats, the fast is valid because the default presumption is that night continues.
The principle 'al-asl baqa' ma kana' (the default is continuation) applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصوم فصل في العوارض الجماعة إلا أبا داود «تسحروا فإن في السحور بركة» والسحور ما يؤكل في السحر وهو السدس الأخير من الليل. وقوله «في السحور هو على حذف مضاف تقديره في أكل السحور بركة بناء على ضبطه بضم السين جمع سحر، فأما على فتحها وهو الأعرف في الرواية فهو اسم للمأكول في السحر كالوضوء بالفتح ما يتوضأ به وقيل : يتعين الضم لأن البركة ونيل الثوب إنما يحصل بالفعل لا بنفس المأكول. كذا في فتح القدير. ومحل الاستحباب ما إذا يتيقن بقاء الليل أو غلب على ظنه أما إذا شك فالأفضل أن لا يتسحر تحرزاً عن المحرم ولم يجب عليه ذلك، ولو أكل فصومه تام لأن الأصل هو الليل. كذا في الهداية.
Book of Fasting, Section on Contingencies. The group except Abu Dawud: 'Take suhoor, for in suhoor there is blessing.' And suhoor is what is eaten at dawn, which is the last sixth of the night. And His statement 'in suhoor' involves an omitted genitive construct, its meaning: in eating suhoor there is blessing, based on its vowel with dammah on the seen as plural of sahr. As for with fatha, which is more known in narration, it is a name for what is eaten at dawn, like wudu' with fatha for what one performs wudu' with. And it is said: dammah is determined because blessing and attainment of reward occur by the action, not by the food itself. Thus in Fath al-Qadeer. And the place of recommendation is when one is certain of the remaining of the night or it is dominant in his opinion. As for if he doubts, it is better not to take suhoor to guard against the prohibited, and it is not obligatory upon him. And if he eats, his fast is complete because the default is the night. Thus in Al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 511

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, discussing the virtue and ruling of suhoor. It begins by citing the hadith narrated by the group except Abu Dawud: 'Take suhoor, for in suhoor there is blessing.' The author explains the linguistic meaning of 'suhoor': with dammah, it refers to the last sixth of the night (the time); with fatha, it refers to the food eaten. The recommended (mustahabb) time for suhoor is when one is certain or has a dominant opinion that night remains. If in doubt, it is better not to eat to avoid the prohibited (eating after dawn). If one eats despite doubt, the fast is valid because the default presumption is that night continues. This follows the Hanafi position that suhoor is recommended, not obligatory.

Key Terms
سحور (suhoor): The pre-dawn meal eaten in the last sixth of the night for fasting.
بركة (barakah): Blessing, increase in good.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person is in doubt about whether dawn has broken, what is the preferred action regarding suhoor?
A) It is obligatory to eat.
B) It is recommended to eat.
C) It is better not to eat.
D) The fast is invalid if one eats.

Key Takeaway
Suhoor is a recommended sunnah; if in doubt, abstaining is better, but eating does not break the fast.

Sin and Qada if Dawn Appears After Suhoor
Ruling on eating suhoor then discovering dawn had risen

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a person eats suhoor with a dominant opinion that night remains but later discovers dawn had risen, and identify the consequence of sin and qada.

Core Idea
Eating suhoor after dawn, even with a dominant belief it is still night, incurs sin and requires qada.

This lesson examines a specific case from Al-Fatawa al-Zuhayriyya: a person takes suhoor believing night remains, then learns dawn had already broken. The text rules that he sins and must make up the fast. We will parse the ruling, understand why both sin and qada apply, and see how this reflects the Hanafi emphasis on precaution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is eating suhoor. The ruling: he sins (athima) and must make up (qada) the fast.
2
Examine the condition
The person had a dominant opinion (ghalaba al-ẓann) that night remained. The text explicitly includes this case.
The ruling is the same even without dominant opinion; the text's absolute wording covers it.
3
Understand the basis
Actual dawn had risen, so eating after dawn is haram. The sin is for violating the prohibition. Qada is because the fast was invalidated.
Intentional eating after dawn invalidates the fast regardless of mistaken belief.
4
Note the authority
The ruling is from Al-Fatawa al-Zuhayriyya, a later Hanafi fatwa collection. This is the mufta bihi position.
It is consistent with the Hanafi principle of ihtiyat (precaution) in fasting.

Source Text

And in Al-Fatawa al-Zuhayriyya: And if he took suhoor then it appeared that the dawn had risen, he sins and makes it up. End quote. And this, in its absolute sense, includes when it was dominant in his opinion that it remained, so he ate suhoor, then it became clear contrary to that, then he sins.
Source pages p. 511

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from Al-Fatawa al-Zuhayriyya. It clarifies that if a person eats suhoor and then it becomes evident that dawn had already broken, he has sinned and must make up that day of fasting. The comment explains that this applies even if he had a dominant opinion that night was still present. The sin is for eating after dawn unintentionally due to lack of precaution. The qada is wajib because the fast was invalidated by intentional eating after dawn (even if he thought it was still night).

Key Terms
أثم (athima): He incurs sin (ithm) for violating the prohibition of eating after dawn.
قضى (qada): He makes up the fast on a later day.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, eating suhoor after actual dawn, even with a dominant belief that it is still night, incurs sin and requires qada.

Eating Suhoor with Doubt: The Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ruling of suhoor with doubt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on eating suhoor when in doubt about dawn, identify the sahih view, and explain its practical consequence.

Core Idea
Eating suhoor with doubt is disliked (makruh tanzihi) — the preferred view of Abu Yusuf.

In Al-Bada'i', an intra-Hanafi dispute is presented: is it disliked to eat suhoor when one is uncertain whether dawn has arrived? Two narrations from two great imams, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, offer different rulings. The author resolves the dispute, declaring Abu Yusuf's view as sahih. This lesson unpacks the positions and the practical implication for fasting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two narrations
Hisham narrates from Abu Yusuf that it is disliked (yukrah); Ibn Sama'ah narrates from Muhammad that it is not disliked.
These are two riwayat within the Hanafi school.
2
Compare the rulings
Abu Yusuf holds it is makruh tanzihi; Muhammad holds it is not makruh. Note that both agree the fast is valid — the dispute is only about desirability.
3
Identify the tarjih
The author states 'the correct view is the statement of Abu Yusuf' (al-sahih qawl Abi Yusuf). Thus the relied-upon position is that it is disliked.
Tarjih here is based on the strength of evidence, not specified in this text.
4
Evaluate the third opinion
Al-Hindwani differentiates based on external signs (drums, adhan). The author dismisses this because signs may be early or late, so it's unreliable.
This indicates that subjective signs are not a sound basis for legal ruling.
5
Derive the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: according to the mufta bihi, eating with doubt is disliked but the fast remains valid. According to Muhammad, there is no dislike.
The student should understand that the difference affects reward, not validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي البدائع : وهل يكره الأكل مع الشك؟ روى هشام عن أبي يوسف أنه يكره، وروى ابن سماعة عن محمد أنه لا يكره، والصحيح قول أبي يوسف وعن الهندواني أنه إذا ظهر علامات الطلوع من ضرب الدبادب والأذان يكره وإلا فلا، ولا تعويل على ذلك لأنه مما يتقدم ويتأخر اهـ.
And in Al-Bada'i': And is eating with doubt disliked? Hisham narrated from Abu Yusuf that it is disliked, and Ibn Sama'ah narrated from Muhammad that it is not disliked, and the correct is the statement of Abu Yusuf. And from al-Hindwani: if signs of dawn appear from beating drums and the call to prayer, it is disliked; otherwise, no. And there is no reliance on that because it can come early or late. End quote.
Source pages p. 511

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding eating suhoor when in doubt about dawn. Two narrations are mentioned: from Abu Yusuf that it is makruh (disliked), and from Muhammad that it is not makruh. The author declares the sahih (correct) position to be that of Abu Yusuf. Then a third opinion from al-Hindwani is noted: if there are external signs like drums or adhan, it is makruh; otherwise not. However, the author dismisses this as unreliable because such signs may be early or late. The relied-upon view is that eating with doubt is disliked (makruh tanzihi).

Key Terms
يكره (yukrah): It is disliked (makruh tanzihi), meaning leaves the better but not sinful.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is haram.
B) It is disliked.
C) It is permissible and not disliked.
D) It is obligatory.

Key Takeaway
Eating suhoor with doubt about dawn is disliked (makruh tanzihi) according to the relied-upon Hanafi view, but the fast remains valid.

Sunnah of Delaying Suhoor and Hastening Iftar
Sunnah rulings on suhoor and iftar timing and sufficiency of water for suhoor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the sunnah rulings of suhoor timing and iftar timing, and explain why even a sip of water fulfills the sunnah of suhoor.

Core Idea
Delay suhoor, hasten iftar; even a sip of water earns blessings.

This lesson explores the recommended practices surrounding the timing of suhoor and iftar. We will see that the Hanafi school considers it sunnah to delay suhoor and hasten iftar, and that even a small amount of water fulfills the sunnah of suhoor, based on a hadith from Ahmad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their sunnah ruling
The passage discusses three acts: the timing of suhoor (delay), the timing of iftar (hasten), and the minimum amount for suhoor (any food or drink, even water). All are sunnah muakkadah.
Suhoor and iftar timings are sunnah, not obligatory.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is a hadith narrated by Ahmad: 'All of suhoor is blessing, so do not leave it, even if one of you takes a sip of water…'
The hadith is ahad (single-chain) so it yields zanni knowledge.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'do not leave it' indicates desirability, not obligation. The phrase 'even a sip of water' shows that any amount, including water, fulfills the sunnah.
The imperative 'do not leave' is interpreted as recommendation due to other evidence.
4
Note the subtlety
The author notes he found no explicit statement that water alone suffices, but the apparent meaning (zahir) of the hadith clearly indicates it. This shows the method of relying on textual implication.
This is an example of expansion (tatbiq) of the hadith's scope.
5
Confirm the mufta bihi
The Hanafi position is that delaying suhoor and hastening iftar are sunnah, and water alone fulfills the sunnah of suhoor. There is no significant ikhtilaf on this point.
The ruling is muttafaq alayh across schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the recommended hastening is to hasten before the intermixing of stars; Qadikhan mentioned it in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir. And I have not seen explicitly that water alone fulfills the sunnah of suhoor, but the apparent meaning of the hadith indicates it, and it is what Ahmad narrated from Abu Sa'id as a musnad: 'All of suhoor is blessing, so do not leave it, even if one of you takes a sip of water, for Allah and His angels send blessings upon those who take suhoor.' And blessing in the hadith linguistically means increase and growth, and the increase includes: increase in strength to perform the fast, increase in permission to eat and drink, and increase in times when supplication is answered. Thus al-Kalabadhi mentioned it, and its meaning is perfectly clear.

Commentary
This passage states the sunnah rulings regarding the timings of suhoor and iftar. It is sunnah to delay suhoor (eat as late as possible) because it provides more strength for the fast. Similarly, it is sunnah to hasten iftar. The recommended hastening is to break fast before the stars become entangled (i.e., soon after sunset). The author then discusses whether drinking water alone suffices to follow the sunnah of suhoor. Although he found no explicit statement, the apparent meaning of the hadith narrated by Ahmad indicates that even a sip of water fulfills the sunnah, as the hadith says 'even a sip of water'. The blessings of suhoor are multiple: physical strength, permission to eat, and opportunity for du'a at that blessed time.

Key Terms
سنة (sunnah): Recommended practice; leaving it is not sinful but missing it is a loss.
تجريع (tajru'): Taking a sip or small amount of water.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It fulfills the sunnah of suhoor.
C) It is only permissible but not sunnah.
D) It is obligatory to have a full meal.

Key Takeaway
The sunnah of suhoor is fulfilled by even a sip of water, and the timings (delaying suhoor, hastening iftar) bring increased blessings.
