Hastening Iftar and Conflicting Testimony on Sunset/Dawn
Rulings on when to break fast and conflicting testimonies about sunset/dawn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hastening iftar, apply the maxim 'al-bayyinat lil-ithbat la lil-nafy' to resolve conflicting testimony about sunset/dawn, and determine whether qada and kaffarah are required in various scenarios.

Core Idea
Break fast only when sunset is dominant; in testimony conflicts, the affirmer's word is accepted over the denier's.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses two connected rulings: the recommended timing for iftar and the evidentiary principle for resolving conflicting testimonies about sunset or dawn. The key maxim is that legal evidence (bayyinat) is for affirmation, not negation — so the testimony of those affirming an event outweighs that of those denying it. We'll examine how this principle applies when witnesses disagree about sunset or dawn, and what consequences (qada and kaffarah) follow if a person acts on the accepted testimony but is later proven wrong.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on hastening iftar
The text states it is recommended (mustahabb) to hasten iftar, except on a cloudy day. The condition for breaking fast is a dominant opinion (ghalabat al-zann) that the sun has set, even if the adhan is given. This establishes a cautious approach: do not break fast unless you are fairly certain.
The term 'ghalaba 'ala zannihi' is a legal standard weaker than certainty but sufficient for action.
2
Understand the maxim 'al-bayyinat lil-ithbat la lil-nafy'
This maxim means that evidence is designed to prove a positive claim, not a negative one. In court, the testimony of those affirming an occurrence (e.g., sunset) is preferred over those denying it. This is the underlying principle for the cases that follow.
This maxim is a Hanafi usul principle; other schools may have different standards.
3
Apply the maxim to conflicting testimonies about sunset
Two witnesses say the sun set, two say it did not. The person breaks fast based on the affirmers. Later it becomes clear the sun had not set. Ruling: he must make up the fast (qada) but no kaffarah, by agreement. This is because the affirmers' testimony is accepted in principle, leading to a valid excuse.
The absence of kaffarah here contrasts with the dawn case below.
4
Apply the maxim to conflicting testimonies about dawn
Two witnesses say dawn has risen, two say it has not. The person eats suhoor believing the affirmers. Later dawn is confirmed. Ruling: he must make up the fast AND pay kaffarah. Why the difference? Because eating after dawn intentionally, even based on testimony, violates the fast once dawn is certain; the testimony only removes the excuse for negligence.
Kaffarah is for intentional violation; qada is for the missed obligatory fast.
5
Analyze the scenario of one witness vs multiple
If one witness says dawn has risen and two say it has not, the person believes the one and eats, then it turns out dawn had risen. Ruling: no kaffarah. This is because the report of a single person is not legally sufficient to establish dawn in such matters; thus his act is not considered intentional based on reliable evidence. Similarly, if multiple informants tell him during suhoor that dawn has risen, and he believes them, then it appears dawn was not yet risen at the start but rose later: no kaffarah because he lacked the intention to fast from the beginning.
The distinction hinges on the legal reliability of the informant(s).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي البزازية : ويستحب تعجيل الإفطار إلا في يوم غيم، ولا يفطر ما لم يغلب على ظنه غروب الشمس وإن أذن المؤذن .اهـ. وذكر قبله : شهدا أنها غربت وآخران بأنها لم تغرب وأفطر ثم بان عدم الغروب قضى ولا كفارة عليه بالاتفاق شهدا على طلوع الفجر وآخران على عدم الطلوع فأكل ثم بأن الطلوع قضى وكفر وفاقاً لأن البينات للإثبات لا للنفي حتى قبل شهادة المثبت لا النافي. ولو واحد على طلوعه وآخران على عدمه لا كفارة عليه. دخلوا عليه وهو يتسحر فقالوا إنه طالع فصدقهم فقال إذن أنا مفطر لا صائم ثم دام على الأكل ثم بان أنه ما كان طالعاً في أول الأكل وطالعاً وقت الأكل الثاني قال النسفي الحاكم : لا كفارة عليه لعدم نية الصوم، وإن كان المخبر واحداً عليه الكفارة لأن خبر الواحد عدلاً أولا في مثل هذا لا يقبل اهـ.
And in Al-Bazzaziyya: It is recommended to hasten iftar except on a cloudy day, and he should not break fast unless he has dominant opinion of sunset, even if the mu'adhdhin calls. End quote. And he mentioned before it: Two witnesses testified that the sun set, and two others that it did not set, and he broke fast, then it became clear that it did not set: he makes up and there is no kaffarah upon him by agreement. They testified to the rising of dawn, and two others to non-rising, and he ate, then it became clear that it had risen: he makes up and there is kaffarah, in agreement, because testimonies are for affirmation, not negation, so the testimony of the affirmer is accepted over the negator. And if one witnesses that it rose and two that it did not, there is no kaffarah upon him. They entered upon him while he was eating suhoor and said it has risen, so he believed them and said 'Then I am one who breaks fast, not fasting,' then he continued eating, then it became clear that it had not risen at the start of eating, and had risen at the time of the second eating. Al-Nasafi al-Hakim said: There is no kaffarah due to lack of intention of fasting; if the informant was one person, there is kaffarah because the report of a single person, even if just, is not accepted in such a matter. End quote.
Source pages p. 512

Commentary
This passage covers several rulings related to iftar timing and conflicting testimony. It states that hastening iftar is recommended except on a cloudy day, and one should not break fast until he has a dominant opinion that the sun has set, even if the call to prayer is given. In case of conflicting testimonies, the principle is that evidence is for affirmation, not negation, so the testimony of those affirming sunrise or sunset is accepted over those denying it. For example, if two testify that the sun set and two that it did not, and he breaks fast, he must make up but no kaffarah. If two testify to dawn and two against, and he eats, he must make up and also pay kaffarah. If one testifies to dawn and two against, no kaffarah. The scenario of being told dawn has risen while eating suhoor is addressed: if he believes multiple informants, no kaffarah because he lacked intention to fast; if one informant, kaffarah because the report of one is not legally acceptable in such a case.

Key Terms
البينات للإثبات لا للنفي (al-bayyinat lil-ithbat la lil-nafy): Legal evidence is for establishing a matter, not disproving it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two witnesses testify that dawn has risen, and two that it has not. A person eats suhoor on the basis of the first testimony, but later dawn is confirmed. What is the ruling?
A) No qada and no kaffarah.
B) Qada and kaffarah.
C) Qada but no kaffarah.
D) Kaffarah but no qada.

Key Takeaway
Hasten iftar only when sunset is probable; in testimony conflicts, affirmers win, but the consequences depend on whether the act was eating after confirmed dawn or breaking fast after sunset.

When Shubha Waives Kaffarah for Breaking Fast
Conditions for kaffarah when breaking fast due to shubha

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which kaffarah is waived due to shubha and apply the concept to scenarios of mistaken belief.

Core Idea
Shubha (plausible doubt) waives kaffarah; ignorance does not.

This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies when kaffarah is not required despite deliberate breaking of a Ramadan fast. The key concept is shubha—a legally recognized doubt arising from similarity of acts or scholarly disagreement. We'll examine the scenarios that generate shubha and those that do not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is deliberately breaking a Ramadan fast. The default ruling is that kaffarah (expiation) is obligatory. The passage explains exceptions.
2
Recognize the two sources of shubha
Shubha can arise from similarity of acts (shubha al-istibha) or from scholarly disagreement (shubha al-ikhtilaf). Both waive kaffarah.
3
Classify each scenario
Examine the given cases: eating after forgetful eating, vomiting, wet dream, cupping/backbiting. Determine whether shubha is present based on similarity or known disagreement.
4
Evaluate the role of seeking a fatwa
If a layman consults a qualified mufti and relies on his fatwa, it creates shubha even if the mufti is wrong. Without consulting, mere ignorance is not an excuse.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The text declares Abu Hanifa's view as correct (al-sahih): no kaffarah in any shubha case. This is the operative ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما لم تجب الكفارة بإفطاره عمداً بعد أكله أو شربه أو جماعه ناسياً لأنه ظن في موضع الاشتباه بالنظير وهو الأكل عمداً لأن الأكل مضاد للصوم ساهياً أو عامداً فأورث شبهة. وكذا فيه شبهة اختلاف العلماء فإن مالكاً يقول بفساد صوم من أكل ناسياً. وأطلقه فشمل ما إذا اعلم بأنه لا يفطره بأن بلغه الحديث أو الفتوى أولاً وهو قول أبي حنيفة وهو الصحيح لأن العلماء اختلفوا في قبول الحديث فإن فقهاء المدينة كمالك وغيره لم يقبلوه فصار شبهة لأن قول الشافعي إذا كان موافقاً للقياس يكون شبهة كقول الصحابي، وكذا لو ذرعه القيء فظن أنه يفطره فأفطر لا كفارة عليه لوجود شبهة الاشتباه بالنظير فإن القيء والاستقاء متشابهان لأن مخرجهما من الفم. وكذا لو احتلم للتشابه في قضاء الشهوة، وإن علم أن ذلك لا يفطره فعليه الكفارة لأنه لم توجد شبهة الاشتباه ولا شبهة الاختلاف وقيد بالنسيان لأنه لو احتجم أو اغتاب فظن أنه يفطره ثم أكل إن لم يستفت فقيها ولا بلغه الخبر فعليه الكفارة لأنه مجرد جهل وأنه ليس بعذر في دار الإسلام، وإن استفتى فقيهاً لا كفارة عليه لأن العامي يجب عليه تقليد العالم إذا كان يعتمد على فتواه فكان معذوراً فيما صنع، وإن كان المفتي مخطئاً فيما أفتى وإن لم يستفت ولكن بلغه الخبر وهو قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام أفطر الحاجم والمحجوم» وقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم الغيبة تفطر الصائم ولم يعرف النسخ ولا تأويله فلا كفارة عليه عندهما لأن ظاهر الحديث
And the kaffarah is not obligatory by his deliberate breaking of fast after eating, drinking, or intercourse forgetfully, because he had a notion in a place of confusion with its like, and that is eating deliberately, because eating is opposed to fasting forgetfully or deliberately, so it generated shubha. Likewise, there is shubha of difference of scholars, for Malik says the fast of one who eats forgetfully is invalid. And it was absolute, so it included whether he knew that it does not break the fast because he was reached by hadith or fatwa first? And it is the statement of Abu Hanifa and it is correct because scholars differed in accepting the hadith, for the fuqaha of Madina like Malik and others did not accept it, so it became shubha. Because the statement of al-Shafi'i when it agrees with qiyas is a shubha like the statement of a companion. And likewise if he was overcome by vomiting and thought it breaks his fast, so he broke fast: no kaffarah upon him due to existence of shubha of confusion with its like, for vomiting and bringing up vomit are similar in that their exit is from the mouth. Likewise if he had a wet dream due to similarity in fulfilling desire. And if he knew that that does not break his fast, then kaffarah is obligatory because there is no shubha of confusion nor shubha of difference. And it is restricted with forgetfulness, because if he was cupped or backbit and thought that it breaks his fast, then he ate: if he did not ask a faqih nor was the report conveyed to him, then kaffarah is upon him because it is mere ignorance and it is not an excuse in Dar al-Islam. And if he asked a faqih, no kaffarah because the layman must follow the scholar if he relies on his fatwa, so he is excused in what he did, even if the mufti erred in his fatwa. And if he did not ask but the report reached him, namely his statement peace be upon him: 'The cupper and the cupped break the fast' and his statement 'Backbiting breaks the fast of the faster' and he did not know of abrogation nor its interpretation, then no kaffarah upon him according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) because the apparent meaning of the hadith...
Source pages p. 512

Commentary
This passage explains when kaffarah (expiation) is not required for deliberately breaking a fast. The key reason is 'shubha' (doubt/delusion). Several scenarios are discussed: if one eats forgetfully, then deliberately breaks fast, kaffarah is not wajib because there is shubha—the act of eating is similar whether forgetful or deliberate. Similarly, the disagreement among scholars (e.g., Malik holds that forgetful eating invalidates the fast) creates shubha. Other cases: vomiting (similar to intentional vomiting), wet dream (similar to sexual discharge). If one knows the act does not break the fast (e.g., cupping, backbiting) but still thinks it does, kaffarah is wajib unless he relied on a scholar's fatwa or an authentic hadith that contradicts his knowledge. The principle is that ignorance is not an excuse in Dar al-Islam, but following a qualified scholar excuses even if the scholar is wrong. The opinion of Abu Hanifa is that kaffarah is not required in these shubha cases.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubha): A doubt or delusion that prevents the legal consequence (kaffarah) due to similarity or conflicting evidence.
كفارة (kaffarah): Expiation for deliberately breaking a Ramadan fast without valid reason: freeing slave, fasting 60 days, or feeding 60 poor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because he intentionally broke it.
B) No, because there is shubha from similarity.
C) Yes, because he knew vomiting does not break fast.
D) No, because he was ignorant.

Key Takeaway
Kaffarah is waived when there is shubha, whether from similarity of acts, scholarly disagreement, or reliance on a scholar's fatwa.

The Blessings of Suhur
Hadith and commentary on the barakah of suhur

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on suhur, explain the legal implication of the hadith's imperative, and identify the three dimensions of barakah mentioned in the commentary.

Core Idea
Suhur is a confirmed sunnah; even a sip of water brings divine blessings and reward.

In this passage, the author cites a hadith to establish the virtue and recommended status of the pre-dawn meal (suhur). We will examine the hadith text, its legal implication in Hanafi usul, and the conceptual expansion of 'barakah' to appreciate both the physical and spiritual dimensions of suhur. This will clarify why suhur is emphasized even if minimal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being discussed (suhur)? What legal ruling does the hadith indicate according to the Hanafi school?
The ruling is derived from the imperative and the context of blessings.
2
Locate the dalil and its transmission
The hadith from Abu Sa'id is the primary evidence. Note its classification as a musnad hadith (from the Sahaba).
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but sufficient for establishing a sunnah.
3
Determine the wajh al-dalalah
How does the imperative 'لا تدعوه' (do not abandon) indicate recommendation (istihbab) rather than obligation (wujub) in Hanafi usul? Consider the accompanying qarinah of blessings and reward.
In Hanafi usul, a command with a clear context of encouragement indicates nadb, not fard.
4
Expand the meaning of barakah
The commentary lists three types of increase (ziyadah) in barakah. Identify each and understand how they go beyond mere physical benefit.
Barakah linguistically means increase; the hadith itself calls suhur 'all blessing'.
5
Note the thamarah (practical consequence)
What is the practical takeaway for a fasting person? Even a sip of water suffices to gain the blessing, and missing suhur means losing a confirmed sunnah.
The ruling is unanimous across schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عن أبي سعيد مسنداً السحور كله بركة فلا تدعوه ولو أن يجرع أحدكم جرعة من ماء فإن الله وملائكته يصلون على المتسحرين (۱) والبركة في الحديث لغة الزيادة والنماء، والزيادة فيه على وجوه : زيادة في القوة على أداء الصوم وزيادة في إباحة الأكل والشرب، وزيادة على الأوقات التي يستجاب فيها الدعاء كذا ذكره الكلاباذي وبيتها في غاية البيان .

And 'barakah' in the hadith linguistically means increase and growth. The increase is in various ways: increase in strength to perform the fast, increase in permission to eat and drink, and increase in the times when supplication is answered. Thus mentioned by al-Kalabadhi, and its clarification is extremely clear.
Source pages p. 512

Commentary
This passage is a hadith narration from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri (may Allah be pleased with him) regarding the virtue of the pre-dawn meal (suhur). The author quotes it to establish the recommendation of suhur. The hadith explicitly calls suhur a 'blessing' (barakah) and commands not to abandon it even for a sip of water. The author then explains that barakah means increase, detailing three types: physical strength to observe the fast, the permissibility of eating and drinking (i.e., the concession given), and the opportunity for du'a during the pre-dawn time (which is a time of acceptance). This highlights that suhur is not merely a physical preparation but also a spiritual opportunity.

Key Terms
السحور (al-suhūr): The pre-dawn meal consumed before the fast begins.
البركة (al-barakah): Linguistically increase and growth; in this context, divine blessings that bring physical, permissible, and spiritual benefits.

Rulings on Breaking Fast Based on Witness Reports
Rules for fasting with contradictory witness testimony about sunset and fajr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the rulings for contradictory testimony about sunset versus fajr, and apply the Hanafi principle that evidence for affirmation is accepted over negation.

Core Idea
Evidence affirms change, not permanence; breaking fast on uncertain testimony requires qada, but kaffarah only if you violated a certain obligation.

This passage from al-Bazaziyya presents a critical distinction in Hanafi fiqh: the rules for breaking fast based on witness testimony differ depending on whether the testimony concerns sunset (ending the fast) or dawn (beginning the fast). The underlying principle is that evidence (bayyinah) is for affirmation (ithbat) of a change, not for negation (nafi). Understanding this distinction is essential for a mufti or judge dealing with contested testimony during Ramadan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage and note the two distinct cases: testimony about sunset (maghrib) and testimony about fajr (dawn). Each involves contradictory witnesses.
2
State the ruling for each scenario
For sunset: if two witnesses say it set, two say it did not, and the person breaks fast, he owes only qada (make-up), no kaffarah. For fajr: similarly, two say it appeared, two say not, and he eats, he owes qada and kaffarah.
3
Extract the legal principle
The passage states: 'because evidence is for affirmation, not negation, so the testimony of the affirming witness is accepted, not the negating.' This is the key principle.
4
Apply the principle to a third scenario
Consider the case of one witness for fajr and two against. The ruling is no kaffarah, only qada. Why? Because the single affirmation does not create certainty, and the principle only gives weight to affirmation over negation when affirmation is plural? The text implies that with one affirmant, the negators outweigh, so no kaffarah.
The number of witnesses affects certainty; one witness is not sufficient for a binding change.
5
Recognize epistemic weight
The difference between sunset and fajr lies in the nature of the act: breaking fast at sunset is permissible (change from prohibition to permission), while eating after fajr is a violation of a confirmed obligation. Hence, kaffarah is added for the latter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي البزازية : ويستحب تعجيل الإفطار إلا في يوم غيم، ولا يفطر ما لم يغلب على ظنه غروب الشمس وإن أذن المؤذن .اهـ. وذكر قبله : شهدا أنها غربت وآخران بأنها لم تغرب وأفطر ثم بان عدم الغروب قضى ولا كفارة عليه بالاتفاق شهدا على طلوع الفجر وآخران على عدم الطلوع فأكل ثم بأن الطلوع قضى وكفر وفاقاً لأن البينات للإثبات لا للنفي حتى قبل شهادة المثبت لا النافي. ولو واحد على طلوعه وآخران على عدمه لا كفارة عليه.
And in al-Bazaziyya: It is recommended to hasten iftar except on a cloudy day, and one should not break the fast unless he is confident that the sun has set, even if the mu'adhdhin calls. End. And it was mentioned before him: Two testified that it set, and two that it did not, and he broke his fast, then it became clear it had not set: he makes up (qada) and no expiation (kaffarah) upon him by agreement. Two testified on the appearance of dawn, and two on its non-appearance, so he ate, then it became clear it had appeared: he makes up and expiates, by agreement, because evidence is for affirmation, not negation, so the testimony of the affirming witness is accepted, not the negating. And if one testified on its appearance and two on its non-appearance, there is no expiation upon him.
Source pages p. 512

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the validity of breaking the fast based on witness testimony. It presents two scenarios: contradictory testimony about sunset and about fajr. For sunset, the preferred view is to err on the side of caution, so if contradictory testimony is given and he breaks fast, only qada (make-up) is required, no kaffarah. This is because the basic principle is that the day continues until certainty of night. For fajr, however, the principle is that once dawn appears, eating becomes forbidden. If contradictory testimony leads him to eat, both qada and kaffarah are required because he deliberately ate when fasting was obligatory. The key distinction: evidence is for affirmation (of a change in ruling) not for negation (of the original ruling). Hence, the testimony of the affirmant (that it set/dawned) is accepted. If one person testifies to dawn and two deny, the single testimony is not sufficient to create certainty, so no kaffarah.

Key Terms
البينات (al-bayyināt): Evidence, here specifically witness testimony.
المثبت (al-muthbit): The witness who affirms a fact (e.g., sunset or fajr).
النافي (al-nāfī): The witness who negates a fact.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Qada and kaffarah
C) No qada or kaffarah
D) Kaffarah only

Key Takeaway
The ruling for breaking fast based on witness testimony hinges on whether the testimony affirms a change (sunset/fajr) or denies it, with evidence for affirmation being accepted over negation.

The Effect of Verbally Abandoning the Intention to Fast
Ruling when a fasting person is informed it is fajr while eating suhur and continues eating

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a fasting person is informed of dawn during suhur and verbally abandons the intention, and distinguish between cases with multiple informants versus a single informant.

Core Idea
Verbally abandoning the intention nullifies the fast; subsequent eating incurs no kaffarah if the abandonment was based on reliable news.

This lesson examines a nuanced scenario in the fast of Ramadan: a person eating suhur is told by multiple people that dawn has appeared. He believes them and says, 'I am not fasting,' then continues eating. Later it turns out dawn had not yet broken. What is the ruling? The Hanafi master al-Nasafi al-Hakim provides a decisive answer that hinges on the legal weight of the information received and the explicit abandonment of intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and key actions
Read the passage carefully. The person is eating suhur and is informed (by multiple people) that dawn has appeared. He believes them and verbally declares 'I am not fasting' (أنا مفطر لا صائم). He then continues eating. Later it becomes clear that dawn had not appeared at the moment of the first eating but appeared later during the second eating.
The verbal declaration is the crucial action.
2
Analyze the effect of the verbal declaration
The statement 'I am not fasting' constitutes an explicit abandonment of the intention (niyyah) to fast. In Hanafi fiqh, intention is a condition (shart) for the validity of the fast. Once the intention is abandoned, the fast becomes void from that moment onward. Therefore, any eating after this declaration is not a break of a valid fast.
This is why no kaffarah is owed – the fast was already invalid.
3
Distinguish based on the number of informants
Al-Nasafi differentiates between multiple informants and a single informant. When multiple people report the dawn (considered reliable news), the declaration is based on valid information, so the intention abandonment is effective. However, if only one person reports, that report (khabar al-wāḥid) is not accepted in such matters initially; thus, the original intention is considered to persist, and the eating becomes a deliberate break of a valid fast, requiring kaffarah.
The legal principle: in matters requiring certainty (like the appearance of dawn), a single upright person's testimony is insufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دخلوا عليه وهو يتسحر فقالوا إنه طالع فصدقهم فقال إذن أنا مفطر لا صائم ثم دام على الأكل ثم بأنه ما كان طالعاً في أول الأكل وطالعاً وقت الأكل الثاني قال النسفي الحاكم : لا كفارة عليه لعدم نية الصوم، وإن كان المخبر واحداً عليه الكفارة لأن خبر الواحد عدلاً أولا في مثل هذا لا يقبل اهـ.
They entered upon him while he was eating suhur and said, 'It (fajr) has appeared.' He believed them and said, 'Then I am a breakfast-eater, not fasting.' Then he continued eating. Then it became clear that it had not appeared during the first eating but had appeared at the time of the second eating. Al-Nasafi al-Hakim said: No kaffarah upon him due to lack of intention for fasting. But if the informant was one person, kaffarah is upon him because the report of one person, even if upright, is not accepted in such matters initially.
Source pages p. 512

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement by al-Nasafi al-Hakim, an authority in the Hanafi school. The scenario: a person is eating suhur and is told by multiple people that dawn has broken. He believes them and verbally declares 'I am not fasting', then continues eating. Later it turns out that dawn had not broken at the start but did so during his meal. The ruling: no kaffarah, because by saying 'I am not fasting', he openly abandoned the intention of fasting. Since intention is a condition for a valid fast, his subsequent eating is not breaking a valid fast. However, if only one person told him, that testimony is not legally weighty (since the evidence of one is not accepted to establish such a fact), so the original intention remains, and eating breaks the fast, requiring kaffarah. The key point is that the declaration of abandoning intention negates the fast, so no compensation is due.

Key Terms
نية الصوم (niyyat al-sawm): Intention to fast; a prerequisite for a valid fast.
خبر الواحد (khabar al-wāḥid): Report of a single person; in legal contexts, it is accepted in some matters but not in matters requiring two witnesses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi al-Hakim, what is the ruling if multiple people tell a fasting person that fajr has appeared, he says 'I am not fasting', continues eating, and later it is found that fajr had not appeared until later?
A) He must make qada and pay kaffarah
B) He must make qada only
C) No qada or kaffarah
D) He must pay kaffarah only

Key Takeaway
Verbally abandoning the intention to fast, based on reliable information, nullifies the fast, so subsequent eating incurs no kaffarah; but with a single informant, the intention remains, and kaffarah is due.

Shubha al-Ishtibah and Shubha al-Ikhtilaf
Introduction to shubha types and examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between shubha al-ishtibah bi al-nazir and shubha ikhtilaf al-'ulama', and apply each to scenarios of breaking the fast.

Core Idea
Doubt from similarity or scholarly disagreement excuses kaffarah for breaking the fast.

This lesson explores the two main sources of doubt that can waive the obligation of kaffarah when one breaks a Ramadan fast. The Hanafi school recognizes that certainty is required for penal obligations; when doubt arises from similarity (ishtibah) or genuine scholarly disagreement (ikhtilaf), the penalty is dropped. We will examine the underlying reasoning and key examples from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of shubha
From the passage, note the phrases 'shubha al-ashbah bi al-nazir' and 'shubha ikhtilaf al-'ulama'. Understand the difference: the first arises from confusion between two similar acts, the second from the existence of a valid differing scholarly opinion.
The text begins with eating forgetfully as an example of similarity doubt, and then mentions Imam Malik's view as example of ikhtilaf doubt.
2
Analyze the example of eating forgetfully
The passage states that intentional and forgetful eating are similar (both oppose the fast), creating doubt. Even if the person knows that forgetful eating does not break the fast (because of a hadith or fatwa), the existence of Malik's opposing view creates a second doubt. The result is no kaffarah.
The author says 'wa huwa al-sahih' – this is the correct Hanafi position.
3
Apply to the vomiting scenario
Involuntary vomiting (qay') is similar to induced vomiting (istiqa') because both exit from the mouth. If one thinks vomiting breaks the fast and then eats, there is similarity doubt, so no kaffarah. Contrast with knowing the ruling – then kaffarah applies.
The text explicitly says 'la kaffarah 'alayhi' due to shubha.
4
Distinguish shubha from mere ignorance
The passage later distinguishes cases where the person is merely ignorant (jahl) of the ruling, such as thinking cupping or backbiting breaks the fast without having consulted a scholar or received authentic report. In that case, kaffarah is required because there is no shubha.
This highlights that shubha requires a plausible basis, not just any mistaken belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما لم تجب الكفارة بإفطاره عمداً . بعد أكله أو شربه أو جماعه ناسياً لأنه ظن في موضع الاشتباه بالنظير وهو الأكل عمداً لأن الأكل مضاد للصوم ساهياً أو عامداً فأورث شبهة. وكذا فيه شبهة اختلاف العلماء فإن مالكاً يقول بفساد صوم من أكل ناسياً. وأطلقه فشمل ما إذا اعلم بأنه لا يفطره بأن بلغه الحديث أو الفتوى أولاً وهو قول أبي حنيفة وهو الصحيح لأن العلماء اختلفوا في قبول الحديث فإن فقهاء المدينة كمالك وغيره لم يقبلوه فصار شبهة لأن قول الشافعي إذا كان موافقاً للقياس يكون شبهة كقول الصحابي، وكذا لو ذرعه القيء فظن أنه يفطره فأفطر لا كفارة عليه لوجود شبهة الاشتباه بالنظير فإن القيء والاستقاء متشابهان لأن مخرجهما من الفم. وكذا لو احتلم للتشابه في قضاء الشهوة، وإن علم أن ذلك لا يفطره فعليه الكفارة لأنه لم توجد شبهة الاشتباه ولا شبهة الاختلاف وقيد بالنسيان لأنه لو احتجم أو اغتاب فظن أنه يفطره ثم أكل إن لم يستفت فقيها ولا بلغه الخبر فعليه الكفارة لأنه مجرد جهل وأنه ليس بعذر في دار الإسلام، وإن استفتى فقيهاً لا كفارة عليه لأن العامي يجب عليه تقليد العالم إذا كان يعتمد على فتواه فكان معذوراً فيما صنع، وإن كان المفتي مخطئاً فيما أفتى وإن لم يستفت ولكن بلغه الخبر وهو قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام أفطر الحاجم والمحجوم» (۱) وقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم الغيبة تفطر الصائم ولم يعرف النسخ ولا تأويله فلا كفارة عليه عندهما لأن ظاهر الحديث واجب العمل به خلافاً لأبي يوسف لأنه ليس للعامي العمل بالحديث لعدم علمه بالناسخ والمنسوخ. ولو لمس امرأة وقبلها بشهوة أو اكتحل فظن أن ذلك يفطره ثم أفطر فعليه الكفارة إلا إذا استفتى فقيهاً فأفتاه بالفطر أو بلغه خبر فيه .
And indeed, kaffarah is not obligatory for his intentional breaking of the fast after his eating, drinking, or intercourse forgetfully, because he has doubt in a place of confusion due to similarity, and his intentional eating is like his unintentional eating because eating is opposed to fasting whether forgetful or deliberate, so it causes a doubt. Likewise, there is a doubt due to the difference of scholars: Malik holds that the fast of one who eats forgetfully is nullified. And it is general so it includes when he knows that it does not break the fast through a hadith or fatwa, and it is the saying of Abu Hanifa and it is correct because the scholars differed on accepting the hadith; the fuqaha of Madinah like Malik etc. did not accept it, so it becomes a doubt. Because the saying of al-Shafi'i, when it agrees with qiyas, becomes a doubt like the saying of a Companion. Similarly, if he is overcome by vomiting and thinks it breaks the fast, then he breaks his fast – no kaffarah upon him due to the existence of similarity-based doubt, because vomiting and inducing vomiting are similar since their exit is from the mouth. Similarly, if he had a wet dream, due to similarity in satisfying desire. But if he knows that that does not break the fast, then kaffarah is upon him because there is no similarity-based doubt nor difference-based doubt. And it is conditioned by forgetfulness because if he did cupping or backbiting and thought it breaks the fast, then ate – if he did not consult a faqih nor had the report (knowledge of the ruling), then kaffarah is upon him because it is mere ignorance and it is no excuse in the abode of Islam. If he consulted a faqih, no kaffarah upon him because the layman must imitate the scholar if he relies on his fatwa, so he is excused in what he did, even if the mufti was mistaken in his fatwa. And if he did not consult but had the report – that is, his (peace be upon him) saying 'The cupper and the cupped break the fast' and his (peace be upon him) saying 'Backbiting breaks the fast of the fasting person' – and he did not know the abrogation nor its interpretation, then no kaffarah upon him according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) because the apparent meaning of the hadith must be acted upon, contrary to Abu Yusuf because the layman is not permitted to act upon the hadith due to his lack of knowledge of the abrogating and abrogated. And if he touched a woman and kissed her with desire, or applied kohl, thinking that it breaks the fast, then broke his fast, kaffarah is upon him unless he consulted a faqih who gave him a fatwa that it breaks the fast, or he had a report about it.
Source pages p. 512

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive explanation of the principle of shubha (doubt) as a factor that waives kaffarah for breaking a fast. The author, likely from al-Muhit or similar, provides a detailed list of scenarios and the underlying reasoning. The key types of shubha are: (1) Shubha al-ishtibah bi al-nazir (doubt due to similarity) – when an act that breaks the fast resembles another act that does not, causing confusion. Examples: eating forgetfully (since eating forgetfully also breaks the fast in Malik's view), vomiting (since unintentional vomiting resembles intentional), wet dream (similarity in satisfying desire). (2) Shubha ikhtilaf al-'ulama' (doubt due to scholarly disagreement) – when respected scholars hold a different ruling, it creates doubt. Example: the hadith about cupping and backbiting: some scholars accepted it as breaking the fast, some did not. The layman who relies on such a hadith without knowledge of abrogation is excused according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) but not according to Abu Yusuf himself (a difference within the school). (3) Shubha al-ijtihad – when a layman consults a mufti and follows his fatwa, he is excused even if the mufti is wrong, because he is obliged to imitate. However, for acts that are clearly not breaking the fast (like touching or kissing without discharge, or applying kohl), there is no shubha, so kaffarah remains unless he had a fatwa or report. The passage ends by noting that backbiting is ambiguous: some mashayikh differ from al-Muhit on whether consulting a faqih creates shubha.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubhah): Doubt or uncertainty that arises from similarity, scholarly disagreement, or a plausible but incorrect belief.
الاشتباه بالنظير (al-ishtibāh bi al-naẓīr): Confusion due to similarity between two acts, one of which breaks the fast and the other does not.
اختلاف العلماء (ikhtilāf al-ʿulamāʾ): Disagreement among recognized scholars regarding a ruling, which can create a valid doubt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a person vomits involuntarily and then deliberately eats, thinking the vomiting already broke the fast?
A) Kaffarah is required
B) No kaffarah required
C) Only qada required
D) Both qada and kaffarah

Key Takeaway
Doubt arising from similarity or scholarly disagreement waives the obligation of kaffarah for breaking the fast.

The Layman and Shubha: Hadith, Fatwa, and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Layman's reliance on hadith and fatwa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the conditions under which a layman is excused from kaffarah when acting on a hadith or a mufti's fatwa, and identify the disagreement between Abu Yusuf and the two imams.

Core Idea
A layman is excused from kaffarah if he relies on a hadith or a mufti, even if mistaken, but not if he acts on mere ignorance.

This lesson focuses on the second part of the passage, which deals with scenarios where the person does not have a direct shubha from similarity or ikhtilaf but relies on external authority: a hadith report or a mufti's fatwa. The Hanafi school grants a concession here, but there is an intra-Hanafi disagreement about whether a layman may act directly on a hadith without knowledge of abrogation. We will analyze the conditions and the preferred view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios of external reliance
The passage discusses: (a) the layman who consults a faqih and follows his fatwa, (b) the layman who has a report (hadith) about an act breaking the fast but does not know abrogation or interpretation.
These are in contrast to mere ignorance (jahl) which does not excuse.
2
Examine the ruling for consulting a faqih
If the layman asks a mufti and follows his fatwa, no kaffarah is required, even if the mufti was wrong. This is because the layman is obligated to imitate (taqlid) a scholar.
The text says 'wa in kana al-mufti mukhti'an' – even if mistaken.
3
Examine the ruling for acting on a hadith
If the layman hears a hadith (e.g., 'cupping breaks the fast') and does not know abrogation or interpretation, then according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) no kaffarah, because the apparent meaning must be acted upon. Abu Yusuf disagrees because the layman lacks ability to determine abrogation.
The author sides with the two, indicating it is the preferred view.
4
Distinguish from cases without external support
If the layman simply assumes an act breaks the fast without any basis (no hadith, no fatwa, no similarity), then kaffarah is required because it is mere ignorance, which is not excused in Dar al-Islam.
The examples given are cupping and backbiting – if he thinks they break the fast without evidence, kaffarah applies.

Key Terms
تقليد (taqlīd): Following the opinion of a qualified scholar without demanding proof.
ناسخ ومنسوخ (nāsikh wa mansūkh): Abrogating and abrogated; refers to the process of one revelation superseding another.

Cases Without Shubha: Knowledge and Ignorance
Boundary cases with no shubha

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify scenarios where kaffarah is required due to absence of shubha, and distinguish between knowledge, shubha, and mere ignorance.

Core Idea
Certain knowledge or mere ignorance (jahl) does not waive kaffarah; only plausible doubt does.

This lesson consolidates the boundary cases from the passage: when a person knows for certain that an act does not break the fast, or when he acts out of pure ignorance without any basis. The text provides clear examples: touching/kissing with desire, applying kohl, cupping/backbiting without consultation. Understanding these negative cases is crucial to grasp the precise scope of shubha.

Guided Path
1
Identify the 'knowledge' cases
The passage earlier said that if a person knows that vomiting or wet dream does not break the fast, then kaffarah is required. Similarly, the end mentions touching/kissing and kohl: if the person knows these do not break the fast but thinks they do, there is no shubha.
Knowledge eliminates doubt; thus kaffarah stands.
2
Identify the 'mere ignorance' cases
The passage gives the example of cupping/backbiting: if the person thinks they break the fast without having consulted a faqih or received a report, it is mere ignorance (jahl), which is no excuse in Dar al-Islam.
The condition 'لم يستفت فقيها ولا بلغه الخبر' is key.
3
Understand why these are not shubha
Shubha requires a plausible basis: either similarity, scholarly disagreement, or reliance on a credible authority. Without these, the mistaken belief is just jahl, which is not a valid legal excuse.
The text states explicitly that jahl is not an excuse.
4
Apply to modern scenarios
Imagine a person who thinks that using a nicotine patch breaks the fast because it enters the body. If there is no scholarly discussion or fatwa on this, it is mere ignorance. But if he consulted a scholar who gave a fatwa (even wrong), it becomes shubha.
The critical factor is the presence of an external basis.

And indeed, kaffarah is not obligatory for his intentional breaking of the fast after his eating, drinking, or intercourse forgetfully, because he has doubt in a place of confusion due to similarity, and his intentional eating is like his unintentional eating because eating is opposed to fasting whether forgetful or deliberate, so it causes a doubt. Likewise, there is a doubt due to the difference of scholars: Malik holds that the fast of one who eats forgetfully is nullified. And it is general so it includes when he knows that it does not break the fast through a hadith or fatwa, and it is the saying of Abu Hanifa and it is correct because the scholars differed on accepting the hadith; the fuqaha of Madinah like Malik etc. did not accept it, so it becomes a doubt. Because the saying of al-Shafi'i, when it agrees with qiyas, becomes a doubt like the saying of a Companion. Similarly, if he is overcome by vomiting and thinks it breaks the fast, then he breaks his fast – no kaffarah upon him due to the existence of similarity-based doubt, because vomiting and inducing vomiting are similar since their exit is from the mouth. Similarly, if he had a wet dream, due to similarity in satisfying desire. But if he knows that that does not break the fast, then kaffarah is upon him because there is no similarity-based doubt nor difference-based doubt. And it is conditioned by forgetfulness because if he did cupping or backbiting and thought it breaks the fast, then ate – if he did not consult a faqih nor had the report (knowledge of the ruling), then kaffarah is upon him because it is mere ignorance and it is no excuse in the abode of Islam. If he consulted a faqih, no kaffarah upon him because the layman must imitate the scholar if he relies on his fatwa, so he is excused in what he did, even if the mufti was mistaken in his fatwa. And if he did not consult but had the report – that is, his (peace be upon him) saying 'The cupper and the cupped break the fast' and his (peace be upon him) saying 'Backbiting breaks the fast of the fasting person' – and he did not know the abrogation nor its interpretation, then no kaffarah upon him according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) because the apparent meaning of the hadith must be acted upon, contrary to Abu Yusuf because the layman is not permitted to act upon the hadith due to his lack of knowledge of the abrogating and abrogated. And if he touched a woman and kissed her with desire, or applied kohl, thinking that it breaks the fast, then broke his fast, kaffarah is upon him unless he consulted a faqih who gave him a fatwa that it breaks the fast, or he had a report about it.
Source pages p. 512

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive explanation of the principle of shubha (doubt) as a factor that waives kaffarah for breaking a fast. The author, likely from al-Muhit or similar, provides a detailed list of scenarios and the underlying reasoning. The key types of shubha are: (1) Shubha al-ishtibah bi al-nazir (doubt due to similarity) – when an act that breaks the fast resembles another act that does not, causing confusion. Examples: eating forgetfully (since eating forgetfully also breaks the fast in Malik's view), vomiting (since unintentional vomiting resembles intentional), wet dream (similarity in satisfying desire). (2) Shubha ikhtilaf al-'ulama' (doubt due to scholarly disagreement) – when respected scholars hold a different ruling, it creates doubt. Example: the hadith about cupping and backbiting: some scholars accepted it as breaking the fast, some did not. The layman who relies on such a hadith without knowledge of abrogation is excused according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) but not according to Abu Yusuf himself (a difference within the school). (3) Shubha al-ijtihad – when a layman consults a mufti and follows his fatwa, he is excused even if the mufti is wrong, because he is obliged to imitate. However, for acts that are clearly not breaking the fast (like touching or kissing without discharge, or applying kohl), there is no shubha, so kaffarah remains unless he had a fatwa or report. The passage ends by noting that backbiting is ambiguous: some mashayikh differ from al-Muhit on whether consulting a faqih creates shubha.

Key Terms
جهل (jahl): Ignorance or lack of knowledge, especially when there is no plausible basis for a mistaken belief.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling for a person who applies kohl thinking it breaks the fast, without having consulted a scholar or heard a report?
A) No kaffarah because he had a sincere belief
B) Kaffarah required because it is mere ignorance
C) No kaffarah because kohl does not break the fast
D) Kaffarah required only if kohl was applied with desire

Key Takeaway
Without a plausible basis (similarity, ikhtilaf, fatwa, or report), a mistaken belief is mere ignorance and does not waive kaffarah.

Breaking Fast After Intention Before Zawal: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on kaffarah for breaking a fast intended before noon

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on kaffarah when a fast is intended before zawal and then broken, and identify the difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Intention before noon makes a fast valid, but breaking it before noon does not incur kaffarah according to Abu Hanifa — only qada is required.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq presents a disagreement within the Hanafi school itself. The ruling on kaffarah for breaking a fast after intending it before zawal differs between the Imam and his two disciples. Understanding this nuance is crucial for correctly applying the school's fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and legal context
The act: a person intends a fast (nafl or qada) before zawal (noon), then breaks it on the same day. The intention is valid because in the Hanafi school, intention for such fasts can be made up to zawal.
The fast starts at dawn, not at intention.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling
Abu Hanifa says: no kaffarah (expiation) is required. Only qada (making up the fast) is necessary because the fast was not fully established in his view.
Qada is agreed upon by all.
3
State the Sahibayn's ruling
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani say: kaffarah is wajib because the intention was valid and breaking a valid fast deliberately requires expiation.
The Sahibayn view the breaking as a full violation.
4
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference is clear: one view requires only qada; the other requires both qada and kaffarah.
This affects the legal obligation of the person.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
Later authorities (like al-Muhit, cited here) prefer Abu Hanifa's view. The mufta bihi is that no kaffarah is required.
The text presents Abu Hanifa's view first, implying reliance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو نوى الصوم قبل الزوال ثم أفطر لم تلزمه الكفارة عند أبي حنيفة خلافاً لهما كذا في المحيط .
And if one intended the fast before zawal (noon), then broke it, kaffarah is not obligatory according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Thus in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 513

Commentary
This passage is a statement of an intra-Hanafi dispute. The issue: a person who intends to fast (for a nafl or missed fast) before noon (zawal) – which is the time until which intention is valid in the Hanafi school – and then breaks the fast. According to Abu Hanifa, no kaffarah is required because the fast was not yet fully obligated; the intention was made but the fast had not been established with continuity until after noon? Alternatively, the view is that since he made a valid intention, breaking it is a sin requiring qada, but kaffarah is not due because the obligation was not complete. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) opine that since the intention was valid, breaking it deliberately incurs kaffarah like any other intentional break. The text notes that this ruling is from al-Muhit, a later Hanafi reference. The operative position (mufta bihi) in the school is presumably that of Abu Hanifa, as indicated by the phrase 'عند أبي حنيفة' presented first.

Key Terms
الزوال (al-zawāl): The time when the sun passes the meridian (noon).
الكفارة (al-kaffārah): Expiation for deliberately breaking a fast; traditionally fasting for two consecutive months or feeding 60 poor people.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is Abu Hanifa's ruling on kaffarah when a fast is intended before zawal and then broken?
A) Kaffarah is wajib
B) Kaffarah is not required
C) Qada is not required
D) Both kaffarah and qada are required

Key Takeaway
Breaking a fast after intending it before zawal does not incur kaffarah according to Abu Hanifa, only qada, and this is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.

The Layman's Madhhab: Following the Mufti's Fatwa
Principle that layman follows mufti's fatwa, with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle that a layperson's madhhab is the fatwa of his mufti, distinguish between unambiguous and ambiguous acts regarding kaffarah, and identify the condition that the mufti must be a recognized authority in the locality.

Core Idea
A layman's madhhab is his mufti's fatwa — but only a qualified mufti's opinion creates shubha that can excuse from kaffarah.

In the Hanafi school, a layperson (al-ʿāmmī) is not required to adhere to a specific madhhab; rather, his religious duty is to seek and follow the fatwa of a qualified mufti. This fatwa becomes his personal madhhab and, if it leads to ambiguity, can prevent the obligation of kaffarah. However, this principle has limits: for acts that are clearly not breaking the fast, even a fatwa does not create a valid doubt. This lesson unpacks the conditions and exceptions from key Hanafi texts.

Guided Path
1
Extract the Core Principle
Identify the statement that 'the madhhab of the layman is the fatwa of his mufti' and understand that it means the layman's obligation is to follow his mufti's ruling, not any particular school.
This is based on Fath al-Qadir and al-Bada'i.
2
Distinguish Unambiguous vs. Ambiguous Acts
Analyze the example of oiling the mustache: if the layman thought it breaks the fast, but the act is clearly not a nullifier, even after consulting a faqih, kaffarah is wajib. This shows that a fatwa only creates shubha if the act is ambiguous.
The same applies to backbiting according to the majority; the author notes al-Muhit disagrees.
3
Note the Condition on the Mufti
From al-Nihaya: the mufti must be one from whom fiqh is taken and whose fatwa is relied upon in the town. Only such a mufti's fatwa creates a shubha; a non-reliable scholar does not.
This prevents arbitrary fatwa-shopping.
4
Recognize the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
There is a disagreement on whether backbiting is ambiguous. The majority in al-Tabyin hold it is unambiguous (no shubha), while al-Muhit considers it ambiguous. The author prefers al-Muhit's view due to shubha.
This shows that even within the school, later authorities may differ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد علم من هذا أن مذهب العامي فتوى مفتيه من غير تقييد بمذهب، ولهذا قال في فتح القدير : الحكم في حق العامي فتوى مفتيه . وفي البدائع : ولو دهن شاربه فظن أنه أفطر فأكل عمداً فعليه الكفارة وإن استفتى فقيهاً أو تأول حديثاً لأن هذا مما لا يشتبه وكذا لو اغتاب .اهـ. وفي التبيين أن عليه عامة المشايخ وهو في الغيبة مخالف لما في المحيط، والظاهر ترجيح ما في المحيط للشبهة . وفي النهاية : ويشترط أن يكون المفتي ممن يؤخذ منه الفقه ويعتمد على فتواه في البلدة وحينئذ تصير فتواه شبهة ولا معتبر بغيره .
And it has been known from this that the madhhab of the layman is the fatwa of his mufti, without restriction to a particular school. Hence it is said in Fath al-Qadir: 'The ruling in regard to the layman is the fatwa of his mufti.' And in al-Bada'i: 'If he oiled his mustache and thought it breaks the fast, then ate deliberately, kaffarah is upon him even if he consulted a faqih or interpreted a hadith, because this is not ambiguous. Similarly, if he backbit.' End. And in al-Tabyin: 'The majority of the mashayikh hold that, and it is contrary to what is in al-Muhit regarding backbiting. And the apparent preference is for al-Muhit due to shubha.' And in al-Nihaya: 'It is a condition that the mufti be one from whom fiqh is taken and whose fatwa is relied upon in the town; then his fatwa becomes a shubha, and others are not considered.'
Source pages p. 513

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement that consolidates the earlier discussion on the layman's position. It establishes a key principle: a layperson (al-ʿāmmī) is not bound to follow any specific madhhab; rather, his religious obligation is to follow the fatwa of a qualified mufti whom he trusts. This means that if a mufti gives him a ruling, that ruling becomes his personal madhhab and creates a shubha (doubt) that can excuse him from kaffarah if he acted on it. However, if the act in question is clearly not something that breaks the fast (like oiling the mustache – which does not enter the stomach, or backbiting – which is a sin but does not break the fast), then consulting a mufti does not create a valid shubha because the ruling is unambiguous. There is a difference of opinion on backbiting: al-Muhit considered it ambiguous (creating shubha), while the majority in al-Tabyin did not. The author prefers al-Muhit's view. Finally, al-Nihaya adds a condition: the mufti must be a recognized authority in the locality for his fatwa to create shubha.

Key Terms
العامي (al-ʿāmmī): A layperson who is not a qualified mujtahid or faqih.
المفتي (al-muftī): A qualified jurist who issues fatwas.
شبهة (shubhah): Doubt that arises from following a mufti's fatwa, similar to earlier use.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the principle established in the text, what is the ruling for a layman who oils his mustache thinking it breaks the fast, then eats deliberately, even after consulting a faqih?
A) No kaffarah because of the fatwa
B) Kaffarah is required because the act is unambiguous
C) Only qada required
D) It depends on the mufti's view

Key Takeaway
A layperson's obligation is to follow the fatwa of a qualified mufti, but the fatwa only creates shubha for ambiguous acts; for unambiguous acts, kaffarah remains wajib.

No Kaffarah for Involuntary Fast Breaker
Ruling on kaffarah for a sleeping or insane woman who eats after being raped

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a woman who is raped while asleep or insane and then eats, and explain why no kaffarah is required.

Core Idea
A fast already broken by coercion cannot be further violated—no kaffarah for subsequent eating.

This lesson addresses two scenarios where a woman's fast is nullified through no fault of her own: a sleeping woman who is raped, and an insane woman who is raped. The key question is whether her subsequent eating (after waking or regaining sanity) triggers kaffarah. The Hanafi school clearly states that because the fast was already invalidated without her sin, the eating does not constitute a new transgression.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is a sleeping or insane woman eating after being raped. The ruling: no kaffarah is due.
2
Determine the cause of nullification
The fast was nullified by the intercourse, not by the eating. The woman had no control over the intercourse.
3
Analyze the presence of sin (jinayah)
Kaffarah is tied to willful transgression. Here there is no jinayah because the nullification happened without her choice.
4
Understand the effect of subsequent eating
Since the fast was already fasid (null), the eating does not constitute nullification or additional sin.
5
Distinguish the insane woman's case regarding qada
The insane woman had valid intention at the start. Insanity does not invalidate the fast itself, but the fast was already broken by rape. She does not owe qada because the fast was not validly broken by her.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النائمة أو المجنونة إذا أكلتا بعدما جومعتا فلا كفارة عليهما لأن الفساد حصل بالجماع قبل الأكل كالمخطئ ولا كفارة لعدم الجناية فالأكل بعده ليس بإفساد وصورتها في النائمة ظاهر . وفي المجنونة بأن نوت الصوم ثم جنت بالنهار وهي صائمة فجامعها إنسان فإن الجنون لا ينافي الصوم إنما ينافي شرطه أعني النية وقد وجد في حال الإفاقة فلا يجب قضاء ذلك اليوم إذا صومها
As for the sleeping or insane woman, if they eat after being sexually assaulted, there is no kaffarah upon them because the nullification (of the fast) occurred by the intercourse before the eating, like the mistaken one, and there is no expiation due to the absence of transgression. So the eating after it is not a nullification. The scenario for the sleeping woman is apparent. And regarding the insane woman: She intended the fast, then became insane during the day while fasting, and a person had intercourse with her. Insanity does not contradict fasting; it only contradicts its condition, namely intention, which was present at the time of lucidity. So qada for that day is not obligatory if her fast is... (the text cuts off)
Source pages p. 513

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement presenting two similar cases: a woman who is asleep and is raped, then upon waking eats; and a woman who has a valid intention for fasting but becomes insane during the day and is raped, then upon regaining sanity eats. In both cases, the fast was already invalidated (fasid) by the intercourse (whether she was asleep or insane, she is not liable for the intercourse). Since the fast was already broken without her willful sin, her subsequent eating does not add any further transgression. Therefore, no kaffarah is required. For the insane woman, an additional point: insanity does not invalidate the fast itself—it only affects the condition of intention, which she already had when lucid. Thus, even though her fast was nullified by intercourse, she does not have to make up that day because the fast was already invalid due to the rape, and she is not considered a deliberate breaker. The text ends abruptly with 'إذا صومها' possibly incomplete. The author then moves to a critical refinement of the word 'majnuna' in the original text.

Key Terms
الفساد (al-fasād): Nullification of the fast; the state of being invalid.
الجناية (al-jināyah): Transgression, sin. Here it refers to willful violation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling for a sleeping woman who is raped during her fast and then eats upon waking?
A) She must make qada and pay kaffarah
B) She must make qada only
C) No qada or kaffarah
D) She must pay kaffarah only

Key Takeaway
A fast invalidated through no fault of the fasting person does not incur kaffarah for subsequent eating.

The Layperson and Reliance on Fatwa: Shubha from the Mufti
Intra-Hanafi dispute on shubha from fatwa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a layperson cannot directly act on a hadith according to Abu Hanifa and state the conditions under which a mufti's fatwa creates a valid shubha that prevents kaffarah.

Core Idea
A layperson's madhhab is his mufti's fatwa; only a qualified mufti's opinion creates a valid shubha.

This lesson examines the foundational principle that a layperson (ʿāmmī) is not obligated to know abrogating and abrogated verses (nāsikh wa mansūkh) and therefore cannot act directly on a general hadith. Instead, his religious duty is to follow the fatwa of a qualified mufti. This principle has direct consequences for kaffarah: if the layperson consults a mufti and acts on his fatwa, any doubt (shubha) generated by that fatwa may exempt him from expiation. We will explore the dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on this point, and the conditions required for a mufti's fatwa to be authoritative.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Key Dispute
The passage opens by stating that it is 'obligatory to act upon it' – meaning a general hadith – contrary to Abu Yusuf. Understand why Abu Hanifa opposes this: the layperson lacks knowledge of abrogation, so direct action on a hadith is unreliable.
This sets the stage for the entire ruling on shubha.
2
Derive the Rule for Layperson's Madhhab
The text explicitly states: 'the madhhab of the layperson is the fatwa of his mufti' and cites Fath al-Qadir. Apply this rule: when a layperson receives a fatwa, his legal obligation is to follow it, and any error due to the fatwa creates a shubha that can drop kaffarah.
This is the core principle for shubha from a fatwa.
3
Apply to Concrete Cases
Consider the examples given: touching/kissing with desire or using kohl, then intentionally breaking the fast. According to the text, kaffarah is due unless the person consulted a mufti who gave a fatwa that such acts break the fast. Contrast with the case of acting on a general hadith without consulting a mufti – then kaffarah is due.
The distinction: fatwa creates shubha; acting on one's own interpretation does not.
4
Note the Conditions for the Mufti
The passage from al-Nihaya specifies that the mufti must be someone from whom fiqh is taken and whose fatwa is relied upon in the town. Only then does his fatwa constitute a valid shubha. Otherwise, the fatwa of an unqualified person does not affect the ruling.
This limits the scope of the exception.
5
Contrast with Obvious Acts
The text mentions that if the act is clearly not a fast-breaker (e.g., oiling the mustache), even a mufti's fatwa does not create shubha, and kaffarah is still due. This highlights that shubha is only recognized when the act itself is ambiguous.
This nuance will be explored further in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصوم فصل في العوارض واجب العمل به خلافاً لأبي يوسف لأنه ليس للعامي العمل بالحديث لعدم علمه بالناسخ والمنسوخ. ولو لمس امرأة وقبلها بشهوة أو اكتحل فظن أن ذلك يفطره ثم أفطر فعليه الكفارة إلا إذا استفتى فقيهاً فأفتاه بالفطر أو بلغه خبر فيه . ولو نوى الصوم قبل الزوال ثم أفطر لم تلزمه الكفارة عند أبي حنيفة خلافاً لهما كذا في المحيط . وقد علم من هذا أن مذهب العامي فتوى مفتيه من غير تقييد بمذهب، ولهذا قال في فتح القدير : الحكم في حق العامي فتوى مفتيه . وفي البدائع : ولو دهن شاربه فظن أنه أفطر فأكل عمداً فعليه الكفارة وإن استفتى فقيهاً أو تأول حديثاً لأن هذا مما لا يشتبه وكذا لو اغتاب .اهـ. وفي التبيين أن عليه عامة المشايخ وهو في الغيبة مخالف لما في المحيط، والظاهر ترجيح ما في المحيط للشبهة . وفي النهاية : ويشترط أن يكون المفتي ممن يؤخذ منه الفقه ويعتمد على فتواه في البلدة وحينئذ تصير فتواه شبهة ولا معتبر بغيره . وأما النائمة أو المجنونة إذا أكلتا بعدما جومعتا فلا كفارة عليهما لأن الفساد حصل بالجماع قبل الأكل كالمخطئ ولا كفارة لعدم الجناية فالأكل بعده ليس بإفساد وصورتها في النائمة ظاهر . وفي المجنونة بأن نوت الصوم ثم جنت بالنهار وهي صائمة فجامعها إنسان فإن الجنون لا ينافي الصوم إنما ينافي شرطه أعني النية وقد وجد في حال الإفاقة فلا يجب قضاء ذلك اليوم إذا أفاقت، فإذا جومعت قضته لطرق المفسد على صوم صحيح، وبهذا اندفع ما قيل إنها كانت في الأصل المجبورة أي المكرهة فصحفها الكاتب إلى المجنونة لإمكان توجيهها كما ذكرناه والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم.
Book of Fasting - Section on Impediments: It is obligatory to act upon it (the hadith) contrary to Abu Yusuf because the layperson cannot act upon hadith due to not knowing the abrogating and abrogated. If a man touched a woman and kissed her with desire, or used kohl, and thought that breaks the fast, then broke his fast, he owes expiation unless he consulted a jurist who gave him a fatwa of breaking the fast, or a report reached him about it. If he intended fasting before noon then broke his fast, expiation is not required according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two companions, as in al-Muhit. It is known from this that the madhhab of the layperson is the fatwa of his mufti without restriction to a madhhab. For this reason it is said in Fath al-Qadir: The ruling for the layperson is the fatwa of his mufti. In al-Bada'i: If he oiled his mustache and thought he broke his fast, then ate intentionally, he owes expiation even if he consulted a jurist or interpreted a hadith, because this is not ambiguous; likewise if he backbited. In al-Tabyin: the majority of scholars hold that he owes expiation, and this regarding backbiting contradicts what is in al-Muhit; the apparent preference is for what is in al-Muhit due to doubt. In al-Nihaya: It is required that the mufti be someone from whom fiqh is taken and whose fatwa is relied upon in the town; then his fatwa becomes a doubt, and others are not considered. As for the sleeping or insane woman, if they ate after being forced to have intercourse, there is no expiation on them because the corruption occurred by intercourse before eating, like the mistaken person, and no expiation for lack of violation; so eating after it is not corruption. The case of the sleeping woman is clear. Regarding the insane woman: she intended fasting then became insane during the day while fasting, and a person had intercourse with her. Insanity does not contradict fasting; it only contradicts its condition, i.e., intention, which existed during consciousness. So she does not have to make up that day if she regains consciousness. If she was forced to have intercourse, she must make it up because a corrupting element came upon a valid fast. With this is refuted what was said that it was originally 'al-majburah' (the coerced) and the scribe misspelled it as 'al-majnunah' (the insane), due to the possibility of its interpretation. And Allah Almighty knows best.
Source pages p. 513

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a detailed intra-Hanafi discussion on when kaffarah (expiation) is due for breaking a fast under the influence of doubt (shubha). The author, Ibn Nujaym, states the ruling that a layperson must act upon a general hadith only if he knows its abrogation status, contrary to Abu Yusuf. Then he discusses cases: if someone touches or kisses with desire, or uses kohl, thinking it invalidates the fast, then intentionally eats, kaffarah is due unless he consulted a mufti who allowed breaking the fast, or a report reached him. This establishes that the layperson's madhhab is his mufti's fatwa. However, in cases like oiling the mustache, which is obviously not a break, kaffarah is due even with consultation because there is no shubha. The author then mentions a conflict between al-Muhit and al-Tabyin regarding backbiting, preferring al-Muhit because backbiting creates shubha. He sets conditions for the mufti: he must be a reliable scholar whose fatwa is accepted locally. Finally, he discusses the sleeping and insane women: if they are forced to have intercourse and then eat, no kaffarah because the fast was already corrupted by the forcible intercourse; the eating after is not a fresh violation. The insane woman's fast is valid if she intended while conscious; if she is later forced to have intercourse, she must make up that day because the corruption occurred on a valid fast. The author concludes by addressing a scribal error where 'majburah' (coerced) was written as 'majnunah' (insane), interpreting the text accordingly.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubha): A legal doubt that prevents penal or expiatory consequences due to conflicting evidence or authoritative opinion.
عامي ('ammi): A layperson who is not a qualified mujtahid and must follow a mufti's fatwa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under what condition does a layperson owe kaffarah after acting on a mufti's fatwa that a certain act breaks the fast?
A) Always, because he still broke the fast.
B) Only if the act was obviously not a fast-breaker.
C) Only if the mufti was not qualified.
D) Never, because a fatwa always creates a shubha.

Key Takeaway
A layperson's valid shubha arises only from a qualified mufti's fatwa on ambiguous acts; obvious acts or direct reliance on hadith without a mufti do not excuse from kaffarah.

Obvious vs. Ambiguous Acts: When Shubha Does Not Protect
Intra-Hanafi dispute on obvious vs. ambiguous acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between obviously non-invalidative acts and ambiguous acts in fasting, and apply the ruling on kaffarah and shubha accordingly.

Core Idea
Obvious acts like oiling the mustache cannot create shubha even with a fatwa; only ambiguous acts generate valid doubt.

In the previous lesson, we learned that a mufti's fatwa can create a shubha that drops kaffarah. However, this is not absolute. The text makes a crucial distinction: if the act is obviously not a fast-breaker (e.g., oiling the mustache), even a mufti's fatwa or personal interpretation does not generate a valid shubha, and kaffarah remains wajib. This lesson explores the basis for this distinction and applies it to the debated case of backbiting, where the author prefers al-Muhit's view that backbiting is ambiguous enough to create shubha.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Oiling Mustache Case
From al-Bada'i: if a man oils his mustache and thinks it breaks the fast, then eats intentionally, he owes kaffarah even if he consulted a mufti or interpreted a hadith. Understand why: the act of oiling is clearly not a fast-breaker by consensus, so no doubt can be entertained.
This establishes the rule for obvious acts.
2
Apply the Rule to Backbiting
The text presents a conflict: al-Muhit holds that backbiting is ambiguous and a fatwa creates shubha, so no kaffarah. Al-Tabyin and the majority hold that backbiting is like oiling – obviously not a physical break – so kaffarah is due even with a fatwa.
Backbiting is a sin but does it break the fast? The dispute centers on whether it is 'obviously not a break' or whether it involves spiritual invalidation.
3
Analyze the Author's Tarjih
The author says 'the apparent preference is for what is in al-Muhit due to doubt'. That is, backbiting is sufficiently ambiguous to generate a valid shubha, so the fatwa drops kaffarah.
This is a nuanced position; note that the author does not explicitly overrule al-Tabyin but gives preference.
4
Derive the General Principle
The distinction depends on the 'obviousness' of the act. If the act is unanimously or overwhelmingly recognized as not affecting the fast (like external application of oil), no shubha. If there is scholarly disagreement or uncertainty (like backbiting's effect on the fast), shubha is possible.
This principle guides future cases.

Source Text

كتاب الصوم فصل في العوارض واجب العمل به خلافاً لأبي يوسف لأنه ليس للعامي العمل بالحديث لعدم علمه بالناسخ والمنسوخ. ولو لمس امرأة وقبلها بشهوة أو اكتحل فظن أن ذلك يفطره ثم أفطر فعليه الكفارة إلا إذا استفتى فقيهاً فأفتاه بالفطر أو بلغه خبر فيه . ولو نوى الصوم قبل الزوال ثم أفطر لم تلزمه الكفارة عند أبي حنيفة خلافاً لهما كذا في المحيط . وقد علم من هذا أن مذهب العامي فتوى مفتيه من غير تقييد بمذهب، ولهذا قال في فتح القدير : الحكم في حق العامي فتوى مفتيه . وفي البدائع : ولو دهن شاربه فظن أنه أفطر فأكل عمداً فعليه الكفارة وإن استفتى فقيهاً أو تأول حديثاً لأن هذا مما لا يشتبه وكذا لو اغتاب .اهـ. وفي التبيين أن عليه عامة المشايخ وهو في الغيبة مخالف لما في المحيط، والظاهر ترجيح ما في المحيط للشبهة . وفي النهاية : ويشترط أن يكون المفتي ممن يؤخذ منه الفقه ويعتمد على فتواه في البلدة وحينئذ تصير فتواه شبهة ولا معتبر بغيره . وأما النائمة أو المجنونة إذا أكلتا بعدما جومعتا فلا كفارة عليهما لأن الفساد حصل بالجماع قبل الأكل كالمخطئ ولا كفارة لعدم الجناية فالأكل بعده ليس بإفساد وصورتها في النائمة ظاهر . وفي المجنونة بأن نوت الصوم ثم جنت بالنهار وهي صائمة فجامعها إنسان فإن الجنون لا ينافي الصوم إنما ينافي شرطه أعني النية وقد وجد في حال الإفاقة فلا يجب قضاء ذلك اليوم إذا أفاقت، فإذا جومعت قضته لطرق المفسد على صوم صحيح، وبهذا اندفع ما قيل إنها كانت في الأصل المجبورة أي المكرهة فصحفها الكاتب إلى المجنونة لإمكان توجيهها كما ذكرناه والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم.
Book of Fasting - Section on Impediments: It is obligatory to act upon it (the hadith) contrary to Abu Yusuf because the layperson cannot act upon hadith due to not knowing the abrogating and abrogated. If a man touched a woman and kissed her with desire, or used kohl, and thought that breaks the fast, then broke his fast, he owes expiation unless he consulted a jurist who gave him a fatwa of breaking the fast, or a report reached him about it. If he intended fasting before noon then broke his fast, expiation is not required according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two companions, as in al-Muhit. It is known from this that the madhhab of the layperson is the fatwa of his mufti without restriction to a madhhab. For this reason it is said in Fath al-Qadir: The ruling for the layperson is the fatwa of his mufti. In al-Bada'i: If he oiled his mustache and thought he broke his fast, then ate intentionally, he owes expiation even if he consulted a jurist or interpreted a hadith, because this is not ambiguous; likewise if he backbited. In al-Tabyin: the majority of scholars hold that he owes expiation, and this regarding backbiting contradicts what is in al-Muhit; the apparent preference is for what is in al-Muhit due to doubt. In al-Nihaya: It is required that the mufti be someone from whom fiqh is taken and whose fatwa is relied upon in the town; then his fatwa becomes a doubt, and others are not considered. As for the sleeping or insane woman, if they ate after being forced to have intercourse, there is no expiation on them because the corruption occurred by intercourse before eating, like the mistaken person, and no expiation for lack of violation; so eating after it is not corruption. The case of the sleeping woman is clear. Regarding the insane woman: she intended fasting then became insane during the day while fasting, and a person had intercourse with her. Insanity does not contradict fasting; it only contradicts its condition, i.e., intention, which existed during consciousness. So she does not have to make up that day if she regains consciousness. If she was forced to have intercourse, she must make it up because a corrupting element came upon a valid fast. With this is refuted what was said that it was originally 'al-majburah' (the coerced) and the scribe misspelled it as 'al-majnunah' (the insane), due to the possibility of its interpretation. And Allah Almighty knows best.

A) Kaffarah is always due because backbiting is a sin.
B) Kaffarah is never due because backbiting is not physical.

D) Kaffarah is due only if the person did not consult a mufti.

Special Cases: Intention Before Noon, Sleep, and Insanity
Intra-Hanafi dispute on intention before noon and special women cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the ruling on kaffarah for breaking fast after intending before noon, and analyze the effect of coercion and insanity on fasting obligations and kaffarah.

Core Idea
Kaffarah is dropped if the fast was already invalidated by an earlier event; intention before noon affects kaffarah only according to Abu Hanifa.

The passage concludes with two special cases that further illustrate the limits of kaffarah. First, the dispute regarding intention to fast before noon: Abu Hanifa holds that if someone intends fasting before noon and then breaks, no kaffarah is due, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say kaffarah is due. Second, the cases of sleeping and insane women who are forced to have intercourse: no kaffarah because the fast was already corrupted by the forcible act before the eating occurred. Additionally, the text clarifies that an insane woman's fast is valid if she intended while conscious; if she is later coerced, she must make up the day but owes no kaffarah. This lesson examines these rulings and their underlying principles.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Intention Before Noon Dispute
The text states: 'If he intended fasting before noon then broke his fast, expiation is not required according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two companions.' Understand the rationale: Abu Hanifa views the intention before noon as insufficient to fully establish the fast; therefore, breaking it is not a complete violation deserving kaffarah. The Sahibayn consider the intention valid and thus kaffarah due.
This dispute is separate from the shubha issue.
2
Examine the Sleeping Woman Case
If a sleeping woman is forced to have intercourse and then eats, no kaffarah. The passage says: 'because the corruption occurred by intercourse before eating, like the mistaken person, and no expiation for lack of violation; so eating after it is not corruption.'
The key: the eating does not cause a fresh corruption; the fast was already invalidated involuntarily.
3
Examine the Insane Woman Case
A woman intends fasting, then becomes insane during the day. Insanity does not affect the fast's validity (since intention existed). If she is then forced to have intercourse, the fast is corrupted, and she must make up the day. No kaffarah because she was coerced (no volition).
This case also addresses a potential scribal error: 'majnunah' might have been 'majburah' (coerced).
4
Derive the Principle
Kaffarah only applies when the invalidating act (eating) is the direct cause of breaking a valid fast. If the fast was already nullified by a prior involuntary act, the subsequent eating is not a fresh violation warranting expiation.
This reinforces the 'direct causation' principle in kaffarah.
