Rulings on Vowing Specific Days (Monday, Thursday, etc.)
Detailed rulings on interpreting ambiguous vow expressions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the number of days required for a vow involving specific day names, indefinite numerical expressions, and duration phrases according to Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Hanafi fiqh fixes the meaning of ambiguous vow expressions by linguistic principles and customary usage, not the vow-maker's subjective intent.

This passage from al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya provides detailed rulings on how to interpret vows that mention specific days (like Monday or Thursday) or use indefinite numbers. Understanding these rules is essential for correctly fulfilling one's obligations when making a vow (nadh). The rulings illustrate a key Hanafi approach: when the wording is ambiguous, the default interpretation is based on apparent meaning and custom, not unexpressed intentions.

Guided Path
1
Classify the vow expression
Identify whether the vow mentions a specific day name (e.g., Monday, Thursday), a numerical expression (e.g., kadha kadha), or a duration (e.g., a month, a year). The interpretation differs for each category.
The passage also covers the term 'Jumu'ah' which can mean the week or the day of Friday depending on intent.
2
Apply the rule for specific day without duration
If the vow is simply to fast a specific day (e.g., 'I vow to fast Monday') and the person fasts it once, it suffices unless he intended perpetuity (al-abad), i.e., to fast every Monday indefinitely.
Intention of perpetuity must be explicit; otherwise, one observance fulfills the vow.
3
Apply the rule for specific day with duration (month/year)
If the vow specifies a duration like 'a month' or 'a year' after naming the day, the person must fast every occurrence of that day within the duration. For example, vowing to fast Thursday for a month means fasting every Thursday that falls in the next 30 days, resulting in 4 or 5 fasts.
For a year, fast every Monday for a year according to the relied-upon view; al-Karkhi said fast thirty such days.
4
Apply the rule for indefinite number expressions
For expressions like 'kadha kadha yawman' (so-and-so days without conjunction), the obligated number is 11 days. If the conjunction 'wa' is used ('kadha wa kadha'), it is 21 days. For 'bid'ata ashar' (several ten), it is 13 days.
These are based on customary usage (urf), not literal linguistic minimums.
5
Determine for 'Jumu'ah' based on intention
If one vows to fast 'Jumu'ah,' and he intends the week (days of Friday) or has no specific intention, he must fast seven days. If he intends the day of Friday, he fasts only Friday, as he is believed in judgment regarding his intention.
This follows the principle that ambiguous words are interpreted according to the speaker's intention if plausible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الفتاوى الظهيرية : ولو نذر صوم يوم الاثنين أو الخميس فصام ذلك مرة كفاه إلا أن ينوي الأبد، ولو أوجب اليوم شهراً صام ما تكرر منه في ثلاثين يوماً يعني إن كان ذلك اليوم يوم الخميس يصوم كل خميس حتى يمضي شهر فيكون الواجب صوم أربعة أيام أو خمسة أيام، وكذلك لو قال لله علي أن أصوم يوم الاثنين سنة. ولو قال لله علي يوماً ويوماً يلزمه صوم يوم إلا أن ينوي الأبد كما إذا قال لامرأته أنت طالق يوماً ويوماً لا . ولو قال لله علي أن أصوم كذا كذا يوماً يلزمه صوم أحد عشر يوماً وهذا مشكل وكان ينبغي أن يلزمه اثنا عشر لأن كذا اسم عدد بدليل أنه لو قال لفلان علي كذا درهماً يلزمه در همان وقد جمع بين عددين ليس بينهما حرف العطف وأقله اثنا فتشبيهه بصوم الأمس صحيح فتدبر قوله : (وكذلك لو قال الله علي أن أصوم يوم الاثنين سنة) كذا في بعض النسخ، وفي بعضها ولو قال بدون كذلك وبعد قوله (سنة) بياض. والذي رأيته في الظهيرية ولو كهذه النسخة وبعد قوله «سنة» ما نصه : وعن الكرخي يصوم ثلاثين مثل ذلك اليوم ا هـ . ورأيت في هامش البحر نسخة بخط بعضهم أنه راجع نسختين من الظهيرية فوجد فيهما ما ذكرنا، والذي رأيته في الخانية بلفظ : وكذا لو قال الله علي أن أصوم يوم الاثنين سنة كان عليه أن يصوم كل اثنين يمر به إلى سنة وعن الكرخي الخ قوله : (ولو قال الله علي يوماً) أي أن أصوم يوماً. وقوله «ويوماً لا أي لا أصومه وقوله إلا أن ينوي «الأبد أي فيلزمه صيام داود عليه السلام كما في التتارخانية .عشر، ولو قال كذا وكذا يلزمه أحد وعشرون، ولو قال بضعة عشر يلزمه ثلاثة عشر، وسيأتي أجناس هذا في كتاب الإقرار ولو قال لله علي أن أصوم جمعة، إن أراد بها أيام الجمعة أو لم تكن له نية يلزمه صوم سبعة أيام، وإن أراد بها يوم الجمعة يلزمه يوم الجمعة لأنه نوى حقيقة کلامه كما لو حلف أن لا يكلم فلاناً يوماً وأراد به بياض النهار صدق قضاء، ولو قال هذا جمع الشهر فعليه أن يصوم كل يوم جمعة تمر في هذا الشهر .
In al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya: If one vows to fast Monday or Thursday and fasts it once, it suffices him unless he intends perpetuity. If he makes a day obligatory for a month, he fasts whatever repeats from that day in thirty days, meaning if that day is Thursday, he fasts every Thursday until a month passes, so the obligation is four or five days. Similarly, if he says 'For Allah I must fast Monday for a year.' And if he says 'For Allah, a day and a day,' he is obliged to fast one day unless he intends perpetuity, just as if he says to his wife 'You are divorced a day and a day' it is not. And if he says 'For Allah I must fast kadha kadha day,' he is obliged to fast eleven days, and this is problematic; it should be twelve because 'kadha' is a noun for number, as evidence if he says 'I owe so-and-so kadha dirhams' he is obliged to two dirhams, and he has combined two numbers without a conjunction, and the minimum is two, so comparing it to fasting yesterday is correct. Ponder. Similarly, if he says 'For Allah I must fast Monday for a year' – in some copies it says that, and in some without 'similarly' and after 'year' there is a blank. What I saw in al-Zahiriyya is like this copy and after 'year' is the text: 'From al-Karkhi: he fasts thirty like that day.' And I saw in the margin of al-Bahr a copy by some handwriting that he checked two copies of al-Zahiriyya and found in them what we mentioned. And what I saw in al-Khaniyya with wording: 'Similarly, if he says For Allah I must fast Monday for a year, he must fast every Monday that passes until a year, and from al-Karkhi...' Ten, and if he says 'kadha kadha' he is obliged to twenty-one, and if he says 'bid'ata ashar' he is obliged to thirteen. The categories of this will come in the Book of Iqrār. And if he says 'For Allah I must fast a Jumu'ah,' if he intends the days of Friday (i.e., the whole week) or has no intention, he must fast seven days; if he intends the day of Friday, he must fast Friday because he intended the reality of his words, just as if he swore not to speak to someone for a day and meant the daytime, he is believed in judgment. And if he says 'this is the collection of the month,' then he must fast every Friday that passes in that month.
Source pages p. 518

Commentary
This lengthy passage from al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya lays out a series of rulings on how to interpret ambiguous vows about fasting specific days. The general principle is that if one vows to fast a day of the week (like Monday) once, it suffices unless he intends perpetuity (abad). If he says 'a month' of that day, he fasts every occurrence of that day within a 30-day period, so he may fast 4 or 5 times depending on when the month starts. If he says 'a day and a day,' only one day is obligated because the repetition is interpreted as emphasis unless he intends perpetuity. For numerical expressions like 'kadha kadha yawman' (so-and-so days), the minimum is 11 (two numbers without conjunction, minimum 2 and 9? Actually the text says it's problematic and should be 12, but the established ruling is 11). Similarly, 'kadha wa kadha' is 21, 'bid'ata ashar' (several ten) is 13. For 'Jumu'ah,' depending on intent, it could mean seven days (the whole week) or just Friday. The passage also quotes al-Karkhi's opinion on Monday for a year. These rulings are based on linguistic principles and customary usage, and they are applied in Hanafi fiqh for making vows. The overall lesson is that one must be precise in wording when making a vow, and the jurists interpret according to the apparent meaning unless there is a clear contrary intention.

Key Terms
يَوْمُ الاِثْنَيْن (yawm al-ithnayn): Monday, a specific day of the week; used in vows to refer to that day.
الأَبَد (al-abad): Perpetuity; intending to fast that day every week indefinitely.
كذا (kadha): An expression denoting an indefinite number; when used twice without a conjunction, it means 11; with 'wa' it means 21; 'bid'ata ashar' means 13.
جُمُعَة (jumu'ah): Friday or the week (days of Friday). In vows, default is seven days unless the person intended the day of Friday.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) 7 days
C) 30 days
D) 11 days

Key Takeaway
The number of days required by a vow is determined by the customary meaning of the words used, not by the vow-maker's unexpressed intentions.

Interpretation of Vows Involving Saturday and Continuous Fasting
Al-Sarakhsi explains interpretation of vows involving Saturday and continuous fasting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for interpreting vows involving repeated days and state the validity of continuous vs. separate fasting.

Core Idea
A vow mentioning 'Saturday eight days' means two Saturdays; 'seven days' means seven Saturdays.

When someone vows to fast on a specific day like Saturday, how do we interpret the number of days? Al-Sarakhsi provides a key principle: if the number exceeds the days of the week, the vow is interpreted as multiple occurrences of that day. Additionally, if one vows a continuous fast, breaking the sequence invalidates the vow; but if no continuity is specified, fasting continuously is acceptable and even better.

Guided Path
1
Identify the vow's wording
What did the person exactly say? Look at the specific day and number mentioned.
The ruling depends on whether the day repeats within the given number of days.
2
Check if the number exceeds the week cycle
If the number is greater than the days in a week (7), the day must repeat; thus interpret as multiple Saturdays. If the number is 7 or less, it's likely one Saturday per the number.
Seven days can mean seven Saturdays because each Saturday is a distinct day within a multi-week period.
3
Apply the principle of tatabu' (continuity)
If the vow specifies 'continuous fasting', then fasting separately does not fulfill it. If no continuity is mentioned, fasting continuously is valid (since it is a better form).
The opposite—vowing separately but fasting continuously—is also valid because the extra continuity does not violate the vow.
4
State the ruling
Conclude whether the fast is valid and what the person must do.
Al-Sarakhsi's view is the most sound (al-asahh) for the interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال شمس الأئمة السرخسي : هو الأصح، ولو قال صوم أيام الجمعة فعليه صوم سبعة أيام، ولو قال الله علي أن أصوم السبت ثمانية أيام لزمه صوم سبتين، ولو قال لله علي أن أصوم السبت سبعة أيام لزمه صوم سبعة أسبات لأن السبت في سبعة أيام لا يتكرر فحمل کلامه على عدد الأسبات بخلاف الثمانية لأن السبت فيها يتكرر، ولو أوجب على نفسه صوماً متتابعاً فصامه متفرقاً لم يجز، وعلى عكسه جاز.
Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi said: This is the most sound. If he said 'fast the days of Friday,' then he must fast seven days. If he said 'For Allah I must fast Saturday eight days,' then he must fast two Saturdays. If he said 'For Allah I must fast Saturday seven days,' then he must fast seven Saturdays because Saturday does not repeat within seven days, so his words are interpreted as referring to the number of Saturdays, unlike eight because Saturday repeats within it. And if he obligated upon himself a continuous fast and fasted it separately, it is invalid; and the opposite is valid.
Source pages p. 519

Commentary
This passage by Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi clarifies how to interpret vows involving the word 'Saturday' when combined with a number. If one says 'fast Saturday eight days,' it means two Saturdays (eight days cannot be consecutive Saturdays because there are only seven days in a week, so it must be interpreted as two separate Saturdays). If one says 'Saturday seven days,' it means seven Saturdays (since seven Saturdays can fit over time). The second ruling concerns the condition of continuity: if one vows a continuous fast (mutatābi') and then fasts with breaks, it does not fulfill the vow; however, if one vows a non-continuous fast and fasts continuously, it is valid (since continuity is better). This is a straightforward ruling based on the intention of the vow.

Key Terms
التتابع (al-tatabu'): Continuity; fasting day after day without interruption.
السبت (al-sabt): Saturday, a day of the week.
أسبات (asbāt): Plural of Sabt; multiple Saturdays.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Two Saturdays
C) Nine Saturdays
D) Nine consecutive days including a Saturday

Key Takeaway
When a vow names a specific day with a number, the number is interpreted as the count of that day's occurrences, considering how many times it can appear in that period.

Conditional Vows and the Qiyas/Istihsan Conflict
Explaining rulings on conditional vows, arrival of a person, and the effect of intent in fulfilling vows.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a conditional vow without the phrase 'for Allah' and explain why qiyas and istihsan produce different outcomes.

Core Idea
A conditional vow without 'for Allah' is not binding by qiyas, but istihsan makes it obligatory if the wording implies a condition.

The Hanafi school often encounters a tension between strict analogy (qiyas) and juristic preference (istihsan). This passage presents several scenarios where the validity of a conditional vow depends on both the wording and the underlying intent. Understanding this tension is key to applying the school's methodology in resolving apparent contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the vow type
Determine whether the vow is conditional (nathr mu'allaq) or unconditional. Note the presence or absence of the phrase 'for Allah' (lillahi).
The first example explicitly includes 'for Allah', while the last does not.
2
Apply qiyas
Apply standard analogical reasoning: if a vow lacks the necessary condition (e.g., the phrase 'for Allah'), by qiyas it is not binding.
Compare with the case of 'If I am cured, I fast' without 'for Allah'.
3
Apply istihsan
Consider istihsan: the intent to create a binding obligation, even without the explicit phrase, may make the vow binding due to custom or public interest.
The passage states: 'by istihsan it is obligatory'.
4
Resolve the conflict
In Hanafi fiqh, istihsan often overrides qiyas when there is a stronger reason, such as fulfilling the apparent intent of the vow.
The mufta bihi position in later Hanafi practice is to adopt istihsan in such cases.
5
Apply to other scenarios
Use the same framework to analyze the other cases: the effect of arrival after noon, the overlap with Ramadan, and problems of continuity.
Each scenario illustrates how qiyas and istihsan might yield different rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال لله علي أن أصوم اليوم الذي يقدم فيه فلان فقدم فيه فلان بعدما أكل أو كانت الناذرة امرأة فحاضت لا يجب شيء في قول محمد، وعلى قياس قول أبي حنيفة يجب القضاء. ولو قدم بعد الزوال لا يلزمه شيء في قول محمد ولا رواية فيه عن غيره. ولو قال لله علي أن أصوم اليوم الذي يقدم فيه فلان شكراً لله تعالى وأراد به اليمين فقدم فلان في يوم من رمضان كان عليه كفارة اليمين ولا قضاء عليه لأنه لم يوجد شرط البر وهو الصوم بنية الشكر، ولو قدم فلان قبل أن ينوي صوم رمضان فنوى به عن الشكر ولا ينوي به عن رمضان بر في يمينه لوجود شرط البر وهو الصوم بنية الشكر وأجزأه عن رمضان كما لو صام رمضان بنية التطوع وليس عليه قضاؤه . ولو قال الله علي مثل شهر رمضان فإن أراد مثله في الوجوب فله أن يفرق، وإن أراد به في التتابع فعليه أن يتابع وإن لم يكن له نية فله أن يصوم متفرقاً لأنه محتمل لهما فكان له الخيار . ولو قال لله علي أن أصوم عشرة أيام متتابعات فصام خمسة عشر يوماً وأفطر يوماً لا يدري أن الخمسة أو الإفطار من من العشرة فإنه يصوم خمسة أيام أخر متتابعات فيوجد عشرة متتابعة . ولو قال لله علي صوم نصف يوم لا يصح بخلاف نصف ركعة حيث يصح عند محمد، ونصف حج لا يصح . ولو نذر صوم شهرين متتابعين من يوم قدوم فلان فقدم في شعبان بني بعد رمضان كما في الحيض. ولو قال إن عوفيت صمت كذا لم يجب عليه حتى يقول لله علي وهذا قياس، وفي الاستحسان يجب، فإن لم يكن تعليق لا يجب عليه قياساً ولا استحساناً نظيره ما إذا قال أنا أحج لا شيء عليه ولو قال إن فعلت كذا فأنا أحج ففعل يلزمه ذلك.
And if he says 'For Allah I must fast the day when so-and-so arrives' and so-and-so arrives after he has eaten, or the one vowing is a woman and she menstruates, nothing is obligatory according to Muhammad, and by qiyas of Abu Hanifa's statement, he must make up. And if he arrives after noon, nothing is necessary according to Muhammad, and there is no narration from others. And if he says 'For Allah I must fast the day when so-and-so arrives as a thankfulness to Allah' and he intended it as an oath, and so-and-so arrives during a day of Ramadan, then he must pay the expiation of the oath and no make-up because the condition of fulfillment—fasting with the intention of thankfulness—did not exist. And if so-and-so arrives before he intends fasting Ramadan, and he intends it for thankfulness and does not intend it for Ramadan, he fulfills his oath because the condition of fulfillment—fasting with the intention of thankfulness—existed, and it suffices for Ramadan just as if he fasted Ramadan with the intention of supererogatory worship, and he does not have to make it up. And if he says 'For Allah, like Ramadan,' if he intends its like in obligation, he may fast separately; if he intends by it continuity, he must fast continuously; and if he has no intention, he may fast separately because it is plausible for both, so he has the choice. And if he says 'For Allah I must fast ten continuous days,' and he fasts fifteen days and breaks one day, not knowing whether the five or the break is from the ten, then he fasts another five continuous days, and thus ten continuous days are achieved. And if he says 'For Allah I must fast half a day,' it is not valid, unlike half a rak'ah which is valid according to Muhammad, and half a Hajj is invalid. And if he vows to fast two continuous months from the day of arrival of so-and-so, and he arrives in Sha'ban, he builds after Ramadan as in menstruation. And if he says 'If I am cured, I fast such-and-such,' it is not obligatory upon him until he says 'For Allah,' and this is by qiyas; by istihsan it is obligatory. If there is no condition, it is not obligatory by qiyas or istihsan. Its analogy is if he says 'I will perform Hajj,' nothing is upon him; but if he says 'If I do such-and-such, I will perform Hajj,' and he does it, it becomes obligatory upon him.
Source pages p. 519

Commentary
This passage covers multiple scenarios involving conditional vows (nathr mu'allaq). The first scenario: if one vows to fast the day of someone's arrival, and he arrives after the person has already eaten (or the woman menstruates), then according to Imam Muhammad no make-up is required, but by analogy (qiyas) on Abu Hanifa's view, one must make up. If the arrival is after noon, Muhammad says nothing is required. The second scenario involves a vow made 'as thankfulness' but intended as an oath: if the condition is fulfilled during Ramadan, the oath is violated and only expiation is due; but if the person arrives before intending the Ramadan fast and fasts with the intention of thankfulness, it fulfills the oath and also counts for Ramadan. Third, if one says 'like Ramadan,' the interpretation depends on his intent. Fourth, if one says 'ten continuous days' and fasts fifteen with a break in between, he can fast five more continuous days to achieve ten consecutive days. Fifth, fasting half a day is invalid (unlike half a rak'ah in prayer according to Muhammad). Sixth, if one vows two continuous months from the day of arrival and the arrival is in Sha'ban, he can build (continue) after Ramadan because Ramadan does not break continuity, like menstruation. Seventh, a conditional statement like 'if I am cured, I fast' without the words 'for Allah' is not binding by qiyas, but by istihsan it is binding if it is a condition (ta'liq), otherwise not. The passage shows the interplay between qiyas and istihsan in Hanafi fiqh, with istihsan often being the preferred method for applying the spirit of the law.

Key Terms
النذر المعلق (al-nathr al-mu'allaq): A vow that is conditional upon the occurrence of something.
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning; deriving a ruling from an original case based on a common effective cause ('illah).
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference; a method of departing from qiyas for a stronger reason, often for public interest or ease.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Not binding by qiyas, but binding by istihsan
B) Not binding at all
C) Binding only if the person intended an oath
D) Binding only if the phrase 'for Allah' is added later

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, istihsan can override qiyas in conditional vows when the wording clearly indicates a binding intent, even if the formal phrase 'for Allah' is missing.

Vows with Vague Numbers in Hanafi Fiqh
Ruling on fixed numbers for vague vow expressions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal interpretation of the vague expressions 'كذا وكذا' and 'بضعة عشر' in vows of fasting and apply them to a given scenario.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, 'كذا وكذا' means 21 fasts; 'بضعة عشر' means 13.

When a person makes a vow using vague number expressions like 'such and such' or 'some ten,' what fasting obligation do they incur? The Hanafi school assigns specific numbers to these phrases based on established legal convention, not on the individual's intent. This lesson clarifies these fixed interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the vague expression
Determine whether the vow contains the phrase 'كذا وكذا' or 'بضعة عشر'.
2
Recall the fixed number
For 'كذا وكذا', the obligation is 21 fasts. For 'بضعة عشر', it is 13 fasts.
These numbers are established by Hanafi legal convention, not by qiyas.
3
Apply to the scenario
Given a vow with one of these phrases, state the number of fasts required.
No other interpretation is accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال كذا وكذا يلزمه أحد وعشرون، ولو قال بضعة عشر يلزمه ثلاثة عشر، وسيأتي أجناس هذا في كتاب الإقرار
And if he said 'such and such' he is obligated twenty-one, and if he said 'some ten' he is obligated thirteen, and the categories of this will come in the Book of Iqrar.
Source pages p. 519

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement about vague numbers in vows. The author gives specific interpretations: 'كذا وكذا' (such and such) means twenty-one days of fasting, and 'بضعة عشر' (between ten and twenty) means thirteen days. These are established fiqhi conventions. The author notes that detailed categories of such expressions will be discussed later in the Book of Iqrar (Acknowledgments). The ruling is straightforward: the numbers are fixed by the legal convention, not by the vow-maker's intent unless specified.

Key Terms
كذا وكذا (kadhā wa kadhā): A vague expression meaning 'such and such' which, in Hanafi fiqh, is interpreted as twenty-one when used in vows.
بضعة عشر (biḍ'ata 'ashar): An expression meaning 'some ten' (between ten and twenty), interpreted as thirteen in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) An unspecified number

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, vague number expressions in vows have fixed legal meanings: كذا وكذا = 21, بضعة عشر = 13, ensuring clarity and enforceability.

Vow to Fast 'Jumu'ah': Intention and Interpretation
Interpretation of vow to fast 'Jumu'ah' or 'days of Jumu'ah'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the number of fasting days required when someone vows 'Jumu'ah' by applying the Hanafi rules on intention and default interpretation.

Core Idea
A vow to fast 'Jumu'ah' without intention obligates seven days; with explicit intent for Friday, only Friday is owed.

The word 'Jumu'ah' in Arabic can refer to a single Friday or to a week of seven days. When someone makes a vow to fast 'Jumu'ah', the number of days required depends on their intention. This lesson explores the Hanafi position on interpreting ambiguous vow language, drawing an analogy to oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the vow wording
Note the phrase 'Jumu'ah' is ambiguous – it can mean Friday or a week. This triggers the need for interpretation.
Also consider variations like 'days of Jumu'ah' or 'this is the plural of the month'.
2
Determine the presence of intention
Ask: Did the person have a specific intention when uttering the vow? If yes, that intention is honoured. If no intention, the default ruling applies.
Intention is given weight based on the analogy to oaths.
3
Apply the ruling based on intention
If person intended 'days of Jumu'ah' or had no intention, he must fast seven days. If intended the single Friday, only that Friday is owed.
The default interpretation of 'Jumu'ah' without intention is a week.
4
Consider alternative formulations
The phrase 'this is the plural of the month' requires fasting every Friday of that month. 'Days of Jumu'ah' explicitly means seven days.
Customary usage of 'days of Jumu'ah' is the entire week.
5
Note the authoritative view
Shams al-A'immah al-Sarakhsi affirms the above as the most correct position, giving weight to the default of seven days.
This is the mufta bihi (operative ruling) in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال لله علي أن أصوم جمعة، إن أراد بها أيام الجمعة أو لم تكن له نية يلزمه صوم سبعة أيام، وإن أراد بها يوم الجمعة يلزمه يوم الجمعة لأنه نوى حقيقة کلامه كما لو حلف أن لا يكلم فلاناً يوماً وأراد به بياض النهار صدق قضاء، ولو قال هذا جمع الشهر فعليه أن يصوم كل يوم جمعة تمر في هذا الشهر . قال شمس الأئمة السرخسي : هو الأصح، ولو قال صوم أيام الجمعة فعليه صوم سبعة أيام
And if he said 'For Allah upon me I shall fast Jumu'ah' – if he intended by it the days of Jumu'ah or he had no intention, he is obligated to fast seven days. And if he intended by it the day of Jumu'ah, he is obligated to fast the day of Jumu'ah, because he intended the reality of his speech, just as if he swore not to speak to so-and-so for a day and intended the white of the day, he is believed judicially. And if he said 'this is the plural of the month' then he must fast every Friday that passes in that month. Shams al-A'immah al-Sarakhsi said: this is the most correct. And if he said 'fasting the days of Jumu'ah' then he is obligated seven days.
Source pages p. 519

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (by comparison to oaths) about the expression 'Jumu'ah' in vows. The word 'Jumu'ah' is ambiguous: it can mean Friday (a single day) or a week (seven days) as a collective noun. The author clarifies that if the vow-maker intends 'days of Jumu'ah' (i.e., a week) or has no specific intention, he must fast seven days. If he intends specifically Friday, then only that Friday is owed. The reasoning is that a person's intention is given weight in fulfilling the literal meaning of their words, analogous to an oath. The author also notes that if someone says 'this is the plural of the month' (i.e., all Fridays of the month), then each Friday of that month must be fasted. Shams al-A'immah al-Sarakhsi considers this the correct position. Finally, if one says 'days of Jumu'ah' explicitly, the ruling is consistently seven days, as 'days' plural indicates a minimum of three, but here the customary meaning of 'days of Jumu'ah' is all seven days of the week.

Key Terms
جمعة (jumu'ah): Ambiguous term meaning either 'Friday' or 'a week' (seven days). In Hanafi vow fiqh, it is interpreted based on intention or lack thereof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One Friday
B) Seven days
C) Three days
D) Thirty days

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi vow fiqh, ambiguous expressions are interpreted according to intention, with a default meaning of seven days for 'Jumu'ah'.

Vows of Consecutive Fasting and the Day of Arrival
Rulings on consecutive fasting and vows tied to arrival

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on vows of consecutive fasting and on vows tied to a person's arrival day, distinguishing when make-up (qaḍā) is required and when it is not based on the condition of fulfillment (sharṭ al-birr).

Core Idea
A vowed consecutive fast must be performed consecutively; an arrival-day vow requires the arrival before the day begins for obligation.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq examines two distinct vow scenarios: consecutive fasting and vows linked to someone's arrival. It clarifies when failure to meet the exact condition triggers a make-up or expiation, and when the vow is simply excused. The discussion includes an intra-Hanafi disagreement between Imām Muḥammad and Imām Abū Ḥanīfa on whether a missed arrival-day fast must be made up.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what is being vowed and the initial ruling: a vow of consecutive fasting requires consecutive performance; if done non-consecutively, it is invalid. The reverse (vowing non-consecutive and doing consecutive) is valid.
2
Analyze the arrival-day vow conditions
When a vow is tied to the day of someone's arrival, the condition of fulfillment (sharṭ al-birr) is that the vow-maker must fast that day with the specific intention (e.g., gratitude). If the arrival occurs after the vow-maker has already eaten or after midday, the condition cannot be met.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imām Muḥammad holds that if the arrival occurs after a preventive event (eating, menstruation), nothing is owed. Imām Abū Ḥanīfa's qiyas would require make-up (qaḍā). The text gives weight to Muḥammad's view.
4
Understand the 'shukran' variant
If the vow is phrased as 'shukran lillāh' and intended as an oath (yamīn), the arrival on a Ramadan day breaks the oath (since one cannot fast with the intention of gratitude during Ramadan), necessitating kaffārat al-yamīn, not qaḍā.
5
Apply the exception when arrival precedes Ramadan intention
If arrival occurs before intending the Ramadan fast, fasting with gratitude intention fulfills the vow and also counts for Ramadan, analogous to a supererogatory intention covering a missed Ramadan day.
This shows the power of intention (niyya) in determining legal effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أوجب على نفسه صوماً متتابعاً فصامه متفرقاً لم يجز، وعلى عكسه جاز. ولو قال لله علي أن أصوم اليوم الذي يقدم فيه فلان فقدم فيه فلان بعدما أكل أو كانت الناذرة امرأة فحاضت لا يجب شيء في قول محمد، وعلى قياس قول أبي حنيفة يجب القضاء. ولو قدم بعد الزوال لا يلزمه شيء في قول محمد ولا رواية فيه عن غيره. ولو قال لله علي أن أصوم اليوم الذي يقدم فيه فلان شكراً لله تعالى وأراد به اليمين فقدم فلان في يوم من رمضان كان عليه كفارة اليمين ولا قضاء عليه لأنه لم يوجد شرط البر وهو الصوم بنية الشكر، ولو قدم فلان قبل أن ينوي صوم رمضان فنوى به عن الشكر ولا ينوي به عن رمضان بر في يمينه لوجود شرط البر وهو الصوم بنية الشكر وأجزأه عن رمضان كما لو صام رمضان بنية التطوع وليس عليه قضاؤه
And if he obligated on himself a consecutive fast and he fasted it non-consecutively, it is not valid; and the opposite is valid. And if he said 'For Allah upon me I shall fast the day in which so-and-so arrives' and so-and-so arrived after he had eaten, or if the vow-maker was a woman and she menstruated, nothing is obligatory according to Muhammad, and by analogy to Abu Hanifa's statement, he must make up (qaḍā'). And if he arrived after midday, nothing is obligatory according to Muhammad, and there is no narration from others about it. And if he said 'For Allah upon me I shall fast the day in which so-and-so arrives, as gratitude to Allah the Exalted' and he intended by it an oath, then so-and-so arrived on a day of Ramadan, upon him is the expiation of an oath and no qaḍā', because the condition of fulfillment (al-birr) did not exist, which is fasting with the intention of gratitude. And if so-and-so arrived before he intended the fast of Ramadan, and he intended it for gratitude and does not intend it for Ramadan, he fulfills his oath due to the existence of the condition of fulfillment, which is fasting with the intention of gratitude, and it suffices him for Ramadan, just as if he fasted Ramadan with the intention of supererogatory (nafl) and there is no qaḍā' upon him.
Source pages p. 519

Commentary
This passage contains several rulings. First, a vow of consecutive fasting requires consecutive performance; if done non-consecutively, it is invalid. However, the opposite (vowing non-consecutive and doing consecutive) is valid, as the requirement is less strict. Second, a vow tied to the day of someone's arrival: if the person arrives after he has already eaten (i.e., the day has passed without fasting from pre-dawn), or if the vow-maker is a woman who menstruates on that day (preventing fasting), Imām Muhammad says no obligation (since the fast was impossible on that day). Abū Ḥanīfa's analogue would require make-up (qaḍā') because the day was designated but not kept. If arrival is after noontime (zawāl), again Imām Muhammad says nothing is owed; there is no narration from others. Third, if the vow is phrased as 'shukran lillāh' (gratitude to Allah) and intended as an oath (yamīn), the ruling changes. If arrival coincides with a day of Ramadan (obligatory fast), the vow cannot be fulfilled because one cannot fast that day with the intention of gratitude (since it is Ramadan), so the oath is broken and expiation (kaffārat al-yamīn) is due, but no make-up for the fast. However, if arrival occurs before one has intended the Ramadan fast (i.e., before the first day of Ramadan), and one fasts that day with the intention of gratitude (not Ramadan), the oath is fulfilled, and it also suffices for the Ramadan fast, analogous to one who fasts Ramadan with nafl intention — it still counts, and no qaḍā' is needed. This shows the interplay between intention and legal effect.

Key Terms
كفارة اليمين (kaffārat al-yamīn): Expiation for breaking an oath: feeding ten poor persons, clothing them, or freeing a slave; if unable, fasting three days.
شرط البر (sharṭ al-birr): Condition for fulfilling an oath: performing the vowed act with the intended intention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imām Muḥammad, if a person vows to fast the day of someone's arrival and the person arrives after he has already eaten, what is required?
A) He must fast the next day
B) He must make up the fast (qaḍā')
C) Nothing is obligatory
D) He must give expiation

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the fulfillment of a conditional vow depends on the existence of its condition (sharṭ al-birr); when the condition is impossible, no obligation arises.

Ambiguous Vow: 'Like the Month of Ramadan'
Ruling on ambiguous vow expression

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a vow of 'like Ramadan' and identify how intention determines the outcome.

Core Idea
An ambiguous vow expression gives the vow-maker a choice absent an intention.

This lesson addresses a specific vow expression: 'For Allah upon me like the month of Ramadan'. The phrase 'mithla shahr Ramadan' can refer either to the obligation level or to the consecutiveness of Ramadan. Hanafi fiqh gives the vow-maker the benefit of the doubt when no intention is specified.

Guided Path
1
Identify the vow expression
The vow is 'For Allah upon me like the month of Ramadan.' This is an ambiguous expression that can be interpreted in two ways.
2
Determine the vow-maker's intention
If the vow-maker explicitly intended similarity in obligation (i.e., the fast is obligatory like Ramadan but not necessarily consecutive), then fasting may be staggered (tafarruq). If intended similarity in consecutiveness (since Ramadan is consecutive), then fasting must be consecutive.
3
Apply the ruling when intention is absent
If no intention is stated, the vow-maker has the choice to fast consecutively or intermittently because the expression supports both meanings.
This is a manifestation of the Hanafi principle that ambiguous words are given the broadest plausible interpretation until a specific intent is proven.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال الله علي مثل شهر رمضان فإن أراد مثله في الوجوب فله أن يفرق، وإن أراد به في التتابع فعليه أن يتابع وإن لم يكن له نية فله أن يصوم متفرقاً لأنه محتمل لهما فكان له الخيار . ولو قال لله علي أن أصوم عشرة أيام متتابعات فصام خمسة عشر يوماً وأفطر يوماً لا يدري أن الخمسة أو الإفطار من من العشرة فإنه يصوم خمسة أيام أخر متتابعات فيوجد عشرة متتابعة . ولو قال لله علي صوم نصف يوم لا يصح بخلاف نصف ركعة حيث يصح عند محمد، ونصف حج لا يصح . ولو نذر صوم شهرين متتابعين من يوم قدوم فلان فقدم في شعبان بني بعد رمضان كما في الحيض
And if he said 'For Allah upon me like the month of Ramadan', if he intended its likeness in obligation (wujūb), he may stagger (tafarruq). And if he intended it in consecutiveness (tutābi‘), he must be consecutive. And if he had no intention, he may fast non-consecutively, because it is plausible for both, so he has the choice. And if he said 'For Allah upon me I shall fast ten consecutive days', then he fasted fifteen days and broke one day not knowing whether the extra five or the break was from the ten, then he must fast five more consecutive days, so ten consecutive are achieved. And if he said 'For Allah upon me fasting half a day', it is not valid, unlike half a rak‘ah which is valid according to Muhammad, and half a hajj is not valid. And if he vowed to fast two consecutive months from the day of so-and-so's arrival, and he arrived in Sha‘bān, he builds after Ramadan, just as in menstruation.
Source pages p. 519

Commentary
This passage bundles several distinct rulings. First, 'like the month of Ramadan' (مثل شهر رمضان): if the vow-maker intends similarity in the mode of obligation (i.e., that the fast be like Ramadan in being obligatory but not necessarily consecutive), he may fast intermittently. If he intends consecutiveness (since Ramadan is consecutive), he must be consecutive. Without intention, he has the choice because the phrase is ambiguous. Second, a vow of ten consecutive days: if one mistakenly fasts fifteen days and breaks one day, not knowing whether the break fell within the ten or the extra days were beyond, he must fast five more consecutive days to ensure ten consecutive days (since the break could have been within the ten, ruining the consecutiveness). Third, a vow of fasting 'half a day' is invalid because fasting requires a full day (from dawn to sunset). This is contrasted with half a rak‘ah in prayer, which is valid according to Imām Muhammad (e.g., a traveler combining prayers). Fourth, a vow of two consecutive months from a person's arrival: if the arrival is in Sha‘bān (the month before Ramadan), one cannot complete two consecutive months because Ramadan intervenes and breaks the consecutiveness? Actually, the text says 'he builds after Ramadan, just as in menstruation' meaning that when a woman menstruates and misses days, she continues after her menses, not starting over; similarly, if Ramadan interrupts the consecutive months, he continues after Ramadan, i.e., Ramadān is excluded and the count resumes. Or it may mean that he starts from the day of arrival in Sha‘bān, then after Ramadan, he completes the remaining days. This shows the principle that obligatory fasts of Ramadan do not break the consecutiveness of a vow, analogous to how menstruation does not break consecutiveness; instead, one continues afterward.

Key Terms
مثل شهر رمضان (mithla shahr Ramaḍān): A vow expression that can mean either in terms of obligation or consecutiveness; intention and context determine.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person vows 'For Allah upon me like the month of Ramadan' without any intention, how should he fast?
A) He must fast consecutively, as Ramadan is consecutive.
B) He may fast either consecutively or intermittently, as he chooses.
C) The vow is invalid because it is ambiguous.
D) He must fast the entire month of Ramadan itself.

Key Takeaway
An ambiguous vow expression gives the vow-maker the choice of fulfillment until intention is clarified.

Vow of Ten Consecutive Days with a Mistake
Ruling on corrective measure for uncertain break in consecutive fasts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the corrective procedure when a vow of ten consecutive fasts is performed with an uncertain break.

Core Idea
If a vow of consecutive days is performed with an uncertain break, one must fast additional days to ensure consecutiveness.

When a person vows to fast ten consecutive days but mistakenly fasts fifteen and breaks one day, not knowing if the break occurred during the ten, Hanafi fiqh requires that he fast five additional consecutive days to guarantee ten consecutive fasts. This ensures the vow is fulfilled without doubt.

2
Apply the Hanafi ruling
To remove the doubt, he must fast five more consecutive days. This assures that at least ten consecutive days have been fasted (the original ten plus the new five cover the uncertain period).
The new fasts must be consecutive to create a block of ten that is unbroken.
3
Verify fulfillment
After completing the five consecutive days, the vow is considered fulfilled because there is now a contiguous ten-day fast: the original fasts before the break and the new fasts ensure consecutiveness.

Vow of Half a Day Fasting is Invalid
Ruling on invalid vow of half-day fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a vow to fast half a day is invalid in Hanafi fiqh and contrast it with half a rak'ah in prayer.

Core Idea
A fasting day must be a full day from dawn to sunset; a half-day vow is invalid.

This lesson addresses a vow to fast 'half a day'. Since fasting in Islamic law is defined as abstention from dawn to sunset for a complete day, a half-day fast is not recognized as a valid act of worship. The text contrasts this with half a rak'ah in prayer, which is valid in certain circumstances according to Imām Muhammad, and half a hajj, which is also invalid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the vow
The vow is 'For Allah upon me fasting half a day.' This is a specific duration that does not match the canonical definition of a fast.
2
Apply the ruling of invalidity
The text states that such a vow is not valid (lā yaṣiḥ) because fasting is prescribed from dawn to sunset. Half a day does not fulfill the minimum requirement.
This is an absolute ruling; no intention can make it valid.
3
Contrast with half a rak'ah
The text notes that half a rak'ah is valid according to Imām Muhammad (e.g., in the context of combining prayers by a traveler). This shows that while half of some acts can be valid, half a day's fast cannot.
The difference is that a rak'ah is a divisible unit of prayer, whereas a fast day is an indivisible period.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال الله علي مثل شهر رمضان فإن أراد مثله في الوجوب فله أن يفرق، وإن أراد به في التتابع فعليه أن يتابع وإن لم يكن له نية فله أن يصوم متفرقاً لأنه محتمل لهما فكان له الخيار . ولو قال لله علي أن أصوم عشرة أيام متتابعات فصام خمسة عشر يوماً وأفطر يوماً لا يدري أن الخمسة أو الإفطار من من العشرة فإنه يصوم خمسة أيام أخر متتابعات فيوجد عشرة متتابعة . ولو قال لله علي صوم نصف يوم لا يصح بخلاف نصف ركعة حيث يصح عند محمد، ونصف حج لا يصح . ولو نذر صوم شهرين متتابعين من يوم قدوم فلان فقدم في شعبان بني بعد رمضان كما في الحيض
And if he said 'For Allah upon me like the month of Ramadan', if he intended its likeness in obligation (wujūb), he may stagger (tafarruq). And if he intended it in consecutiveness (tutābi‘), he must be consecutive. And if he had no intention, he may fast non-consecutively, because it is plausible for both, so he has the choice. And if he said 'For Allah upon me I shall fast ten consecutive days', then he fasted fifteen days and broke one day not knowing whether the extra five or the break was from the ten, then he must fast five more consecutive days, so ten consecutive are achieved. And if he said 'For Allah upon me fasting half a day', it is not valid, unlike half a rak‘ah which is valid according to Muhammad, and half a hajj is not valid. And if he vowed to fast two consecutive months from the day of so-and-so's arrival, and he arrived in Sha‘bān, he builds after Ramadan, just as in menstruation.
Source pages p. 519

Commentary
This passage bundles several distinct rulings. First, 'like the month of Ramadan' (مثل شهر رمضان): if the vow-maker intends similarity in the mode of obligation (i.e., that the fast be like Ramadan in being obligatory but not necessarily consecutive), he may fast intermittently. If he intends consecutiveness (since Ramadan is consecutive), he must be consecutive. Without intention, he has the choice because the phrase is ambiguous. Second, a vow of ten consecutive days: if one mistakenly fasts fifteen days and breaks one day, not knowing whether the break fell within the ten or the extra days were beyond, he must fast five more consecutive days to ensure ten consecutive days (since the break could have been within the ten, ruining the consecutiveness). Third, a vow of fasting 'half a day' is invalid because fasting requires a full day (from dawn to sunset). This is contrasted with half a rak‘ah in prayer, which is valid according to Imām Muhammad (e.g., a traveler combining prayers). Fourth, a vow of two consecutive months from a person's arrival: if the arrival is in Sha‘bān (the month before Ramadan), one cannot complete two consecutive months because Ramadan intervenes and breaks the consecutiveness? Actually, the text says 'he builds after Ramadan, just as in menstruation' meaning that when a woman menstruates and misses days, she continues after her menses, not starting over; similarly, if Ramadan interrupts the consecutive months, he continues after Ramadan, i.e., Ramadān is excluded and the count resumes. Or it may mean that he starts from the day of arrival in Sha‘bān, then after Ramadan, he completes the remaining days. This shows the principle that obligatory fasts of Ramadan do not break the consecutiveness of a vow, analogous to how menstruation does not break consecutiveness; instead, one continues afterward.

Key Terms
نصف يوم (niṣf yawm): Half a day; a period that does not meet the definition of a full fast from dawn to sunset.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, is a vow to fast half a day valid?
A) Yes, by analogy to half a rak'ah.
B) Yes, if intended as a supererogatory act.
C) No, it is not valid.
D) Yes, but must be made up with a full day.

Key Takeaway
A vow requiring half a day's fasting is invalid because fasting is defined as a full day's abstention.

Vow of Two Consecutive Months Interrupted by Ramadan
Ruling on consecutiveness of vow months interrupted by Ramadan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the obligatory Ramadan fast does not break the consecutiveness of a vow to fast two consecutive months.

Core Idea
Ramadan is an obligatory interruption that does not nullify the consecutiveness of a vow; one continues after Ramadan.

If one vows to fast two consecutive months from a specific date, and that date falls in Sha‘bān, the month of Ramadan intervenes. Hanafi fiqh treats Ramadan's fasts as an interruption that does not break the consecutiveness; rather, after Ramadan, the remaining days are completed. The analogy is given to a menstruating woman who continues her fast after her menses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the vow and the complication
The vow is to fast two consecutive months starting from the day of a person's arrival. If that arrival occurs in Sha‘bān (the month before Ramadan), the month of Ramadan falls between the two months.
2
Apply the ruling that Ramadan does not break consecutiveness
The text says 'he builds after Ramadan, just as in menstruation.' That means he counts the days he fasted before Ramadan, then after Ramadan, he completes the remaining days to make two months. Ramadan itself is counted as part of the consecutive period? No, Ramadan is not part of the vow; it is an independent obligation. The vow's consecutiveness is maintained despite the interruption because the interruption is obligatory and unavoidable.
The analogy: a woman who misses some days of her consecutive vow due to menstruation continues after her menses, not starting over.
3
Calculate the fulfillment
For example, the person arrives in the middle of Sha‘bān. He fasts the remaining days of Sha‘bān (say 15 days). Then Ramadan comes. After Ramadan and Eid, he resumes fasting the remaining days of the first month plus the full second month, ensuring two consecutive months in total, with Ramadan acting as a break that does not reset the consecutiveness.
The key is that the consecutiveness requirement is satisfied as long as the fasts are in continuous order, allowing the interruption of Ramadan.

Source Text

Invalid Vows to Created Beings
Fatwa-like ruling on common invalid vows

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on vows made to saints or dead people and explain the three reasons for invalidity.

Core Idea
A vow is an act of worship; directing it to a created being is invalid and can constitute shirk.

This passage addresses a widespread practice in some Muslim cultures: people vowing to saints or pious individuals in hopes of getting their needs fulfilled. The Hanafi jurists rule such vows invalid by consensus, and warn that underlying beliefs can amount to disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize the common practice described: a person in need promises gold, silver, etc. to a saint (dead or alive) in exchange for help. The ruling is that such a vow is invalid (bāṭil) by consensus.
2
Analyze the first reason: directed to a created being
The vow is addressed to a created being (makhlooq). Since a vow is an act of worship ('ibādah), and worship is only for Allah, directing it to a creature invalidates it.
3
Analyze the second reason: the recipient is dead
The person vowing to a dead saint promises something to someone who cannot own property. A dead person cannot take possession, so the vow is meaningless.
4
Analyze the third reason: potential kufr
If the person believes the dead saint can act independently of Allah, that belief is disbelief (kufr) because it attributes divine power to a creature.
5
Note the exception
If the person says 'O Allah, I have vowed...' (i.e., the vow is actually to Allah, even if uttered in the presence of a saint), then it may be considered a valid vow to Allah.
The exception is only if the intention is clear; otherwise, the common practice is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد قدمنا أن النذر لا يصح بالمعصية للحديث لا نذر في معصية الله تعالى» فقال الشيخ قاسم في شرح الدرر : وأما النذر الذي ينذره أكثر العوام على ما هو مشاهد كأن يكون لإنسان غائب أو مريض أو له حاجة ضرورية فيأتي بعض الصلحاء فيجعل ستره على رأسه فيقول يا سيدي فلان إن رد غائبي أو عوفي مريضي أو قضيت حاجتي فلك من الذهب كذا أو من الفضة كذا أو من الطعام كذا أو من الماء كذا أو من الشمع كذا أو من الزيت كذا، فهذا النذر باطل بالإجماع لوجوه منها أنه نذر مخلوق والنذر للمخلوق لا يجوز لأنه عبادة والعبادة لا تكون للمخلوق. ومنها أن المنذور له ميت والميت لا يملك. ومنها إن ظن أن الميت يتصرف في الأمور دون الله تعالى واعتقاده ذلك كفر اللهم إلا أن قال يا الله إني نذرت
And we have already presented that the vow is not valid with disobedience due to the hadith 'No vow in disobedience to Allah'. Then Sheikh Qasim said in Sharh al-Durar: 'As for the vow that most common people make, as is witnessed – such as for a person who is absent or ill or has a pressing need, so he comes to some pious person and puts his cloth on his head and says: O my master so-and-so, if you return my absent, or cure my sick, or fulfill my need, then for you such gold, such silver, such food, such water, such wax, such oil – this vow is invalid by consensus (ijmā'), for reasons: 1) It is a vow to a created being, and a vow to a created being is not permissible because it is an act of worship, and worship is not for a created being. 2) The promised-to is dead, and a dead person owns nothing. 3) If one believes that the dead can dispose of affairs independently of Allah, that belief is disbelief. Except if he says: 'O Allah, I have vowed...'
Source pages p. 520

Commentary
This passage is a fatwa-like ruling explaining the invalidity of common vows made to saints or dead people. It begins by restating the principle from a hadith that vows in disobedience are invalid. Then quoting Sheikh Qasim from Sharh al-Durar, it describes a common practice: a person in need approaches a pious figure (often a saint), demands help, and promises gold, silver, etc. in return. This vow is invalid by scholarly consensus (ijmā') for multiple reasons. First, the vow is directed to a created being (makhlooq), but a vow is an act of worship ('ibādah), which is only for Allah. Second, the recipient (the saint) is often dead, and a dead person cannot own property; thus the promise is meaningless. Third, if the person believes the dead saint has independent power to act, that constitutes disbelief (kufr) because it attributes divine power to creation. The exception: if the person says 'O Allah, I have vowed...' (i.e., the vow is actually to Allah, seeking His reward, not to the saint), then it may be valid. This passage serves as a strong warning against shirk and superstitious practices common in some cultures.

Key Terms
نذر المعصية (nadhr al-ma'ṣiyah): A vow that involves disobedience to Allah, such as vowing to a created being.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Sheikh Qasim, what is the ruling on a common person vowing 'O my master so-and-so, if you cure my sick, then for you such gold'?
A) Valid if the saint is alive
B) Invalid but permissible
C) Invalid by consensus, and potentially kufr
D) Valid if the gold is given to charity

Key Takeaway
Vows directed to created beings are invalid; if accompanied by belief in independent power, they constitute kufr.

Vowing the Last Day of First Half and First Day of Second Half
States the ruling for a specific vow of fasting two consecutive days

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the days required by a vow phrased as 'the last day of the first half and the first day of the second half' and explain the reasoning behind the interpretation.

Core Idea
A vow to fast the last day of the first half and the first day of the second half obligates the 15th and 16th.

When a person makes a vow using phrases that refer to the halves of a month, the exact days must be identified according to the conventional division of the month. The Hanafi scholars, as recorded in al-Zahiriyya, al-Walwalijiyya, and al-Khaniyya, interpret 'the last day of the first part of the month' as the 15th and 'the first day of the last part' as the 16th. This ruling is a straightforward application of the customary understanding of month halves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the vow's wording
Read the Arabic expressions: 'آخر يوم من أول الشهر' (akhir yawm min awwal al-shahr) and 'أول يوم من آخر الشهر' (awwal yawm min akhir al-shahr).
These phrases refer to parts within a single lunar month.
2
Determine the natural division of a month
A lunar month is conventionally divided into two halves: the first half (days 1–15) and the second half (days 16–30).
This division is based on customary usage (urf) in the Hanafi school.
3
Map each phrase to the corresponding day
The 'last day of the first half' is the 15th, and the 'first day of the second half' is the 16th.
The mapping is a direct result of the half-month division.
4
State the resulting obligation
The vow therefore obligates fasting both the 15th and the 16th consecutively.
If the vow is broken, kaffarah may be required according to the rules of vows.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولوقال لله علي صوم آخر يوم من أول الشهر وأول يوم من آخر الشهر لزمه الخامس عشر والسادس عشر. الكل من الظهيرية والولوالجية والخانية
And if he says: 'I have a vow to Allah to fast the last day of the first part of the month and the first day of the last part of the month,' the fifteenth and sixteenth are incumbent upon him. All of this is from al-Zahiriyya, al-Walwaljiyya, and al-Khaniyya.
Source pages p. 520

Commentary
This is a ruling from the furu' books. The author clarifies the interpretation of a vow that uses the expressions 'last day of the first part of the month' and 'first day of the last part of the month'. Since the month is divided into two halves, the first half ends on the 15th and the second half begins on the 16th. Therefore, the vow obligates fasting the 15th and 16th of that month. The sources cited are al-Zahiriyya, al-Walwaljiyya, and al-Khaniyya.

Key Terms
آخر يوم من أول الشهر (akhir yawm min awwal al-shahr): The last day of the first half of the month, i.e., the 15th day.
أول يوم من آخر الشهر (awwal yawm min akhir al-shahr): The first day of the second half of the month, i.e., the 16th day.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The 14th and 15th
C) The 15th and 16th
D) The 10th and 20th

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school interprets 'last day of first half' as the 15th and 'first day of second half' as the 16th for vows of consecutive fasting.

Vowing to Fast the Day a Specific Person Arrives
Explains rulings for a conditional vow tied to arrival

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that 'yawm' in fasting vows refers to daytime to determine when a conditional fast becomes due.

Core Idea
In fasting vows, 'day' means daylight — arrival at night triggers no obligation.

This passage from al-Walwalji and al-Khaniyya examines a conditional vow to fast on the day a particular person arrives. The key legal question is what constitutes 'yawm' in such a vow, and how the timing of the arrival affects the obligation. Understanding this ruling requires distinguishing between the daytime (nahar) and the full 24-hour day, and applying the rule that a vow attached to a time becomes like an unrestricted vow when the time arrives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the vow and its condition
The person says: 'I have a vow to Allah to fast the day on which so-and-so arrives, forever.' Recognize that the obligation is conditional upon the arrival occurring within the daytime.
The vow is perpetual (every time the condition occurs), but focus on the first occurrence.
2
Define 'yawm' in the context of fasting
In Hanafi fiqh, 'yawm' when associated with fasting means the daylight hours (from true dawn to sunset), not the full 24-hour calendar day.
This is because the act of fasting is specific to daytime.
3
Analyze arrival at night
If the person arrives at night, no daytime is associated with that arrival. Since the condition (arrival during the day) is not met, no fast is required.
The night is not considered 'yawm' for fasting purposes.
4
Analyze arrival before zawal without having eaten
If the person arrives before zawal (noon) and has not eaten, then the condition is fulfilled, and he must fast that same day because the daytime is still valid for fasting.
Zawal is the moment when the sun passes the meridian.
5
Analyze arrival before zawal after eating, or after zawal
If he arrives before zawal but has already eaten, or after zawal, then the fast for that day is no longer valid. The vow, being attached to a time, becomes like an unrestricted vow (mudaf ila al-waqt ka al-mursal), and he must fast a substitute day in the future.
This is because the condition triggered the obligation but the immediate day is unsuitable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزاد الولوالجي فروعاً وبعضها في الخانية وهي : ولو قال لله علي أن أصوم اليوم الذي يقدم فيه فلان أبداً فقدم فلان ليلاً لم يجب عليه شيء لأن اليوم إذا قرن به ما يختص بالنهار كالصوم يراد به بياض النهار، وإذا كان كذلك لم يوجد الوقت الذي أوجب فيه الصوم وهو النهار ولو قدم يوماً قبل الزوال ولم يأكل صامه وإن قدم قبل الزوال وأكل فيه أو بعد الزوال ولم يأكل فيه صام ذلك اليوم في المستقبل ولا يصوم يومه ذلك لأن المضاف إلى الوقت عند وجود الوقت كالمرسل، ولو أرسل كان الجواب هكذا.
And al-Walwalji added branches, some of which are in al-Khaniyya, and they are: If he says: 'I have a vow to Allah to fast the day on which so-and-so arrives, forever,' and he arrives at night, nothing is incumbent upon him, because the day, when associated with something specific to the daytime like fasting, is meant as the daylight. And if that is the case, then the time for which he obligated the fast, namely the daytime, has not occurred. And if he arrives during the day before zawal and he has not eaten, he fasts it. But if he arrives before zawal and he had eaten, or after zawal and he had not eaten, he fasts that day in the future and does not fast that day, because what is added to a time, when the time occurs, is like an unrestricted [vow]; and if it were unrestricted, the answer would be like this.
Source pages p. 520

Commentary
This is a branch concerning a conditional vow to fast the day a specific person arrives. The key principle is that in such vows, 'day' (yawm) refers to the daylight hours (nahar), not the full 24-hour period. Therefore, if the person arrives at night, no day is associated with the arrival, and no fast is required. If the person arrives during the day before zawal (noon) and the person has not yet eaten, he must fast that day because the condition has occurred within the daytime. However, if he arrives before zawal but he had already eaten (making fasting invalid for that day), or if he arrives after zawal (when fasting for that day is no longer valid), then he does not fast that day but must fast a substitute day in the future. This is because the vow, once the time condition is triggered, becomes like an unrestricted vow that must be fulfilled later.

Key Terms
اليوم (al-yawm): In the context of fasting vows, it means the daylight portion, not the full 24-hour day.
المضاف إلى الوقت (al-mudaf ila al-waqt): A vow that is added to a specific time; upon occurrence of that time, it becomes like an unrestricted vow.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone vows to fast the day a certain person arrives, and that person arrives at night, what is the ruling?
A) He must fast the next day
B) He must fast a substitute day
C) Nothing is required
D) He must fast that night

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a conditional vow to fast on the day of arrival only obligates fasting if the arrival occurs during the daytime, otherwise no fast is required.

Unconditional vs Conditional Vows: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on Fasting in Rajab
Distinguishes between mudaf and mu'allaq vows

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between unconditional (mudaf) and conditional (mu'allaq bi al-shart) vows and state the Hanafi positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on early fulfillment.

Core Idea
An unconditional vow to fast in Rajab can be fulfilled early; a conditional vow must wait for Rajab.

Vows to perform worship at a specific future time raise the question: can the act be done early? This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on whether a vow to fast in Rajab may be fulfilled before Rajab. The answer depends on whether the vow is unconditional (mudaf) or conditional (mu'allaq bi al-shart). We'll explore the reasoning behind each position and the practical consequence of the disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of vows
Distinguish between an unconditional vow (e.g., 'I vow to fast in Rajab') and a conditional vow (e.g., 'When Rajab comes, I must fast'). The former is an idafah (addition to a time); the latter is ta'liq (suspension on a condition).
2
State Abu Yusuf's position and his dalil
Abu Yusuf holds that an unconditional vow to fast in Rajab is valid if performed before Rajab. His evidence: it is an idafah, analogous to expediting charity (like zakat) before its due time.
The analogy (qiyas) rests on the permissibility of early payment in ibadat when time is not a condition of validity.
3
State Muhammad's position and his dalil
Muhammad holds that the unconditional vow cannot be fulfilled before Rajab because the time is intrinsic to the act. Fasting before Rajab fails to meet the vow's condition.
Muhammad views the time as a shart (condition) for the act, not merely a time attribute.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if someone vows to fast in Rajab and fasts in Jumada al-Akhirah, Abu Yusuf considers it valid (no need to repeat); Muhammad does not. The same applies to prayer vowed in Rajab.
Later Hanafi practice often follows Abu Yusuf's view for unconditional vows.
5
Note the agreement on conditional vows
Both Abu Yusuf and Muhammad agree that a conditional vow ('When Rajab comes...') cannot be performed before Rajab because the condition has not occurred.
This shows the boundary: idafah allows early performance; ta'liq does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو نذر صوماً في رجب أو صلاة فيه جاز عنه قبله في قول أبي يوسف لأنه إضافة خلافاً لمحمد . وإن كان معلقاً بالشرط بأن قال إذا جاء شهر رجب فعلي أن أصوم لا يجوز قبله لأن المعلق بالشرط لا يكون سبباً قبل الشرط، ويجوز تعجيل الصدقة المضافة إلى وقت كالزكاة .
And if he vows a fast in Rajab or a prayer in it, it is valid for him to perform it before Rajab in the opinion of Abu Yusuf, because it is an addition (of the act to a time), contrary to Muhammad. And if it is conditional upon a condition, such as saying 'When Rajab comes, upon me is to fast,' it is not valid before Rajab because what is conditional upon a condition is not a cause before the condition. And it is permissible to expedite a charity added to a time, like zakat.
Source pages p. 520

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the validity of fulfilling a vow to fast in Rajab before Rajab. Abu Yusuf argues that an unconditional vow to fast in Rajab is valid to perform in advance because it is an 'idafah' (addition of the act to a future time), and such an addition allows early performance, analogous to giving charity or zakat before its due time. Muhammad disagrees, holding that the time (Rajab) is integral to the vow, and the act cannot be performed before its appointed time. However, if the vow is explicitly conditional (mu'allaq bi al-shart), like 'When Rajab comes, I must fast,' then both agree it cannot be performed before Rajab because the condition has not occurred. The text also notes that expediting charity assigned to a future time (like zakat) is permissible, which supports Abu Yusuf's analogy.

Key Terms
إضافة (idafah): Attribution of a worship act to a future time, allowing its performance in advance.
معلق بالشرط (mu'allaq bi al-shart): A vow that is explicitly conditional on a future event; cannot be performed before the condition occurs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, if someone vows to fast in Rajab without any condition, can they fast before Rajab?
A) Yes
B) No
C) Only if they also vow to pray
D) Only if Rajab has started

Key Takeaway
An unconditional vow to perform an act in a specific future time (idafah) may be fulfilled early according to Abu Yusuf, but a conditional vow (ta'liq) must wait for the condition.

Vowing 'This Month a Day' Interpreted as Any Day
Explains the interpretation of a vow phrased as 'fast this month a day.'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a vow phrased as 'to fast this month a day' and explain why it is interpreted as any day within that month.

Core Idea
When a vow's wording is ambiguous, it is interpreted to fulfill the intended obligation with leeway.

This passage addresses a specific type of vow: a person declares, 'I have a vow to Allah to fast this month a day.' At first glance, the phrase seems contradictory—a month cannot be a day. The Hanafi jurists clarify that the phrase is understood as a vow to fast one day within that month, giving the person the flexibility to choose any day. This reflects the principle of giving the benefit of the doubt to the vower and ensuring the vow can be fulfilled.

Guided Path
1
Identify the vow formulation
The vow is: 'I have a vow to Allah to fast this month a day.' Note the apparent contradiction: a month is a period, not a single day.
2
Recognize the interpretive problem
The phrase cannot be taken literally because a month cannot be a day. The word 'yawman' (a day) must be understood in a different sense.
3
Apply the Hanafi interpretation
The vow is interpreted as a commitment to fast one day during that month. The mention of the month indicates the time frame, not a specific day.
The text compares it to saying 'a time from the times.'
4
Determine the leeway
The vower may choose any day within the month, and even if the month passes, he has until his death to fulfill the vow. This reflects the principle of ease.
The phrase 'musawwa'an alayhi' indicates spaciousness.
5
Confirm the ruling's scope
This interpretation applies only when the wording is ambiguous. If the vow is clear (e.g., 'fast this month'), the ruling differs (entire month).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال لله علي صوم هذا الشهر يوماً لزمه صوم ذلك الشهر بعينه متى شاء موسعاً عليه إلى أن يموت لأن الشهر لا يتصور أن يكون يوماً حقيقة وهو بياض النهار فحمل على الوقت فصار كما لو قال لله علي أن أصوم هذا الشهر وقتاً من الأوقات.
And if he says: 'I have a vow to Allah to fast this month a day,' fasting that very month is incumbent upon him whenever he wishes, with leeway until he dies, because the month cannot literally be a day—it is the daylight—so it is interpreted as the time, and it becomes as if he said: 'I have a vow to Allah to fast this month a time from the times.'
Source pages p. 520

Commentary
This ruling addresses a vow phrased as 'to fast this month a day.' Since the month cannot be a single day, the phrase is interpreted as a vow to fast during the month, any day he chooses. He has the option to fast any day of that month, and if he delays, he must eventually fast before death. The expression 'yawman' (a day) is taken as a general time within the month, similar to saying 'a time from the times.'

Key Terms
موسعاً عليه (musawwa'an alayhi): He has leeway; he can choose any day within the month to fulfill the vow.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone vows to fast 'this month a day,' which day must they fast?
A) The first day of the month
B) The last day of the month
C) Any day of the month they choose
D) The day the vow was made

Key Takeaway
A vow to fast 'this month a day' is fulfilled by fasting any one day of that month, with leeway until death.

Default Fasts for Vague Vows: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Arabic Plurals
Default numbers for vague vows of fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default number of fasts for unspecified vows of days, months, and years according to Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and explain the linguistic basis of their disagreement.

Core Idea
When a vow lacks specification, Hanafi imams disagree on the minimum plural number: Abu Hanifa uses 10, Sahibayn use 7 for days, 12 for months, and lifetime for years.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a common scenario: someone makes a vow to fast but does not specify how many days, months, or years. Without a clear number, the vow could be invalid. The Hanafi school provides default numbers based on linguistic principles. However, there is a well-known intra-madhhab dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, over what those defaults should be. Understanding this dispute clarifies how Hanafi jurists resolve ambiguity in vows using Arabic usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the vow terms
The passage examines four terms: 'days' (ayyam), 'months' (shuhur), 'years' (sinin), and 'zaman/hin' (time). For each, the imams give default numbers when the vow is unspecified.
2
Memorize Abu Hanifa's defaults
Abu Hanifa holds that the 'small plural' (jam' qalil) in Arabic is up to ten. Therefore: days=10, months=10, years=10. For 'zaman/hin' he had no knowledge of 'dahr'; the Sahibayn fix it at six months.
For years, Abu Hanifa says ten, but Sahibayn say 'dahr' (lifetime) unless the vower intended three.
3
Memorize the Sahibayn's defaults
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) follow common usage: days=7 (common minimum), months=12 (full year), years=lifetime (dahr), and for 'zaman/hin' they say six months.
The Sahibayn's view is often considered more practical.
4
Understand the linguistic basis
The dispute hinges on whether the 'small plural' (up to ten) or 'common usage' (urf) should determine the default. Abu Hanifa favors a strict linguistic interpretation; the Sahibayn favor common speech.
This reflects a broader usul principle: al-haqiqah vs al-urf.
5
Note the practical difference (thamarah)
The different defaults affect how many fasts the vower must perform. For example, vowing 'months' could mean 10 or 12 fasts. The mufta bihi today often leans toward the Sahibayn, but both positions are valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال لله علي صيام أيام ولا نية له كان عليه صيام عشرة أيام عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما سبعة أيام. ولو قال لله علي صيام أيام لزمه صوم جمع قليل، ولو قال صيام الشهور فعشرة، وقالا صيام اثنى عشر شهراً. ولو قال لله علي صيام السنين لزمه صيام عشرة وقالا : لزمه صيام الدهر إلا أن ينوي ثلاثاً فيكون ما نوى. ولو قال لله علي صيام الزمن والحين ولا نية له كان على ستة أشهر. والزمن مثل الحين في العرف ولا علم لأبي حنيفة بصيام دهر إذا نذره وقالا : على ستة أشهر . الكل من الولوالجي.
And if he says: 'I have a vow to Allah to fast days' and he had no intention, it is incumbent upon him to fast ten days according to Abu Hanifa, and according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) seven days. And if he says: 'I have a vow to Allah to fast days' [without qualification], it is incumbent upon him to fast a small plurality. And if he says: 'fasting months,' then ten months, and they (the two) say twelve months. And if he says: 'I have a vow to Allah to fast years,' it is incumbent upon him to fast ten years, and they say it is incumbent upon him to fast all one's life unless he intended three, then it is what he intended. And if he says: 'I have a vow to Allah to fast al-zaman and al-hin' and he had no intention, it is incumbent upon him to fast six months. And 'al-zaman' is like 'al-hin' in common usage, and Abu Hanifa has no knowledge of fasting 'dahr' if he vows it, and they (the two) say: six months. All from al-Walwalji.
Source pages p. 520

Commentary
This passage presents default interpretations for vague vows of fasting. When someone vows 'days' without specifying number, Abu Hanifa considers the minimum plural in Arabic to be ten days, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) consider the minimum plural to be seven days. For 'months', Abu Hanifa says ten months, the Sahibayn say twelve. For 'years', Abu Hanifa says ten years, the Sahibayn say it means all one's life (dahr) unless the person intended a specific number like three. For the words 'zaman' and 'hin', both are similar in common usage, and Abu Hanifa has no specific ruling for 'dahr' when vowed; the Sahibayn say it is six months. These rulings are from al-Walwalji and serve as default values when the vow is ambiguous.

Key Terms
جمع قليل (jam' qalil): The small plural in Arabic, typically used for numbers up to ten; here it indicates the minimum number of days.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if someone vows to fast 'days' without specifying, how many days must they fast?
A) 3 days
B) 7 days
C) 10 days
D) 12 days

Key Takeaway
The default number for an unspecified vow depends on the interpretation of Arabic plurals: Abu Hanifa uses 10 for days, months, and years; the Sahibayn use 7, 12, and lifetime respectively.
